Leaving a House vs. a City: The Hanafi Oath of Residence
Rulings on oaths not to reside in a house or city, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on moving belongings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on what constitutes residence in oaths not to reside in a house versus a city, and apply the mufta bihi to a given scenario.

Core Idea
For oaths of residence, custom ('urf) determines when one is still a resident: family and belongings keep you in a house, but physical departure suffices for a city.

This passage from the chapter on oaths of dwelling presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on what it means to 'leave' a residence for the purpose of an oath. The core principle is that residence is defined by custom, and the ruling differs sharply between a house and a city. You will learn the three positions of the Hanafi imams – Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad – and the preferred view for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position for oaths not to reside
Identify that the oath requires leaving the residence; if the person is still considered a resident by custom, he breaks the oath.
The standard is birr (fulfillment) vs. hanith (breach).
2
Identify the key distinction between house and city
Note that for a house, leaving oneself is insufficient if family and belongings remain; for a city, physical departure is enough. Trace the reliance on 'urf.
Custom considers a person a resident of a house through family and goods, but not of a city after leaving.
3
Trace the three views of the imams on moving belongings
For a house: Abu Hanifa requires all belongings to be moved; Abu Yusuf requires most; Muhammad requires what suffices for daily needs. Understand each evidence (ta'lil).
The dispute is only for a house; for a city the imams agree with Abu Yusuf's narration.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and the basis for tarjih
Identify that Muhammad's view is preferred (ahsan wa arfaq) for the house, and Abu Yusuf's view for the city. Why? Practicality and ease.
Also note that moving to a lane or mosque does not free one from the oath regarding a house.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يكون يمينا و ما لا يكون يمينا .
قسم کھائی کہ اس گھر میں نہیں رہوں گا خود نکل گیا اور ساز و سامان اس کے اہل و عیال اسی میں ہیں اور لوٹنے کا ارادہ بھی نہیں ہے حانث ہو جائے گا
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَسْكُنُ هَذِهِ الدَّارَ فَخَرَجَ بِنَفْسِهِ وَمَتَاعُهُ وَاهْلُهُ فِيهَا وَلَمْ يُرِدِ الرُّجُوعَ إِلَيْهَا حَيْثُ لِأَنَّهُ يُعَدُّ سَاكِنَا بِبَقَاءِ أَهْلِهِ وَمَتَاعِهِ فِيهَا عُرْفًا فَإِنَّ السُّوقِيَّ عَامَّةَ نَهَارِهِ فِى السُّوقِ وَيَقُولُ أَسْكُنُ سِكَةٌ كَذَا وَالْبَيْتُ وَالْمُحَلَّةُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الدَّارِ
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور جس شخص نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اب اس گھر میں نہیں رہوں گا پھر وہ خود نکل گیا مگر اس کا سامان اور اس کے متعلقین بال بچے) اسی گھر میں رہ گئے۔ ساتھ ہی اس گھر میں اس کے واپس آنے کا ارادہ بھی نہیں ہے پھر بھی وہ حانث ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ اس مکان میں اس کے بال بچے واسباب ہونے سے عرف میں اس گھر کا باشندہ کہلائے گا۔ جیسا کہ ایک بازاری آدمی ( دوکاندارمثلا ) دن بھر بازار میں رہتا ہے لیکن وہ پوچھنے پر کہتا ہے کہ میں فلاں گلی اور فلاں گھر میں رہتا ہوں۔ بہیت اور محلہ کا حکم دار کے حکم کے جیسا ہے۔
قسم کھائی اس شہر میں نہیں رہوں گا تو بچے اور سامان منتقل کرنے پر موقوف نہیں
وَلَوْ كَانَ الْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمِصْرِ لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ الْبَرُّ عَلَى نَقْلِ الْمَتَاعِ وَالْأَهْلِ فِيْمَارُوِيَ عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعَدُّ سَاكِنَ فِي الَّذِي انْتَقَلَ عَنْهُ عُرْفًا بِخِلَافِ الْأَوَّلِ وَالْقَرْيَةِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمِصْرِ فِي الصَّحِيحِ مِنَ الْجَوَابِ ثُمَّ قَالَ ابو حَنِيفَةَ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نَقْلِ كُلِ الْمَتَاعِ حَتَّى لَوْبَقِيَ وَتَدْيَحْنَتُ لأنَّ السُّكْنى قَدْثَبَتَ بِالْكُلَّ فَيَبْقَى مَابَقِيَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفٌ يُعْتَبَرُ نَقُلُ الْأَكْثَرِ لَاتَ نَقْلَ الْكُلِّ قَدْيَتَعَدَّرُ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ يُعْتَبَرُ نَقْلُ مَا يَقُومُ بِهِ كَدْ خَذَا نِيَتِهِ لِأَنَّ مَاوَرَاءَ ذَالِكَ لَيْسَ مِنَ السُّكْنى قَالُوا هَذَا أَحْسَنُ وَاَرْفَقُ بِالنَّاسِ وَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَنْتَقِلَ إِلَى مَنْزِلِ اخَرَبِلَا تَاخِيْرِ حَتَّى يَرْفَإِنِ انْتَقَلَ إِلَى السَّكَةِ أَوْإِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ قَالُوا لَا يُبَرُّدَ لَهُ فِي الزِيَادَاتِ أَنَّ مَنْ خَرَجَ بِعَمَالِهِ مِنْ مِصْرِهِ فَمَالَمْ يَتَّخِذُ وَطَنَا اخَرَيَبْقَى وَظُنُّهُ الْأَوَّلُ فِي حَقِّ الصَّلَوةِ كَذَا هَذَا.
.....
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ سم کھائی کہ میں اس شہر میں نہیں رہوں گا تو قسم میں سچا ہونا بال بچے اور اسباب کو منتقل کرنے پر موقوف نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے۔ کیونکہ جس شہر سے منتقل ہو گیا ہے۔ عرف میں اس شہر کا باشندہ نہیں کہلاتا ہے۔ بخلاف پہلی صورت کے اور صحیح قول کے مطابق گاؤں کا حکم شہر کے حکم کے جیسا ہے۔ پھر امام اعظم نے فرمایا ہے کہ تم میں سچا ہونے کے لئے گھر کے ایک ایک سامان کا منتقل کر لینا ضروری ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر میخ ( کیل ) بھی گھر میں رہ گئی تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ گھر میں رہائش پورے اسباب سے ثابت ہوئی تھی۔ تو جب تک اس اسباب میں سے کچھ بھی وہاں باقی رہے گا وہاں کی رہائش باقی کبھی جائے گی۔ اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ اکثر سامان منتقل کر لینا ہی کافی
Chapter on what constitutes an oath and what does not.
He swore that he will not reside in this house, then he himself left, but his equipment and his family are still in it, and he does not intend to return: he becomes a breaker of the oath.
He (al-Quduri) said: And whoever swore he will not reside in this house, then he left himself, but his belongings and his family are in it, and he did not intend to return to it, because he is considered a resident due to the remaining of his family and belongings in it by custom ('urf). Indeed, a market trader spends most of his day in the market, yet he says 'I reside in such-and-such lane.' The house and the lane are like the house.
Translation: Al-Quduri said: And the person who swore that he will not reside in this house anymore, then he himself left, but his goods and his dependents (children) remained in that house, and he also has no intention of returning, yet he became a breaker. Because due to his children and goods being in that house, in custom he is called a resident of that house. Just as a market person (a shopkeeper e.g.) spends the day in the market, but when asked he says 'I live in such-and-such lane and such-and-such house.' The ruling of a lane and a locality is like that of a house.
And if the oath is regarding the city, then being free from the oath is not contingent upon moving the belongings and family, according to what is narrated from Abu Yusuf, because he is not considered a resident of that which he has moved away from by custom, contrary to the first case. And a village is like a city in the correct answer.
Then Abu Hanifa said: It is necessary to move all of the belongings, such that if a tent peg remains, he becomes a breaker because residence was established by the whole, so it remains as long as any part of it remains. And Abu Yusuf said: Moving most (al-akthar) is sufficient, because moving all may be difficult. And Muhammad said: Moving what suffices (ma yaqumu bihi) is sufficient, because beyond that is not part of residence. They (the scholars) said: This is best and most convenient for the people. And it is appropriate that he moves to another dwelling without delay until he removes [the rest?]. If he moves to a lane or to a mosque, they said he is not free from the oath. And in al-Ziyadat: Whoever leaves his city with his work, as long as he does not take another abode, his first abode remains his place (watan) for the purpose of prayer. Similarly here.
Translation: And if he swears that he will not reside in this city, then being truthful in the oath is not contingent upon moving the children and goods, as narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. Because in custom, one who has moved from a city is not called a resident of that city, contrary to the first case. And according to the correct opinion, a village is like a city. Then Imam Abu Hanifa said: To be truthful, it is necessary to move every single item of the house, such that if a nail remains, he becomes a breaker. Because residence in the house was established by the whole, so as long as any part of that remains, the residence remains. And Imam Abu Yusuf said: Moving most is sufficient, as moving all may be difficult. And Imam Muhammad said: Moving what suffices is sufficient, considering his intention, because beyond that is not residence.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on oaths of entering and dwelling, and it presents rulings and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding oaths not to reside in a house or a city. The core principle is that residence is determined by 'urf (custom). For a house, if the swearer leaves but his family and belongings remain, he is still considered a resident and thus breaks his oath. This is analogized to a shopkeeper who spends the day in the market but says he lives in a certain lane. For a city, however, physical relocation suffices; moving family and goods is not necessary to be free of the oath. The ikhtilaf among the imams concerns what constitutes leaving a house: Abu Hanifa requires removal of all belongings; Abu Yusuf requires most; Muhammad requires enough for daily necessities. The author notes that Muhammad's view is most practical (ahsan wa arfaq). Additional rules: moving to a lane or mosque does not free one from the oath regarding a house; a reference from al-Ziyadat indicates that for prayer rulings, changing one's 'watan' requires intent to settle.

Key Terms
عرف ('urf): Custom or common practice used to determine what constitutes residence.
مصر (misr): A city or large town.
دار (dar): A house or dwelling.
بر (birr): Truthfulness or fulfillment of an oath; being free of the oath's obligation.
حانث (hanith): The one who breaks his oath.
نقل (naql): Moving or transferring belongings to end residence for the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi imam's view on moving belongings for a house oath is considered 'ahsan wa arfaq' (best and most convenient)?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad
D) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad

Key Takeaway
For oaths of residence, 'urf determines residence: for a house, belongings matter; for a city, physical departure suffices. The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view for house, Abu Yusuf's for city.

Fulfilling an Oath to Vacate a House: How Much Belongings Must Be Moved?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fulfilling oath to vacate a house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fulfilling an oath to vacate a house and distinguish between the views of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn regarding the quantity of belongings to be moved.

Core Idea
Moving sufficient belongings for household management fulfills an oath to vacate — not all belongings are required.

When someone swears to leave a house, what constitutes moving out? The Hanafi school has an internal difference. In this passage, we learn that Imam Muhammad adopted a practical view: transferring only the essentials for household management suffices. The mashayikh preferred this view. Let's examine the proof and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is fulfilling an oath to vacate a house. The dispute: what quantity of belongings must be moved to be considered 'moved out'?
The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) vs. Imam Muhammad.
2
State the two positions
Sahibayn require moving all belongings. Imam Muhammad requires only enough to establish household management.
The passage states: 'امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ جتنے سامان کے منتقل کر لینے سے خانہ داری کا انتظام پورا ہو سکتا ہے۔'
3
Note the tarjih
The mashayikh (later scholars) preferred Imam Muhammad's view because it is practical and easier for people.
Passage: 'مشایخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ قول بہت خوب اور لوگوں کے عملی طور پر زیادہ آسان ہے۔'
4
Examine the dalil
The proof from al-Ziyadat uses qiyas: a traveler's homeland remains until he permanently adopts a new one; similarly, a person's house remains his residence until he establishes a new one with sufficient belongings.
The analogy shows that moving all belongings is not necessary; establishing household management suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الدخول والسكنى
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم . ہے۔ کیونکہ کبھی تو پورے سامان و منتقل کر لینا نا ممکن ہو جاتا ہے (اس پر فتویٰ ہے۔ جیسا کہ کافی میں ہے ) اور امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ جتنے سامان کے منتقل کر لینے سے خانہ داری کا انتظام پورا ہو سکتا ہے۔ اس کو منتقل کر لینا کافی ہوگا۔ کیونکہ سکونت کے لئے اس سے زیادہ سامان کا ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ مشایخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ قول بہت خوب اور لوگوں کے عملی طور پر زیادہ آسان ہے۔ واضح ہو کہ ایسے شخص کے لئے یہ لازم ہوگا کہ وہ قسم کے فورا بعد بلا تاخیر اپنا سامان دوسرے مکان میں منتقل کرنا شروع کر دے تا کہ وہ اپنی قسم میں سچا ہو۔ اور اگر اس نے اپنا سامان گھر سے نکال کر لی کو چہ میں یا مسجد میں منتقل کیا تو بھی قسم میں سچا نہ ہوگا۔ زیادات میں اس کی دلیل مروی ہے کہ وہ شخص جو اپنے بال بچوں کو لے کر دوسرے شہر میں گیا تو نماز کے معاملہ میں مقیم اور مسافر کے سلسلہ میں) پہلا وطن باقی رہے گا ۔ جب تک کہ دوسرے کو وطن نہ بنا لے ایسا ہی یہاں بھی ہے۔
Chapter of the oath regarding entering and residing. Ashraf al-Bidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume six. Because sometimes it is not possible to transfer all belongings (and fatwa is upon this, as in al-Kafi). Imam Muhammad said: Transferring enough belongings by which household management can be established suffices. Because for residence, more belongings than that are not necessary. The mashayikh said: This opinion is excellent and easier for people in practice. It is clear that for such a person, it is necessary that immediately after the oath, without delay, he starts transferring his belongings to another house so that he becomes truthful in his oath. And if he takes his belongings out of the house and transfers them to an alley or a mosque, he will not be truthful in his oath. In al-Ziyadat, its proof is narrated: That a person who took his family to another city, in the matter of prayer (regarding resident and traveler) his first homeland remains until he makes the second his homeland. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of an oath to vacate a house. The issue: what constitutes moving residence for the purpose of fulfilling an oath? The default view requires moving all belongings. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani states that moving sufficient belongings to establish household management is enough, as complete transfer may be impractical. The mashayikh prefer this view for its ease. The text stresses immediate commencement of transfer; delaying would breach the oath. Transferring belongings to a non-dwelling place like an alley or mosque does not fulfill the oath, as it does not establish a new residence. The proof from al-Ziyadat uses analogy: a traveler moving family to another city retains his original homeland for prayer rulings until he adopts the new city as permanent home; similarly, transferring belongings to a new house does not count until the new house is made the actual residence.

Key Terms
سکونت (sukūnat): Residence; the legal state of dwelling in a place for continuity.
قسم (qasam): Oath; a legal commitment invoking Allah's name to perform or refrain from an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view of the mashayikh, what quantity of belongings must be moved to fulfill an oath to leave a house?
A) All belongings must be moved.
B) Enough to establish household management.
C) Only heavy furniture.
D) No belongings need to be moved if the person intends to leave.

Key Takeaway
Imam Muhammad's view that moving sufficient belongings for household management suffices is the preferred position for fatwa.

Oath Regarding Entering a Mosque After Reconstruction
Four rulings on oath when mosque undergoes changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi rulings on whether entering a mosque after its physical or legal transformation breaks an oath (yamin).

Core Idea
A mosque's identity for an oath depends on its continuous status as a mosque on the same site, not its physical form.

A person swears not to enter a particular mosque. Later the mosque changes: it is demolished and rebuilt, turned into a house and then rebuilt, extended, or the person goes onto the roof. Does entering break the oath? The Hanafi school provides four clear rulings based on whether the mosque retains its legal identity.

Guided Path
1
First case: Rebuilt on same site
The mosque is demolished and rebuilt on the same plot. The Hanafi rule: the site retains its identity as the mosque originally sworn. Entering the new building makes the person hanith (oath‑breaker).
The oath refers to the location, not the structure.
2
Second case: Converted to house then rebuilt
The mosque first collapsed and was made into a house, losing its legal status as a mosque. Later it was rebuilt as a mosque. This is a new mosque; entering does not make one hanith.
Interruption of mosque status creates a new entity.
3
Third case: Mosque extended
An extension is added to the original mosque. Entering only the extension does not break the oath. The extension is not considered part of the original mosque for that oath.
The oath is strictly interpreted; the extension is outside the original boundaries.
4
Fourth case: Going onto the roof
If the person goes onto the roof of the mosque, he does not become hanith because the roof is not considered the interior of the mosque. This is provided the roof is 'non‑Arabic' (i.e., not part of the prayer area).
Accessing the roof is not entering the mosque.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نمبرا کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ اس مسجد میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ بعد میں جبکہ وہ مسجد گرا کر دوبارہ بنائی گئی اور اس میں داخل ہوا تو وہ حانث ہو گیا۔ نمبر اور اگر وہ ٹوٹ پھوٹ گئی اور اسے کسی نے گھر بنا لیا۔ پھر اسے بھی تو ڑ کر دوبارہ مسجد بنائی گئی تو اس میں داخل ہونے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ ں ہونے سے نمبر اور اگر اصلی مسجد میں کچھ حصہ اور بڑھایا گیا پھر اس حصہ میں وہ داخل ہوا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ نمبر اور اگر یہ تم کائی کہ مسجد میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ پھر کسی طرح اس کی چھت پر چلا گیا تو وہ حالت نہیں ہوگا بشرطیکہ وہ بھی یعنی وہ غیر عربی ہو۔ اسی پر فتوی ہے۔
Number one: Someone swore that he will not go to this mosque. Later, when that mosque was demolished and rebuilt, and he entered it, he became a breaker of the oath (hanith). Number two: And if it collapsed and someone made it into a house. Then that was also demolished and a mosque was built again, then entering it does not make him hanith. Number three: And if some portion was added to the original mosque, then he entered that portion, he does not become hanith. Number four: And if he swore that he will not go to the mosque, then somehow he went onto its roof, he does not become hanith, provided that (the roof) is also non-Arabic. Upon this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage states four rulings regarding an oath to not enter a specific mosque. First: if the mosque is demolished and rebuilt on the same site, it remains the same entity, so entering it breaks the oath. Second: if the mosque was first turned into a house (ceasing to be a mosque), then later rebuilt as a mosque, it is a new entity; entering does not break the oath. Third: extending the mosque with additional space does not change its identity; entering the extension is like entering the mosque, but the rule here says it does not break the oath? This seems counterintuitive. However, the text states 'he does not become hanith'. Perhaps the extension is considered not part of the original mosque for the oath? This may be based on custom or legal fiction. Fourth: going onto the roof is not entering the mosque interior, so oath not broken. The phrase 'provided it is non-Arabic' may refer to a condition about the roof's legal status. These rulings follow the Hanafi principle that an oath is strictly interpreted based on the object's legal identity at the time of oath.

Key Terms
حانث (ḥānith): One who breaks his oath; liable for atonement (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to enter a particular mosque, and the mosque is demolished and rebuilt on the same plot, does entering the new mosque break the oath?
A) Yes, because it is considered the same mosque.
B) No, because it is a new building.
C) Only if the name remains the same.
D) Only if the person intended the original building.

Key Takeaway
A mosque's identity for an oath is determined by continuous existence as a mosque on the same site; structural changes do not break that identity, but loss of mosque status does.

Oath Not to Enter: Voluntary vs Involuntary Entry
Rulings on oath not to enter a house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath not to enter a house is broken only by voluntary, commanded, or complete entry, and identify when entry is involuntary.

Core Idea
An oath to not enter a house is only broken by voluntary entry; involuntary entry does not make one ḥānith.

This passage from the Hidayah presents a series of scenarios clarifying when an oath not to enter a house is breached. The overarching principle is that the breach depends on the person's intention and control over the entry. Physical presence alone is not sufficient; the act must be attributed to the oath-maker's choice or command. By understanding these rulings, you will avoid common misunderstandings about oath-breaking in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and the Prohibited Act
The oath is 'I will not enter this house.' Entry is the act of moving from outside to inside. The question is when this act constitutes a violation.
2
Distinguish Voluntary vs Involuntary Entry
Voluntary entry includes walking in, riding in, or being carried by someone you commanded. Involuntary entry includes being carried without your command, being thrown by wind, or a horse bolting against your control.
The key is whether the entry is attributable to your choice or instruction.
3
Apply the Hanafi Rule on Command and Consent
If you command someone to carry you in, it is your act. If they carry you without command, it is not your act even if you were inwardly pleased. Inner consent does not transfer the act to you.
External command (verbal) is required; mere internal willingness is insufficient.
4
Consider Partial and Full Entry
Entering only one foot does not count as entry. The entire body must be inside for the oath to be broken.
Partial entry is like incomplete entrance; the prohibition applies to full entry.
5
Handle Re-entry Scenarios
If you are forced in, then voluntarily exit and later re-enter by choice, the later voluntary entry breaks the oath. The earlier forced entry does not count.
Each entry event is evaluated independently based on volition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نمبر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں اس گھر میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ پھر وہ سوار ہو کر یا پیدل یا اس کے کہنے سے اسے کوئی اٹھا کر لے گیا تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ نمبر اور اگر وہ گھوڑے پر سوارتھا کہ اچانک گھوڑ اہدک کر اس کے روکتے رہنے کے باوجود اسے اس گھر میں لے گیا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ نمبر اور اگر کوئی دوسرا شخص اس کو اس کے حکم کے بغیر اٹھا کر لے گیا تو بھی حانث نہ ہو گا۔ خواہ دل سے راضی ہو یا راضی نہ ہو۔ خواہ روک سکتا ہو یا نہیں۔ عامہ مشائخ کا یہی قول ہے۔ اور یہی صحیح ہے۔ نمبر اور اگر صرف ایک پاؤں اندر داخل کیا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔ نمبر اور اگر پھل کر کچھ گھر میں گر پڑایا ہوا کے جھونکے نے اسے اندر گرا دیا میچ یہ ہے کہحان نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر کوئی اسے زبردستی لے گیا پھر نکل آیا۔ پھر اپنے اختیار سے اندر گیا تو فتویٰ یہ ہے کہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔
Number: Someone swore that he will not go into this house. Then he went riding, or walking, or someone carried him at his command, then he becomes hanith. Number two: And if he was riding a horse and suddenly the horse bolted, and despite his trying to stop it, it took him into the house, he does not become hanith. Number three: And if another person carried him without his command, even if he was inwardly pleased or not, whether he could prevent it or not, he does not become hanith. This is the statement of the majority of the mashayikh, and it is correct. Number four: And if he only inserted one foot inside, he does not become hanith; this is correct. Number five: And if he fell into the house due to a gust of wind, the ruling is that he does not become hanith. And if someone forced him to enter, then he came out, then he entered by his own choice, the fatwa is that he becomes hanith.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage details multiple scenarios of entering a house when one has sworn not to enter. The key principle is that entry must be by the person's own volition or by their command to be a breach. Ruling 1: Entering by riding, walking, or being carried by someone he ordered counts as voluntary entry. Ruling 2: If an animal he rides uncontrollably goes inside, it is involuntary – no breach. Ruling 3: Being carried by someone without his order is not a breach, even if he consents internally, because consent alone does not transfer the act to him. Ruling 4: Partial entry (one foot) is not considered full entry, so no oath-breaking. Ruling 5: Entering due to wind is involuntary. Finally, if one is forced in and then later voluntarily enters after leaving, it becomes a breach because the later entry is willful.

Key Terms
حانث (ḥānith): One who breaks his oath; requires kaffarah (expiation).
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears not to enter a house. Another person carries him inside without his command. According to the Hanafi ruling, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he entered.
B) Yes, if he was pleased.
C) No, because he did not command the act.
D) No, only if he verbally protested.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to enter a house is broken only by voluntary, commanded, and complete entry; involuntary, uncommanded, or partial entry does not make one ḥānith.

Interpreting 'Bayt' in Oaths: The Role of Custom (Urf)
Explains difference between Arab and local custom in interpreting 'bayt' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Arabic literal meaning of 'bayt' and the local custom ('urf) in the context of oaths, and apply the Hanafi rule that custom governs interpretation when no intention is specified.

Core Idea
In oaths, local custom overrides literal Arabic meaning: 'bayt' means the whole house, not just a room.

The word 'bayt' in oaths can be ambiguous: does it refer to a single room or the entire dwelling? The Hanafi school resolves this by looking at the intention of the swearer, and when none exists, by referring to local custom ('urf). This lesson examines a classic scenario from the hadaya commentaries and shows how the fatwa is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
A person swears not to enter a specific 'bayt' (room/house) without specific intention. This is the base case from the text.
No intention (niyyah) means we rely on external interpretation.
2
Apply the Arabic literal meaning
In Arabic, 'bayt' specifically means a single room. 'Manzil' is a house with multiple rooms, and 'dar' is a mansion. Under this meaning, entering the courtyard does not break the oath because the courtyard is not the room.
This is the mashayikh's reference to Arab idiom.
3
Apply the local custom
In the local custom (South Asian context), 'bayt', 'manzil', and 'dar' are all synonymous for 'house'. Therefore, entering the courtyard of the house that contains the room is considered entering the 'bayt', thus breaking the oath.
The custom redefines the scope of the term.
4
Determine the fatwa
The mashayikh and later commentaries (al-'Inayah, Fath al-Qadir) state that the fatwa is based on local custom, not the literal Arabic. Therefore, the oath is broken by entering the courtyard.
The fatwa prioritizes 'urf over lughah when no intention is specified.
5
Understand the legal principle
The Hanafi principle is that 'urf (custom) is a valid source for interpreting ambiguous terms in oaths, contracts, and other matters where no explicit text or intention exists. This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference).
This principle is not limited to oaths but extends to mu'amalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نمبرا قسم کھائی کہ اس بیت میں داخل نہ ہوں گا۔ اور اس کی کچھ نیت نہیں ہے پھر وہ اس احاطہ کے اندر آیا جس میں یہ بیت واقع ہے تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ مشایخ نے کہا ہے کہ وہ عرب کے محاورہ کے مطابق ہے۔ یعنی ان کے محاورہ میں بہیت ایک کمرہ ہے۔ اور جس میں کئی کمرے ہوں وہ منزل ہے اور جس میں کئی منزلیں ہوں وہ دار ہے ۔ مگر ہمارے عرف میں دار و منزل و بیت سب کو گھر ہی کہتے ہیں۔ اس لیئے وہ صحن میں جانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس پر فتویٰ ہے۔ یہی عینی و فتح القدیر وغیرہ فقہ کی کتابوں میں مذکور ہے۔
Number: He swore that he will not enter this bayt (room/house). And he had no specific intention. Then he came into the enclosure in which this bayt is located. Then he is not hanith. The mashayikh said that this is in accordance with Arab idiom. That is, in their idiom, bayt is a room. And that in which there are many rooms is manzil (house), and that in which there are many houses is dar (mansion). But in our custom, all of dar, manzil, and bayt are called house (ghar). Therefore, if he enters the courtyard, he becomes hanith. And upon this is the fatwa. This is mentioned in books of fiqh like al-'Inayah and Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses the interpretation of the word 'bayt' in oaths. The mashayikh clarify that in Arabic, 'bayt' specifically means a single room, while 'manzil' is a house with multiple rooms, and 'dar' is a large dwelling with multiple houses. However, in the local custom (South Asia), all these terms are synonymous for 'house'. In an oath, if the swearer has no specific intention, the term is interpreted according to local custom ('urf). Therefore, entering the courtyard of a house containing the specific room he swore about would break his oath, because in local usage 'bayt' means the entire dwelling. The fatwa is according to local custom, as mentioned in authoritative Hanafi commentaries.

Key Terms
بیت (bayt): In Arabic: a room. In local custom: a house.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom or common usage that influences legal interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to enter a 'bayt' (room) but enters the courtyard of the house, does he break the oath according to fatwa?
A) No, because the courtyard is not the room.
B) Yes, because in local custom 'bayt' means the whole house.
C) Only if he also enters the room.
D) Only if he intended the courtyard.

Key Takeaway
When no intention is specified in an oath, the Hanafi school interprets ambiguous terms like 'bayt' according to local custom ('urf), not the literal Arabic definition.

Oath Not to Leave the Mosque: Attribution by Command
Ruling on oath breach through commanded removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath not to leave a mosque when the oath-taker commands another to carry him out, and apply the principle of attribution (isnad) to determine if the oath is broken.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, commanding another to act makes the act yours — like riding an animal that moves.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in oaths: when a person swears not to leave a mosque, but orders someone to carry him out. Does he break the oath? Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir provides the ruling using the principle of attribution. The key is whether the act is directly or indirectly caused by the oath-taker's command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the action
The oath is not to leave the mosque. The action in question is ordering another person to carry you out.
2
Determine the ruling
If the oath-taker commands someone to carry him out, he becomes hanith (breaks the oath). If he is forced out or merely gives consent without a command, he does not break the oath.
The consequence of breaking the oath is that kaffarah (expiation) becomes wajib.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: analogising to riding an animal that moves out. Just as the rider's act of moving is attributed to him even though the animal does the walking, the commander's act of leaving is attributed to him through his command.
The analogy is explicit in the text: 'فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا رَكِبَ دَابَّةً فَخَرَجَتْ'
4
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is that the commander is the direct cause of the act. The commanded person is treated as a tool, just as the animal is a tool for the rider.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas.
5
Note the exceptions
If someone is carried out by force (without his command) or with mere consent (without an explicit command), the act is not attributed to him and he does not break the oath.
Attribution requires an explicit command, not mere agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَخْرُجُ مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ فَأَمَرَ إِنْسَانًا فَحَمَلَهُ فَأَخْرَجَهُ حَيْثُ لَانَّ فِعْلَ الْمَأْمُورِ مُضَافَ إِلَى الْأَمْرِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَارَكِبَ دَابَّةٌ فَخَرَجَتْ وَلَوْ اَخْرَجَهُ مُكْرَها لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ الْفِعْلَ لَمْ يَنْتَقِلْ إِلَيْهِ لِعَدْمِ الْأَمْرِ وَلَوْ حَمَلَهُ بِرَضَاهُ لَا بِأَمْرِهِ لَا يَحْنَتُ فِي الصَّحِيحِ لِاَنَّ الْاِنْتِقَالَ بِالْأَمْرِلَا بِمُجَرَّدِ الرِّضَاءِ
He said: And whoever swore that he will not leave the mosque, then he commanded a person to carry him out, and he did so, he becomes hanith, because the action of the commanded one is attributed to the commander, so it becomes as if he rode an animal and it went out. And if someone carried him out by force, he does not become hanith, because the act is not transferred to him due to lack of command. And if someone carried him with his consent but not by his command, he does not become hanith in the correct view, because transfer is by command, not mere consent.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The issue: an oath not to leave a mosque. If the oath-taker orders another person to carry him out, it is attributed to him, just as riding an animal that moves out. The analogy: the commanded person is like a tool. However, if someone forces him out without his command, it is not his doing. Similarly, if someone carries him out with his consent but no command, it is not a breach, because consent alone does not make the act his; attribution requires an explicit command. This is the correct view.

Key Terms
مضاف (muḍāf): Attributed; the act of the commanded is legally attributed to the commander.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to leave a mosque, and another person carries him out with his consent but without his command, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because he consented.
B) No, because there was no command.
C) Only if he verbally agreed.
D) Only if he could have prevented it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath not to leave a mosque is broken only if the oath-taker directly or through command causes the exit; mere consent or coercion does not trigger breach.

Oath Restricting Exit to Funeral: Subsequent Acts Not Hanth
Ruling on oath restricting exit; additional errands after allowed exit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between the act of exiting (khuruj) and subsequent movement in oath-related cases.

Core Idea
Only the initial exit from the house counts; once outside, further travel does not break the oath.

This lesson examines a common scenario in oath-related fiqh: when a person restricts his exiting to a specific purpose, does any subsequent movement invalidate the oath? Imam Muhammad provides a clear answer based on the literal meaning of 'exit' (khuruj). The key is that the oath only prohibits the act of leaving the house, not actions taken after one is already outside.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its exception
Locate the exact wording of the oath: 'I will not leave my house except for a funeral.' The exception (istithna') is the funeral. All other exits are prohibited under the oath.
The exception is part of the oath; it defines what is allowed.
2
Recognize the act of exiting (khuruj)
The oath prohibits the act of 'khuruj' (exiting) from the house. Once the person steps outside for the funeral, the act of exiting is complete.
Khuruj is a single act, not a continuous state.
3
Distinguish exit from subsequent travel
After the initial allowed exit, any further movement (e.g., going to the market, visiting someone) is not a new exit from the house. The oath is not concerned with movements after the exit.
The oath does not restrict travel after exiting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَخْرُجُ مِنْ دَارِهِ إِلَّا إِلَى جَنَازَةٍ فَخَرَجَ إِلَيْهَا ثُمَّ أَتَى حَاجَةً أُخْرَى لَمْ يَحْنَثْ لِأَنَّ الْمَوْجُودَ خُرُوجٌ مُسْتَثْنَى وَالْمُضِيُّ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ بِخُرُوجٍ
He said: If he swears that he will not go out of his house except for a funeral, then he goes out for it, then attends to another need, he does not break his oath because what occurred is an excepted exit, and going after that is not an exit.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This is a ruling from Imam Muhammad. If a person swears that he will only leave his house for a funeral, and he leaves for the funeral, then after that he goes somewhere else (for another need), he does not break his oath. The reason is that the exit was the exception (for the funeral), and what happens after the initial exit is not considered a new exit from the house; he is already outside. The oath only restricts the act of exiting, not subsequent movements. This is a straightforward ruling without additional proofs.

Key Terms
خروج مستثنى (khurūj mustathnā): An exit that is excepted from the oath; the oath prohibits all exits except the specified one.
خروج (khurūj): The act of exiting from a place, specifically leaving the house in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears: 'I will not leave my house except for a funeral.' He goes out for a funeral, then goes to the market. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he left for a non-funeral purpose.
B) Yes, because he left again for the market.
C) No, because the initial exit was for the funeral, and subsequent movement is not a new exit.
D) No, because going to the market is also an exception.

Key Takeaway
The oath only prohibits the initial act of exiting; after an allowed exit, subsequent travel does not break the oath.

8 min
Oath to Go to Mecca: Linguistic Precision in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on oaths with different verbs of travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each verb (yakhruj, ya'ti, yadhab) and explain the intra-madhhab dispute over yadhab.

Core Idea
The verb in an oath determines when it is broken — departure or arrival.

In Hanafi oaths, the specific verb used matters greatly. The same act of travel can break an oath at different moments depending on whether the oath uses 'exit', 'come', or 'go'. This passage from Al-Hidayah illustrates this principle and an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three verb formulations
List the three verbs used in the oath: yakhruj (exit), ya'ti (come), yadhab (go).
These are common verbs of travel in Arabic.
2
Determine the moment of breaking for each
For yakhruj: breaking upon departure (khuruj) with intent; for ya'ti: breaking upon entry (dukhul); for yadhab: dispute — preferred view is like yakhruj.
The author explicitly states the rulings.
3
Analyze the linguistic basis
Khuruj means separation from inside to outside; itiyan means arrival; dhabab means departure (zawal).
Linguistic precision drives the rulings.
4
Note the Quranic evidence for itiyan
The verse 'fa'tiya Fir'awna' (Quran 20:47) shows that itiyan means arrival, hence requires entry.
Dalil is from Quran but its implication is zanni.
5
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute on yadhab
Some say yadhab is like itiyan (requires arrival); the author prefers like yakhruj because dhabab means departure. This is al-ashahh.
The tarjih is based on linguistic meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَخْرُجُ إِلَى مَكَّةَ فَخَرَجَ يُرِيدُهَا ثُمَّ رَجَعَ حَنِثَ لِوُجُودِ الْخُرُوجِ عَلَى قَصْدِ مَكَّةَ وَهُوَ الشَّرْطُ إِذِ الْخُرُوجُ هُوَ الِانْفِصَالُ مِنَ الدَّاخِلِ إِلَى الْخَارِجِ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْتِيَهَا لَمْ يَحْنِثْ حَتَّى يَدْخُلَهَا لِأَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الْوُصُولِ قَالَ اللهُ تَعَالَى فَأْتِيَا فِرْعَوْنَ فَقُولَا لَهُ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَذْهَبُ إِلَيْهَا قِيلَ هُوَ كَالْإِتْيَانِ وَقِيلَ كَالْخُرُوجِ وَهُوَ الْأَصَحُ لِأَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الزَّوَالِ
If he swears that he will not go out (yakhruj) to Mecca, then he goes out intending it, then returns, he breaks his oath because the exit with intent to go to Mecca occurred, and that is the condition, because exit is separation from inside to outside. If he swears that he will not come (ya'ti) to it, he does not break his oath until he enters it, because it is expression of arrival. Allah Almighty said: 'So go (fa'tiya) to Pharaoh and say to him.' If he swears that he will not go (yadhab) to it, some say it is like coming (ya'ti), and some say it is like exiting (yakhruj), and that is the most correct, because it is expression of departure.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of different verbs in oaths concerning going to a place. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute with some difference of opinion. The author, following Imam Muhammad, clarifies that the verb 'yakhruj' refers to the act of exiting, so if one starts the journey with intent, the oath is broken even if he returns. The verb 'ya'ti' (come) refers to arrival, so the oath is only broken upon entering the place. The evidence for 'ya'ti' is the Quranic verse 'fa'tiya Fir'awn' meaning 'come to Pharaoh' i.e., arrive. For 'yadhab' (go), there are two views: one likens it to 'ya'ti' (requires arrival) and the other to 'yakhruj' (requires departure). The author states the latter is more correct (al-ashahh) because 'yadhab' means departure from one's place. This is a useful lesson on how word choice matters in oaths.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khurūj): Exiting; separation from inside to outside. In oaths, it means the act of leaving the present location.
الإتيان (al-ītiyān): Coming; arrival at the destination.
الذهاب (al-dhahāb): Going; departure from one's place. In the preferred view it is like khuruj.
الحنث (al-hanth): The breaking of an oath; when the condition prohibited in the oath occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which verb requires actual entry into Mecca to break the oath?
A) Yakhruj
B) Ya'ti
C) Yadhab (preferred view)
D) All three

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the verb determines the act that breaks the oath; 'go' (yadhab) is treated like 'exit' (yakhruj) in the preferred view.

When Hanth Occurs in an Oath of Future Action
Ruling on oath to go to Basra; dies before fulfilling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an oath to perform a future act is broken if the swearer dies before fulfillment, and explain the legal reasoning based on the possibility of fulfillment.

Core Idea
An oath is broken only at the last moment of life because until then fulfillment was possible.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Imam Muhammad's Al-Jami' al-Saghir: a person swears to go to Basra but dies before going. When exactly does the oath break? The answer reveals a key principle in Hanafi oath law—the timing of hanth depends on the continuous possibility of fulfillment. Understanding this helps you apply similar rulings to other delayed obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath to perform a future action (going to Basra). The ruling: if the person dies before fulfilling, the oath is broken (hanth).
The hanth is not immediate but delayed.
2
Locate the dalil
The source is the statement of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah). The proof is the logical reasoning (talil): 'because fulfillment before that was possible.'
No explicit Quran or Hadith is cited; the ruling is based on the nature of obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase 'before that was possible' indicates that as long as the person is alive, he could theoretically fulfill the oath. Only at death does the possibility cease, so hanth must occur at the last moment of life.
This shows that possibility (imkan) is a legal factor in determining breach.
4
Consider the scope
This principle applies to any oath to perform an act that is possible to do until death. If the act becomes impossible earlier (e.g., the place is destroyed), hanth might occur earlier.
The ruling is limited to oaths with a continuous window for performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَأْتِيَنَّ الْبَصْرَةَ فَلَمْ يَأْتِهَا حَتَّى مَاتَ حَنِثَ فِي آخِرِ جُزْءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ حَيَاتِهِ لِأَنَّ الْبَرَّ قَبْلَ ذَلِكَ مُمْكِنٌ
He said: If he swears that he will certainly come to Basra, then he does not come until he dies, he breaks his oath in the last part of the parts of his life, because fulfillment before that was possible.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This ruling from Al-Jami' al-Saghir (Imam Muhammad) explains a situation where a person swears to go to Basra but dies before going. The oath is considered broken only at the very last moment of life, because before that there was a possibility that he might fulfill it. The hanth occurs at death because that is when the possibility ends. This shows that in oaths with a future obligation, the time for fulfillment extends until the moment of death. This is a ruling_statement with implied reasoning (talil) about the possibility of fulfillment.

Key Terms
جزء الحياة (juz' al-ḥayāh): Part of life; the final moment before death is the last part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At the time he swears.
B) At the moment he decides not to go.
C) In the last moment of his life.
D) He never breaks the oath because he is dead.

Key Takeaway
An oath to perform a future act is broken only at the moment of death because possibility of fulfillment exists until that point.

Oath Conditioned on Capacity (Istita'ah): Health vs Power
Explanation of istita'ah in oaths: health vs. divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default interpretation of 'istita'ah' in Hanafi oath rulings and distinguish between the judicial (qada') and religious (diyana) acceptance of a divergent intention.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'if able' defaults to health and means, not divine power; intentions of the real meaning are accepted only before Allah.

When a person swears an oath conditioned on 'ability' (istita'ah), Hanafi jurists must determine what the oathbreaker meant by that term. Does it refer to the theological capacity that Allah creates at the moment of action, or the ordinary capacity of being healthy and unobstructed? This passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir resolves the question by appealing to customary usage, and then addresses whether a person's claim of intending the theological meaning is accepted in court or only in conscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and condition
The oath is 'I will come tomorrow if I am able'. The condition is 'if I am able' (istita'ah).
2
State the default interpretation
The default meaning of istita'ah is capacity of health and means (sihha), not divine power (qudra).
3
Apply the conditions from Al-Jami' al-Saghir
If the person is healthy, not prevented by the ruler, and no other impediment, then the condition is met. Failure to act results in breaking the oath (hanth).
These are the three factors that indicate ability in the common sense.
4
Analyze the proof: customary usage
The real (haqiqi) meaning of istita'ah is what accompanies the act, but common usage (urf) applies it to soundness of instruments. When unrestricted, the word takes the common meaning.
This is a linguistic principle: al-haqiqah al-urfiyyah prevails.
5
Resolve the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on judicial acceptance
If the swearer intended the real meaning (qudra), some say it is accepted both diyanatan and qada'an; the stronger view is only diyanatan, because it contradicts the apparent meaning.
The court rules based on apparent meaning, not hidden intentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَيَأْتِيَنَّهُ غَدًا إِنِ اسْتَطَاعَ فَهَذَا عَلَى اسْتِطَاعَةِ الصِّحَّةِ دُونَ الْقُدْرَةِ وَفَسَّرَهُ فِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَقَالَ إِذَا لَمْ يَمْرَضْ وَلَمْ يَمْنَعْهُ السُّلْطَانُ وَلَمْ يَعْرِضْ لَهُ أَمْرٌ لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى إِتْيَانِهِ فَلَمْ يَأْتِ حَنِثَ وَإِنْ عَنَى اسْتِطَاعَةَ الْقَضَاءِ دَيَّنَ فِيمَا بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الِاسْتِطَاعَةِ فِيمَا يُقَارِنُ الْفِعْلَ وَيُطْلَقُ الْإِسْمُ عَلَى سَلَامَةِ الْآلَاتِ وَصِحَّةِ الْأَسْبَابِ فِي الْمُتَعَارِفِ فَعِنْدَ الْإِطْلَاقِ يَنْصَرِفُ إِلَيْهِ وَتَصِحُّ نِيَّةُ الْأَوَّلِ دِيَانَةً لِأَنَّهُ نَوَى حَقِيقَةَ كَلَامِهِ ثُمَّ قِيلَ يَصِحُّ قَضَاءً أَيْضًا لِمَا بَيَّنَّا وَقِيلَ لَا يَصِحُّ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ
If he swears: 'I will certainly come to you tomorrow, if I am able' then this is upon capacity of health, not divine power. And Imam Muhammad explained it in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he does not fall ill, and the ruler does not prevent him, and there does not occur to him something that makes him unable to come, and he still does not come, he breaks his oath. If he intended capacity of divine decree (al-qada'), he is to be believed between himself and Allah. This is because the real meaning of capacity is what accompanies the act, but the name is commonly used for soundness of instruments and health of causes. So when it is unrestricted, it applies to the common meaning. It is valid for him to intend the first meaning (real capacity) as a matter of faith (diyanatan) because he intended the real meaning of his words. Then it is said that it is also valid judicially (qada'an), and it is said it is not valid because it is contrary to the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir explains the meaning of 'istita'ah' (capacity) when used as a condition in oaths. The default interpretation is capacity in the sense of health and external means (sihha), not the divine capacity (qudra) that Allah creates at the moment of action. For example, if a person swears to come tomorrow 'if I am able', and he is healthy, free from obstacles, and not prevented, then he breaks his oath if he fails to come. If he claims that he intended the Islamic theological meaning of capacity (the power that must accompany the act), his claim is accepted religiously (diyanah) but not judicially (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent meaning. There is a difference of opinion on whether it is accepted judicially: some say yes, others no. The author probably holds the latter view. This shows how legal interpretation of common words in oaths relies on customary usage.

Key Terms
استطاعة الصحة (istiṭā‘at al-ṣiḥḥa): Capacity in the sense of being healthy and having the necessary means, without divine power.
استطاعة القدرة (istiṭā‘at al-qudra): Capacity in the sense of the divine power that accompanies the act; the real theological capacity.
ديانة (diyāna): Religiously; between the person and Allah, as opposed to legally in a court.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judicially; in a court of law, based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears: 'I will meet you tomorrow, if Allah enables me (in istata'tu bil-qudra).' He claims he intended the theological capacity (qudra) and did not come. He is healthy and nothing prevented him. Judicially, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the default interpretation is health and means.
B) No, because his intention is valid.
C) Yes, because he did not come.
D) No, because he invoked Allah's enabling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath rulings, 'if able' defaults to health and means; claims of intending divine power are accepted only before Allah, not in court.

Oath to Go to Basra: Hanath Upon Death
Ruling on timing of hanath in a non-time-bound oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when hanath occurs in a non-time-bound oath and apply the Hanafi principle to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
In a non-time-bound oath, hanath occurs only at the last moment of life because fulfillment was possible until then.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling from al-Quduri: an oath to go to Basra without a time limit. The key question is when the oath becomes broken (hanath) if the person dies without going. The Hanafi school, as presented here, determines that hanath occurs at the very end of life, just before death. This is based on the principle that the possibility of fulfillment exists until the last moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is an oath to come to Basra with no time limit. The ruling is that if the person dies without fulfilling it, he becomes hanith (sinful) only at the last moment of life.
The ruling specifies the exact timing of hanath.
2
Understand the Legal Cause (Talil)
The reason given is that before death, fulfillment was possible (al-barr kana mumkinan). As long as the person is alive, he could still fulfill the oath. Therefore, hanath cannot be established before death.
The talil is a rational argument, not a scriptural proof.
3
Consider the Intra-Hanafi Variance
Some later scholars argued that hanath occurs when the person begins to leave or upon actual departure. However, the text asserts that the soundest view (asahh) is that hanath is at death, because the meaning of 'going' is leaving one's place, which is only certain at death.
The mufta bihi position is the one stated in the matn.
4
Apply to a Similar Scenario
Take an oath without a time frame (e.g., 'I will give charity'). If the person dies without doing it, hanath occurs at death. If the oath had a time limit (e.g., 'by Friday'), hanath occurs immediately when the deadline passes.
The presence or absence of a time condition is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَأْتِيَنَّ الْبَصْرَةَ فَلَمْ يَأْتِهَا حَتَّى مَاتَ حَنِثَ فِي آخِرِ جُزْءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ حَيَاتِهِ لِأَنَّ الْبَرَّ قَبْلَ ذَلِكَ كَانَ مُمْكِنًا
He said: And if someone swore, 'I will definitely come to Basra,' and he did not come to it until he died, he becomes sinful (hanith) in the last part of his life, because before that it was possible (that he would fulfill it).
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam al-Quduri, commented upon by the author of Ashraf al-Hidayah. It deals with an oath to perform an act (coming to Basra) that is not bound by a specific time. The ruling: if the person dies without fulfilling the oath, he becomes hanith (sinful for breaking the oath) only at the last moment of his life, just before death. The reason is that as long as he was alive, there was hope that he might still fulfill the oath. The hanath is not considered before that because the possibility of fulfillment existed. This is a standard Hanafi position from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
حَنِثَ (hanitha): To break an oath, incurring sin and required expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does hanath occur for someone who swears to go to Basra but dies before going?
A) Immediately upon swearing
B) When he decides not to go
C) In the last part of his life
D) Never, because death excuses the oath

Key Takeaway
In a non-time-bound oath, hanath occurs at the last moment of life because the possibility of fulfillment existed until then.

Permission Required for Each Exit in an Oath
Ruling on oath with permission exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether one permission suffices for multiple exits in a wife's oath, and distinguish the effect of different exception clauses.

Core Idea
An oath with 'except with my permission' requires permission for each act; finality words end the oath entirely.

Imagine a husband swears that his wife will not go out without his permission, then permits her once. Does that permission cover all future exits, or is a new permission needed each time? The Hanafi school has a clear answer based on the wording of the oath. This lesson will help you navigate the nuances of exception clauses in oaths, distinguishing between conditional permission and finality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the exception
Read the oath: 'My wife will not go out except with my permission.' The exception (istithna') is 'with my permission.' Determine what the exception excludes.
The exception is tied to each act of exiting, not a single blanket permission.
2
Apply the ruling to repeated exits
If the wife exits once with permission and then later without permission, the oath is broken (hanath) because the permission does not carry over. Each exit requires its own permission.
The exception is interpreted narrowly to cover only the act coupled with permission.
3
Understand the diyanatan vs qaḍa'an distinction
If the swearer intended one permission to suffice, his inner intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qaḍa'an) because his words conflict with the apparent meaning.
This protects the oath-breaker from sin but not from judicial consequences.
4
Contrast with finality clauses
If the oath uses 'except that I permit you' or 'until I permit you,' then one permission ends the oath entirely. After that, any exit without permission does not break the oath.
The word 'until' indicates a limit (ghayah), so the oath terminates upon fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا تَخْرُجُ اِمْرَأَتُهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ فَاذَنَ لَهَامَرَّةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ مَرَّةً أُخْرَى بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ حَيْتَ وَلَا بُدَّ مِنَ الْإِذْنِ فِي كُلّ خُرُوجِ لِاَنَّ الْمُسْتَنى حُرُوجٌ مَقْرُونَ بِالإِذْنِ وَمَا وَرَاءَ هُ دَاخِلٌ فِي الْحَظْرِ الْعَامِ وَلَوْنَوَى الْإِذْنَ مَرَّةً يُصَدَّقُ دِيَانَةٌ لَاقَضَاءً لأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ لكِنَّهُ خِلَافَ الظَّاهِرِ وَلَوْ قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ اذَنَ لَكِ فَسَاذَنَ لَهَا مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ بَعْدَهَا بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ هَذِهِ كَلِمَةٌ غَايَةٌ فَيَنْتَهِي الْيَمِينُ بِهِ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ حَتَّى اذَنَ لَكِ
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میری بیوی میری اجازت کے بغیر باہر نہیں جائے گی یعنی اپنی عورت سے یہ کہا کہ اگر تم میری اجازت کے بغیر باہر گئی تو تم کو طلاق ہے۔ پھر اس عورت کو ایک بار باہر جانے کی اجازت دی اس کے بعد وہ باہر جا کر واپس آئی پھر دوسری بار نئی اجازت کے بغیر چلی گئی تو اسے طلاق واقع ہوگئی۔ ایسی قسم میں ہر بار باہر جانے کے لئے نئی اجازت کا ہونا ضروری ہے کیونکہ قسم سے بچنے کے لئے اس نے ایسی قسم سے مستثنیٰ کیا ہے جو اجازت کے ساتھ ہو۔ اس لئے اجازت کے بغیر جس طرح بھی وہ جائے گی اس قسم کے حکم میں داخل رہے گی۔ البتہ اگر اس نے کہتے وقت ہی یہ نیت کی ہو کہ صرف ایک مرتبہ کی اجازت کافی ہے۔ تو دریا تا اس کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ قضاء نہیں یعنی قاضی اس کی تصدیق نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ معنی اگر چہ اس کے کلام سے نکلتے ہیں مگر ظاہر کے خلاف ہیں۔ اور اگر یوں کہا کہ اگر تم باہر کی تو تم کو طلاق ہے یہاں تک کہ تم کو میں اجازت دوں پھر ایک مرتبہ اسے اجازت دیدی اور وہ باہر گئی پھر وہ دوبارہ اجازت کے بغیر ہی نکل گئی تو وہ حانث نہ ہوگا۔ یعنی اس عورت کو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ یہ کلمہ (الی ان ) انتہا کے لئے مستعمل ہے لہذا اس کے معنی یہ ہوئے کہ تمہارے نکلنے پر طلاق کا حکم اس وقت تک باقی رہے گا کہ میں تم کو اجازت دیدوں۔ اس لئے اجازت دینے پر قسم کی انتہاء ہو جائے گی۔ جیسے کہ اگر یوں کہا کہ اگر میری اجازت کے بغیر نکلی تو تم کو طلاق ہو گی۔ یہاں تک کہ میں تم کو اجازت دے دوں۔
He said: Whoever swears that his wife will not go out except with his permission, then he permitted her once and she went out, then she went out another time without his permission, he is guilty (hanatha). Permission is necessary for every exit because the exception is an exit coupled with permission, and what is beyond that is included in the general prohibition. If he intended permission once (i.e., that one permission suffices), he is believed religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qaḍa'an) because it is a possible meaning of his words but contrary to the apparent. And if he said 'except that I permit you', and he permitted her once, then she went out, then after that she went out without his permission, he is not guilty (lam yahnath) because this word is a term of finality (ghayah), so the oath ends with it, just as if he said 'until I permit you'.

Translation: Al-Quduri said: If someone swore that his wife will not go out without his permission, i.e., he said to his wife: 'If you go out without my permission, you are divorced.' Then he gave her permission once to go out, and she went out and returned, then she went out a second time without new permission, then divorce occurred. In such an oath, new permission is required for every exit, because to avoid the oath he made an exception that is connected to permission. So any exit without permission falls under the general prohibition. However, if at the time of speaking he intended that one permission is sufficient, then he is believed religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qaḍa'an), because although this meaning is possible from his words, it is contrary to the apparent. And if he said: 'If you go out, you are divorced, until I permit you,' and then he permitted her once, and she went out, then she went out again without permission, he is not guilty (hanath), i.e., divorce does not occur, because the word 'until' (ila an) indicates a limit, so the meaning is that the ruling of divorce upon your exit remains until I permit you. Hence, upon giving permission, the oath ends. Similarly, if he said: 'If you go out without my permission, you are divorced, until I permit you.'
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the ruling on an oath regarding a wife's exit. The key principle is that when an oath makes an exception for permission, the permission is attached to each individual act of exiting. Therefore, a single permission does not suffice for all future exits; each exit requires a fresh permission. However, if the swearer intended that one permission would cover all, his internal intention is accepted in religious matters (diyanatan) but not in a court of law (qaḍa'an) because his words conflict with the apparent meaning. If the oath uses the word 'illa an' (except that) or 'hatta' (until), then giving permission once ends the oath entirely, so subsequent exits without permission do not break the oath. This distinction hinges on whether the exception word indicates a limit or a condition.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath or vow, typically involving a condition and consequence.
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath by fulfilling or violating the condition.
استثناء (istithna'): An exception clause in an oath, excluding certain actions from the prohibition.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously or before Allah; an internal state accepted in matters of faith but not in court.
قضاء (qaḍa'an): Judicially; in a court of law, based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears, 'My wife will not go out except with my permission,' and he permits her once, then she goes out again without permission, what is the ruling?
A) He is not guilty because one permission suffices.
B) He is guilty because each exit requires separate permission.
C) He is guilty only if he intended permission for each exit.
D) He is not guilty if she went out for a short time.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath with 'except with my permission' requires permission for every act, but words of finality like 'until' end the oath with one permission.

Oath of Immediate Effect (Yamin al-Fawr)
Explaining the concept of an immediate oath (yamin al-fawr)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of yamin al-fawr and apply it to determine when an immediate oath is broken or not.

Core Idea
An immediate oath prevents only the imminent action; a delayed action does not break the oath.

This lesson introduces the unique Hanafi concept of yamin al-fawr (immediate oath). The ruling is based on customary usage (`urf), which governs the interpretation of oaths. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly applying conditional oaths in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Read the two examples given: a woman about to go out and a man about to beat his slave. In each case, an oath is pronounced conditionally on the imminent action.
The actions are imminent at the moment of the oath.
2
Identify the legal effect
In both examples, when the action is delayed and then performed later, the oath is not broken. The text states 'lam yahnath' (he is not guilty).
3
Grasp the rationale
The justification is that the speaker's intention, according to custom (`urf), is to prevent the specific immediate action, not future actions. Oaths are interpreted based on customary usage.
This is a unique derivation (tafarrada) by Imam Abu Hanifa.

Source Text

فائده .... پس جبکہ ایک بار طلاق دے دی تو قسم کی انتہاء ہوگئی۔ پھر اگر اجازت کے بغیر نکلی تو طلاق نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح اس لفظ میں جو مسئلہ میں مذکور ہے قسم کی انتہاء ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر کسی شخص کی بیوی نے باہر نکلنا چاہا اس وقت شوہر نے کا کہ اگر تم باہر کی تو تم کو طلاق ہے۔ یہ سن کر عورت کچھ دیر بیٹھ گئی۔ پھر باہر نکل گئی تو طلاق نہیں ہوگی۔ اسی طرح اگر زید نے اپنے غلام کو مارنا چاہا اس وقت خالد نے اس سے کہا کہ اگر تم نے اس غلام کو مارا تو میرا غلام آزاد ہے۔ اس وقت زید نے نہیں مارا پھر کچھ دیر بعد مارا تو خالد حانث نہیں ہو گا یعنی اس کا غلام آزاد نہیں ہو گا۔ اس قسم کی قسم کو فوری قسم یمبین فور ) کہا جاتا ہے۔ اس قسم کا استنباط صرف امام ابو حنیفہ رح نے کیا ہے۔ اس نام کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ کہنے والے نے چاہا ہے اس نکلنے اور مارنے سے جس کے گذرنے پر وہ تیار ہو چکا ہے اسے اس کے ارادہ سے روک دے۔ اور عرف میں یہی مراد ہوا کرتی ہے۔ اور قسم کا اعتبار عرف پر ہوتا ہے۔
And if a woman intended to go out, and he said: 'If you go out, you are divorced,' and she sat (delayed), then she went out, he is not guilty. Similarly, if a man intended to beat his slave, and he said to him: 'If you beat him, my slave is free,' and he left him (did not beat then), then he beat him after that, he is not guilty. This is called 'yamin al-fawr' (immediate oath). Abu Hanifa alone (tafarrada) made it apparent (iẓhār). Its justification is that the speaker's intention, customarily ('urfan), is to prevent that specific (immediate) beating or exit, and oaths are based on custom.

Benefit: So when he gave divorce once, the oath ended. Then if she went out without permission, divorce does not occur. Similarly, in this word which is mentioned in the issue, the oath will end. And if a man's wife intended to go out, and the husband said: 'If you go out, you are divorced.' Hearing this, the woman sat for a while, then went out, then divorce does not occur. Similarly, if Zayd intended to beat his slave, and Khalid said to him: 'If you beat this slave, my slave is free.' Zayd did not beat at that time, then after some time he beat, then Khalid is not guilty, i.e., his slave is not freed. This type of oath is called 'immediate oath' (yamin fawr). This derivation (istinbat) was made only by Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). The reason for this name is that the speaker intended to prevent that particular going out and beating which he was about to do, so he stops him from his intention, and in custom ('urf) this is the intended meaning, and oaths are based on custom.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of yamin al-fawr (immediate oath), an inferential ruling unique to Imam Abu Hanifa. The principle is that when someone makes an oath conditional on an action that the other party is about to do imminently, the oath is meant to prevent that specific impending action. If the action is deferred and done later, the oath is not broken because the deterrent effect was achieved at the moment of threat. The basis is 'urf (custom): people use such threats to prevent immediate action, not future actions. This is a key Hanafi usul principle that oaths are interpreted according to customary usage. Only Imam Abu Hanifa explicitly derived this rule, though the Sahibayn may have different views elsewhere.

Key Terms
يمين فور (yamin al-fawr): An immediate oath intended to prevent an imminent action; if the action is delayed, the oath is not broken.
عرف ('urf): Customary usage, which governs the interpretation of oaths.
تفرد (tafarrada): He alone (Abu Hanifa) made this derivation; it is not shared by the other founding imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the concept of yamin al-fawr (immediate oath), if a man says to his wife who is about to go out, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' and she delays and goes out later, what is the ruling?
A) She is divorced because the condition was fulfilled.
B) She is not divorced because the oath was meant to prevent the immediate action.
C) She is divorced only if she intended to divorce.
D) The oath is invalid because it was not explicit.

Key Takeaway
The immediate oath (yamin al-fawr) is a unique Hanafi concept where the oath is considered fulfilled by preventing the imminent action, so a delayed action does not break it.

Oath in Response to a Dinner Invitation
Ruling on interpretation of an oath made in response to an invitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a response oath and an initiating oath, and state the ruling on when an oath made in response to an invitation is broken.

Core Idea
A response oath is restricted to the invited meal; adding extra words makes it a new, general oath.

Have you ever wondered if an oath made in reply to an invitation applies everywhere or only to the invitation? This lesson from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies the Hanafi position: the context of a response binds the oath to the specific invitation. Understanding this distinction prevents unintended violations of oaths and highlights the role of contextual clues (qara'in) in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of statement
Determine whether the oath was made in direct response to an invitation (jawab) or initiated independently. The key is whether the wording is purely responsive or adds extra details.
The text contrasts 'If I have lunch' (responsive) with 'If I have lunch today' (initiating).
2
Analyze the scope of the oath
If it is a response, the oath's condition refers only to the invited meal. If the person eats elsewhere, the condition is not triggered. If extra wording (e.g., 'today') is added, the oath becomes independent and covers any meal within that timeframe.
The principle: 'al-jawab yanbit 'ala al-su'al' (the response follows the question).
3
Apply the ruling to the scenario
In the given case, Khalid's oath was in reply to Zayd's invitation to breakfast. When Khalid ate at home, he did not violate the oath because the condition applied only to Zayd's breakfast. If he had added 'today', he would have violated the oath by eating at home.
The outcome: no expiation (kaffarah) required, slave not freed.
4
Generalize the rule
This ruling shows that context (qarinah) shapes legal interpretation. In oaths, the specific wording and situation determine the scope of the obligation.
Similar reasoning applies to other conditional statements in fiqh (e.g., vows, contracts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوْ قَالَ لَهُ رَجُلٌ إِجْلِسُ فَتَغَدَّ عِنْدِى فَقَالَ إِنْ تَعَدَّيْتُ فَعَبْدِي حُرِّفَخَرَجَ فَرَجَعَ إِلَى مَنْزِلِهِ وَتَعَدَّى لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَهُ خَرَجَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ فَيَنْطَبِقُ عَلَى السُّوَالِ فَيَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى الْغَدَاءِ الْمَدْعُو إِلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَاقَالَ إِنْ تَغَدَّيْتُ الْيَوْمَ لِأَنَّهُ زَادَ عَلَى حَرْفِ الْجَوَابِ فَيُجْعَلُ مَبْتَدِئًا
ترجمہ اگر زید نے خالد سے کہا کہ آؤ بیٹھو اور میرے ساتھ ناشتہ کھا لو۔ تو خالد نے جواب دیا اگر میں ناشتہ کھالوں تو میراغلام آزاد ہے۔ پھر خالد نے وہاں سے اٹھ کر اپنے گھر میں جا کر ناشتہ کھالیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ یعنی اس کا غلام آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خالد کا کلام زید کے جواب کے طور پر تھا۔ اس لئے اس جواب کو زید کے سوال کے مطابق سمجھنا ہوگا۔ لہذازید کے ناشتہ پر ہی اس کی قسم موقوف رہے گی جس کی اس نے دعوت دی تھی۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر خالد نے جواب میں یہ کہا ہوتا کہ اگر آج میں ناشتہ کھالوں تو میر اغلام آزاد ہے۔ یعنی اس صورت میں خالد حانث ہو کر اس کا غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ متعین جواب سے بڑھا کر جواب دیا ہے۔ اس لئے اسے نئے طور پر قسم کھانے والا کہا جائے گا۔
If a man said to him: 'Sit and have lunch with me,' and he said: 'If I have lunch, my slave is free,' then he went out and returned to his house and had lunch, he is not guilty because his speech came in the context of a response, so it applies to the question (i.e., the invitation), thus it is directed to the invited lunch. Contrary to if he said: 'If I have lunch today,' because he added to the response wording, so he is considered as initiating (a new oath).

Translation: If Zayd said to Khalid: 'Come, sit and have breakfast with me.' Khalid replied: 'If I have breakfast, my slave is free.' Then Khalid got up from there and went to his own house and had breakfast, he is not guilty, i.e., his slave is not freed, because Khalid's speech was in response to Zayd. Therefore, this response must be understood according to Zayd's question. Hence, his oath is restricted to the breakfast to which he was invited. In contrast, if Khalid had said in response: 'If I have breakfast today, my slave is free,' then in that case Khalid would be guilty and his slave would be freed, because he added beyond the specific response, so he is considered as a new swearer.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies the interpretation of oaths made in response to an invitation. The key principle is that when someone makes an oath in reply to an invitation, the oath is construed as applying only to the specific invited meal, not to a general meal. This is because the speech is in the context of a response (jawab) and must align with the invitation (su'al). If the person adds extra words like 'today,' it becomes an independent oath no longer tied to the invitation, thus applying to any meal that day. The ruling demonstrates the importance of context (qarinah) in interpreting oaths in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
جواب (jawab): A response or reply, which determines the scope of an oath made in reply to a question or invitation.
سوال (su'al): The question or invitation that prompts the oath.
مبتدئ (mubtadi'): One who initiates an oath independently, not as a response.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man is invited to lunch and replies, 'If I have lunch, my slave is free.' He then goes home and has lunch. What is the ruling?
A) His slave is freed because he fulfilled the condition.
B) His slave is not freed because the oath applied only to the invited lunch.
C) His slave is freed only if he added the word 'today'.
D) The oath is invalid because it was conditional.

Key Takeaway
When an oath is made in response to an invitation, it is interpreted as applying only to the specific subject of the invitation; extra wording can turn it into an independent oath.
