Riding the Animal of a Trade-Slave: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on oath involving a madhun slave's animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the effect of swearing not to ride the animal of a person's trade-permitted slave, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of their differences.

Core Idea
If you swear not to ride Zayd's animal, riding his slave's animal may or may not break your oath—depending on debt and intention.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of oaths when the object (the animal) is owned by a person but customarily attributed to his trade-permitted slave. The three founding imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—diverge on whether the oath is violated, based on factors such as the slave's indebtedness and the swearer's intention. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply usul principles like ownership, custom ('urf), and the role of intention in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the oath scenario: a man swears not to ride 'the animal of so-and-so.' He then rides the animal of so-and-so's trade-permitted slave (madhun). The question is: does this breach the oath?
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, involving all three founding imams.
2
Outline Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the slave's indebtedness. If the slave is fully indebted (dayn mustaghriq), there is no ownership for the master, so the oath is never broken even if intended. If not fully indebted, the master owns the animal, but custom attributes it to the slave; thus, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended the slave's animal.
Abu Hanifa's view is the most nuanced, considering both ownership and custom.
3
Explain Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that in all cases, the oath is broken if the swearer intended the slave's animal. The attribution to the master is defective, so intention is necessary.
Abu Yusuf focuses on the defective attribution, requiring intention.
4
Explain Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the oath is always broken, regardless of intention or debt, because the real ownership is with the master. Custom and debt do not affect the legal reality.
Muhammad prioritizes the legal ownership ('milk') over customary attribution.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a person may be guilty or not depending on which imam's view is followed. In fatwa, a mufti must know which position is operative in his school.
Later Hanafi works often prefer Abu Hanifa's view as more comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَرْكَبُ دَابَّةَ فَلَانٍ فَرَكِبَ دَابَّةَ عَبْدِ مَا دُونَ لَهُ مَدْيُونٌ أَوْ غَيْرُ مَدْيُونٍ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ عِندَ ابِي حَنِيفَةَ إِلَّا إِنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ عَلَيْهِ دَيْنٌ مُسْتَغْرَقُ وَلَا يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ نَوَى لِأَنَّهُ لَا مِلْكَ لِلْمَوْلَى فِيهِ عِنْدَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ الدَّيْنُ غَيْرُ مُسْتَغْرَقِ أَوَلَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَيْهِ دَيْنٌ لَا يَحْنَتُ مَالَمْ يَنْوِهِ لانَّ الْمِلْكَ فِيهِ لِلْمَوْلَى لَكِنَّهُ يُضَافُ إِلَى الْعَبْدِ عُرُفًا وَكَذَا شَرْعَاقَالَ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ بَاعَ عَبْدًا وَلَهُ مَالٌ فَهُوَ لِلْبَائِعِ الْحَدِيثُ فَتَخْتَلُ الْإِضَافَةُ إِلَى الْمَوْلَى فَلَابُدَّ مِنَ النِّيَّةِ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ فِي الْوُجُوهِ كُلَّهَا يَحْنَتُ إِذَانَوَاهُ لاخْتِلَالِ الْإِضَافَةِ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَنْوِلا عَتِبَارِ حَقِيقَةِ الْمِلْكِ إِذِ الدَّيْنُ لَا يَمْنَعُ وُقُوعَهُ لِلسَّيِّدِ عِندَهُما
ترجمہ اگر مثلا زید نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں خالد کے جانور پر سوار نہیں ہوں گا۔ پھر زید خالد کے ایسے غلام کے جانور پر سوار ہوا جسے کاروبار کی اجازت دی ہوئی ہے۔ خواہ ایسا غلام کسی کا مقروض ہو یا نہ ہوتو زید حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ یہ قول امام ابو حنیفہ گا ہے۔ البتہ اگر اس غلام پر اتنا قرض ہو گیا ہو کہ اس کے کل مال اور اس کی ذات کے برابر ہو گیا ہو تو زید ہر گز حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اگر چہ اس نے اس کی نیت بھی کی ہو۔ یعنی خالد یا اس کے غلام کے کسی جانور پر سوار نہ ہونے کی نیت ہو تو بھی حانث نہیں ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ امام اعظم کے نزدیک اس غلام میں مولی کی کچھ بھی ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر وہ غلام اتنے زیادہ قرض میں ڈوبا ہوا نہ ہو یا اس پر کچھ بھی قرض نہ ہو تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ تاوقتیکہ اس کی بھی نیت نہ ہو۔ یعنی اگر یہ نیت کی ہو کہ خالد کے غلام کے جانور پر بھی سوار نہ ہوں گا تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا، ورنہ نہیں کیونکہ ایسے غلام میں موٹی کی ملکیت باقی ہے۔ یعنی اس کے غلام کا جانور بھی مولیٰ کا ہی جانور ہے۔ اگر چہ عرف میں وہ اس غلام کا جانور کہلاتا ہے۔ اور شریعت میں بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ چنانچہ رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے کہ جس نے اپنا غلام بیچا اور غلام کی ملکیت میں کچھ مال ہو تو وہ بائع کا ہو گا۔ الخ اس بناء پر مولی کی طرف نسبت کرنے میں خلل ہوگا لہذا ایسی صورت میں نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہوگا۔ اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ تمام صورتوں میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔ بشرطیکہ غلام کے جانور کی نیت کی ہو۔ کیونکہ مولی کی طرف اس کی نسبت ناقص ہے۔ اسلئے نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ وہ ہر صورت اور ہر حال میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اگر چہ نیت نہ کرے۔ کیونکہ حقیقی ملکیت مولیٰ ہی کی ہے اس لئے اس کا اعتبار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ قرض ہونے سے صاحبین کے نزدیک مولیٰ کی ملکیت کا ہونا منع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
And whoever swears, 'I will not ride the animal of so-and-so,' then he rode the animal of a trade-permitted slave (madhun), whether that slave is indebted or not, he is not guilty according to Abu Hanifa, except that if the slave has a debt that covers all his property (mustaghriq), he is not guilty even if he intended (the slave's animal in his oath) because in Abu Hanifa's view, the master has no ownership in that property. And if the debt is not covering all or there is no debt, he is not guilty unless he intended (the slave's animal) because the ownership is with the master, but it is customarily attributed to the slave, and likewise in shariah. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave and the slave has property, it belongs to the seller' (the hadith). Therefore, the attribution to the master is defective, so intention is necessary. Abu Yusuf said: In all cases he is guilty if he intended (the slave's animal) because the attribution is defective. Muhammad said: He is guilty even if he did not intend, due to considering the reality of ownership, because debt does not prevent ownership from being established for the master in their view (i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's view).

Translation: For example, if Zayd swore, 'I will not ride the animal of Khalid,' then Zayd rode the animal of Khalid's trade-permitted slave (madhun), whether that slave is indebted or not, Zayd is not guilty. This is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa. However, if that slave has so much debt that it covers all his property and his person, then Zayd is never guilty, even if he intended (the slave's animal). Even if he intended not to ride any animal of Khalid or his slave, still he is not guilty, because according to Imam A'zam, the master has no ownership in that slave's property. And if the slave is not overwhelmed by debt or has no debt, then Zayd is not guilty unless he also intended (the slave's animal). If he intended not to ride the animal of Khalid's slave, then he is guilty; otherwise not, because in such a slave, the master's ownership remains. That is, the slave's animal is also the master's animal, although customarily it is called the slave's animal, and the shariah also has this ruling. Thus, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave and the slave has property, it belongs to the seller.' Therefore, the attribution to the master is defective, so in such a case, intention is necessary. Imam Abu Yusuf said: In all cases he is guilty, provided he intended the slave's animal, because the attribution to the master is defective, so intention is necessary. Imam Muhammad said: He is guilty in every case and every situation, even if he did not intend, because the real ownership is the master's, so it is considered, because debt does not prevent the master's ownership according to the Sahibayn.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute involving the three founding imams. The issue is whether swearing not to ride 'the animal of so-and-so' (a specific person) includes the animal of that person's trade-permitted slave (madhun). Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if the slave is heavily in debt (debt consuming all assets), the master has no ownership, so even intending the slave's animal does not cause guilt. If the slave is not so indebted, the master owns the animal, but custom ('urf) attributes it to the slave, so the oath only applies if the swearer intended the slave's animal. Abu Yusuf agrees that intention is needed but says in all cases, if he intends the slave's animal, he is guilty. Muhammad goes further: regardless of intention or debt, he is always guilty because ownership is with the master. The hadith about selling a slave is used to show that attribution to the master is not always direct. The practical consequence: in fatwa, which opinion is relied upon? The text does not explicitly give tarjih, but later Hanafi works often prefer Abu Hanifa's view. This demonstrates the range of ijtihad within the school.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون ('abd madhun): A trade-permitted slave who is allowed by his master to engage in business.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that covers all of a slave's property and even his person, rendering the master's ownership nullified in Abu Hanifa's view.
إضافة (idafa): Attribution or association of property to a person; here, the animal is often attributed to the slave customarily, not to the master.
ملک (milk): Ownership, which is the basis for determining whether the animal falls under the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man swears not to ride the animal of Zayd, and then rides the animal of Zayd's trade-permitted slave who is not indebted, what is the ruling?
A) He is always guilty.
B) He is not guilty unless he intended the slave's animal.
C) He is never guilty.
D) He is guilty only if the slave is indebted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's disagreement on this issue shows that oath interpretation depends on ownership, custom, and intention—all of which must be considered.

Oaths in Response to Invitations: Reply vs. Initiation
Ruling on an oath conditioned on eating breakfast with the inviter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an oath made in response to an invitation is restricted to the invitation's context versus when it becomes an independent oath, and state the ruling for each case.

Core Idea
An oath in reply to an invitation is bound to that invitation; adding extra wording makes it an independent oath.

When you respond to an invitation with a conditional oath, the wording matters. The Hanafi school applies a key interpretive principle: speech that is a direct reply to a question is understood in the context of that question. This lesson examines how this principle applies to oaths concerning eating breakfast at an invitation, and the crucial difference when the oath includes additional time words like 'today'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine the exact wording of the oath and whether it was spoken in direct response to an invitation or independently.
The act is the conditional statement made by the invitee.
2
Assess the wording: reply or initiating?
Check if the oath contains any extra words beyond a simple reply (e.g., adding 'today'). If it is a plain reply, it takes the status of jawab; if extra words are added, it becomes mubtadi' (initiating).
The key phrase is 'خرج مخرج الجواب' (came in the form of a reply).
3
Apply the principle of restriction
For a reply oath, the condition is automatically restricted to the specific invitation context. For an initiating oath, the condition applies generally.
The principle: speech in reply is confined to the subject of the question.
4
State the ruling on hanath
If the condition is not met within the restricted context, the oath is not broken (no hanath). If met in the broader context, the oath is broken and kaffarah is due.
Hanath occurs only if the condition is fulfilled as per the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الخروج والاتيان والركوب وغير ذلك. لَوْ قَالَ لَهُ رَجُلٌ إِجْلِسُ فَتَغَدَّ عِنْدِى فَقَالَ إِنْ تَعَدَّيْتُ فَعَبْدِي حُرِّفَخَرَجَ فَرَجَعَ إِلَى مَنْزِلِهِ وَتَعَدَّى لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَهُ خَرَجَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ فَيَنْطَبِقُ عَلَى السُّوَالِ فَيَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى الْغَدَاءِ الْمَدْعُو إِلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَاقَالَ إِنْ تَغَدَّيْتُ الْيَوْمَ لِأَنَّهُ زَادَ عَلَى حَرْفِ الْجَوَابِ فَيُجْعَلُ مَبْتَدِئًا
Chapter on oaths concerning going out, coming, riding, and other matters. If a man says to him: 'Sit and have breakfast with me,' and he says: 'If I have breakfast, my slave is free,' then he goes out, returns to his home, and has breakfast, he does not become sinful (hanith) because his speech came in the form of a reply, so it corresponds to the question and is restricted to the breakfast to which he was invited. Unlike if he said: 'If I have breakfast today,' because he added to the word of the reply, so he is considered as initiating [an oath].
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on oaths made in response to an invitation. The key principle is that when a person responds to an invitation with a conditional oath, the condition is understood to be tied to the invitation itself. Therefore, if the invitee swears 'if I have breakfast, my slave is free' in response to 'come have breakfast with me,' the condition refers only to having breakfast with the inviter. If the invitee goes home and eats breakfast alone, the oath is not broken (he is not hanith). However, if the invitee adds the word 'today' to his response, saying 'if I have breakfast today,' then he is considered to be initiating a new oath independent of the invitation. In that case, eating breakfast anywhere that day would break the oath. This distinction highlights the importance of context and wording in oaths.

Key Terms
حنث (hanith): The state of breaking an oath, incurring legal consequences such as expiation or freeing a slave.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, typically using the condition 'if... then...'
خرج مخرج الجواب (kharaja makhraj al-jawab): The oath is spoken in the form of a reply to a question or invitation, which restricts its interpretation to that context.
ابتداء (ibtida'): Initiating an oath independently, without being bound by a prior question or invitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person is invited to breakfast and responds, 'If I eat breakfast, my slave is free.' He then eats breakfast at home. According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the status of his oath?
A) He is hanith (oath-breaker) and must free his slave.
B) He is not hanith because the condition was not fulfilled within the invitation context.
C) He is hanith only if he ate at the inviter's house.
D) The oath is invalid because it was conditional.

Key Takeaway
An oath made in direct reply to an invitation is interpreted within the context of that invitation; adding extra wording removes this restriction and makes the oath general.

Oath on Riding the Animal of a Ma'dhun Slave (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath and ma'dhun slave's animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding an oath not to ride a specific person's animal when riding that person's ma'dhun slave's animal, and identify the role of intention and debt status.

Core Idea
An oath on a ma'dhun slave's animal is broken based on intention and debt, causing a three-way Hanafi dispute.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of an oath not to ride a specific person's animal when the rider uses the animal of that person's ma'dhun slave. The three master imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—differ on whether the oath is broken, and the role of the swearer's intention and the slave's debt status. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists balance ownership, custom, and intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and oath
The scenario: a person swears, 'I will not ride the animal of so-and-so.' Then he rides the animal of that person's ma'dhun (permissioned) slave. The question: is the oath broken (hanith)?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the slave's debt: if the slave's debts cover his entire value (mustaghriq), the oath is not broken even with intention, because the master has no ownership. If debts are partial or absent, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended to include the slave's animal.
This is because legal ownership lies with the master, but custom attributes the animal to the slave.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that in all cases, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended to include the slave's animal, because the attribution to the master is imperfect.
He requires intention always, regardless of debt.
4
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the oath is broken even without intention, because real ownership of the animal belongs to the master, and debt does not prevent that ownership.
He gives priority to legal ownership over custom.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a case where the slave is not deeply in debt and the swearer did not intend the slave's animal, the oath is not broken according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but is broken according to Muhammad. This affects whether expiation (kaffarah) is required.
The mufta bihi may follow Abu Hanifa's view, but the text does not specify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَرْكَبُ دَابَّةَ فَلَان فَرَكِبَ دَابَّةَ عَبْدِ مَا دُون لَهُ مَدْيُون أَوْ غَيْرُ مَدْيُون لَمْ يَحْنَتْ عِندَ ابِي حَنِيفَةَ إِلَّا إِنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ عَلَيْهِ دَيْنٌ مُسْتَغْرَقُ وَلَا يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ نَوَى لِأَنَّهُ لَا مِلْكَ لِلْمَوْلَى فِيهِ عِنْدَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ الدَّيْنُ غَيْرُ مُسْتَغْرَقِ أَوَلَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَيْهِ دَيْنٌ لَا يَحْنَتُ مَالَمْ يَنْوِهِ لانَّ الْمِلْكَ فِيهِ لِلْمَوْلَى لَكِنَّهُ يُضَافُ إِلَى الْعَبْدِ عُرُفًا وَكَذَا شَرْعَاقَالَ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ بَاعَ عَبْدًا وَلَهُ مَالٌ فَهُوَ لِلْبَائِعِ الْحَدِيثُ فَتَخْتَلُ الْإِضَافَةُ إِلَى الْمَوْلَى فَلَابُدَّ مِنَ النِّيَّةِ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ فِي الْوُجُوهِ كُلَّهَا يَحْنَتُ إِذَانَوَاهُ لاخْتِلَالِ الْإِضَافَةِ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَنْوِلا عَتِبَارِ حَقِيقَةِ الْمِلْكِ إِذِ الدَّيْنُ لَا يَمْنَعُ وُقُوعَهُ لِلسيدِ عِندَهُما
Whoever swears 'I will not ride the animal of so-and-so,' then rides the animal of a slave who has been given permission [to trade], whether that slave has a debt or not, he does not become hanith according to Abu Hanifa, except that if the slave has a debt that covers his entire value, he does not become hanith even if he intended [to include the slave's animal], because in his view the master has no ownership in it. And if the debt is not covering the entire value or there is no debt, he does not become hanith as long as he does not intend it, because ownership in it belongs to the master, but customarily it is attributed to the slave, and similarly in Sharia. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave who has property, the property belongs to the seller' — the hadith. So the attribution to the master is problematic; thus intention is necessary. Abu Yusuf said: In all cases he becomes hanith if he intended it, because the attribution is imperfect. Muhammad said: He becomes hanith even if he did not intend, considering real ownership, for debt does not prevent [ownership] from being established for the master in their view.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute concerning an oath not to ride a specific person's animal, when the swearer rides the animal of that person's ma'dhun slave (a slave given permission to trade). Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the slave's debt status: if the slave's debts cover his entire value (mustaghriq), the master has no ownership, so the oath is not broken even with intention. If the slave is not heavily in debt, the master owns the animal in reality, but custom attributes it to the slave. Therefore, the oath is only broken if the swearer intended to include the slave's animal. This is based on the principle that oaths follow custom ('urf). Abu Yusuf holds that in all cases, intention is required because the attribution to the master is imperfect. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the oath is broken even without intention, because real ownership prevails. The hadith 'Whoever sells a slave who has property, the property belongs to the seller' is cited to show that in Sharia, property is attributed to the master, but custom may differ. This dispute illustrates the tension between literal ownership and customary attribution in oaths.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, thus can own property and incur debts.
حنث (hanith): The state of breaking an oath.
مستغرق الدين (mustaghriq al-dayn): A slave whose debts exceed or equal his total wealth, rendering him effectively bankrupt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person swears not to ride Zaid's animal, and then rides the animal of Zaid's ma'dhun slave who is not in debt, what is required for the oath to be broken?
A) He automatically breaks the oath.
B) He breaks the oath only if he intended to include the slave's animal.
C) He never breaks the oath because the slave's animal is not Zaid's.
D) He breaks the oath only if the slave is in debt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath involving a ma'dhun slave's property is broken according to custom, intention, and debt status, leading to an intra-school dispute.

Oath Not to Eat Morning Meal with the Inviter
Ruling on hanth based on wording of response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath is broken (hanth) based on the explicit wording of the response, and apply the rule to distinguish between scenarios where the condition is tied to the inviter versus a specific day.

Core Idea
The wording of an oath determines the condition; eating alone does not break an oath conditioned on eating with the inviter.

Oaths in Islamic law are binding promises that carry consequences if broken. However, the exact wording of the oath determines the condition upon which the consequence (hanth) depends. In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, we examine a case where a person swears that if he eats the morning meal, his slave is free. The ruling hinges on whether the response ties the condition to eating with the inviter or makes it absolute for the day. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi principles of oath interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and Its Wording
Read the two scenarios: (1) The invitee responds 'If I eat the morning meal' without specifying time; (2) He responds 'I will not eat the morning meal today'. Note the difference in wording.
2
Determine the Implicit Condition
In the first scenario, the context (responding to an inviter) implies the condition is eating with the inviter. In the second, the addition of 'today' makes the condition about the day, independent of the inviter.
Hanafi rule: oaths are interpreted literally and contextually.
3
Analyze the Trigger for Hanth
For hanth to occur, the condition must be fulfilled exactly as stated. In scenario 1, only eating with the inviter triggers hanth; eating elsewhere does not. In scenario 2, any morning meal that day triggers hanth.
The existence of the condition (shart) is necessary for hanth.
4
Apply the Rule to a New Scenario
Consider a person who says 'If I eat lunch with Ahmed, my slave is free' and then eats alone at home. Apply the same principle: the condition is tied to Ahmed, so no hanth.
The ruling is consistent: wording determines the condition.

باب اليمين في الخروج والاتيان والركوب وغير ذلك .
۸۰
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم کہہ دیا کہ اگر میں نے صبح کا کھانا کھا لیا تو میرا اعلام آزاد ہے۔ تو اس صورت میں داعی کے ساتھ کھانا کھا لینے سے وہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی اس کا غلام آزاد ہوگا۔ کیونکہ متکلم کا کلام داعی کے ساتھ صبح کا کھانا کھانے کی شرط کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ چنانچہ اس وقت داعی کے ساتھ صبح کا کھانا کھانے کی صورت میں شرط پائے جانے سے حث مستلزم ہوگا۔ اگر بد عو نے داعی کے صبح کا ناشتہ نہیں کیا بلکہ اپنے گھر جا کر صبح کے وقت کھانا کھایا تو حانث نہ ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ یمین میں حانث ہونے کیلئے داعی کیساتھ کھانے کی شرط کا وجود ضروری ہے۔ جبکہ یہاں وہ شرط موجود نہیں۔ لہذا داعی کے ساتھ کھانا کھانے کے علاوہ اپنے گھر یا کسی دوسری جگہ پر صبح کا کھانا کھانے سے وہ حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ داعی کی دعوت میں شریک نہ ہونے کیلئے جواب میں مدعو کے مطلق یہ الفاظ اگر میں نے صبح کا کھانا کھانا کافی ہیں۔ بعد میں خواہ داعی کے ساتھ دوسرے وقت کا کھانا کھائے یا خود کسی دوسری جگہ پر کھانا کھائے تو مدعو حانث نہ ہو گا۔ اگر مدعو نے داعی کے جواب میں یہ الفاظ آج صبح کا کھانا نہیں کھاؤں گا“ کہے تو وہ اس دن جہاں بھی صبح کا کھانا کھائے گا حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مدعو نے اپنے کلام میں ” آج کا لفظ بڑھا کر دائی کے جواب کے طور پر کلام نہیں کیا۔ بلکہ اسے از سر نو کر کے آج کے دن سے مشروط کر دیا۔ اور یہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ یمین میں حانث ہونے کیلئے شرط کا وجود مستلزم ہے۔ چنانچہ لفظ " آج کی شرط یا قید کے بعد مدعو آج کے دن جہاں بھی صبح کا کھانا کھائے گا حانث ہو جائے گا۔ بایں وجہ کہ مدعو کے مذکورہ جملے ( آج صبح کا کھانا نہیں کھاؤں گا ) میں دائی کے ساتھ ناشتے کی شرط موجود نہیں۔ اسلئے اگر اس نے داعی کے علاوہ کسی دوسری جگہ پر بھی صبح کا کھانا کھا لیا تو لفظ ” آج کے حوالے سے شرط موجود ہونے کے باعث اس پر جسٹ لازم ہو گا۔ یعنی اس کی قسم ٹوٹ جائے گی اور غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
He said: If I have eaten the morning meal, then my slave is free. In this case, by eating with the inviter, he becomes hanith, meaning his slave is free. Because the speaker's speech requires the condition of eating the morning meal with the inviter. Thus, when the condition of eating with the inviter is fulfilled, hanth is necessitated. If the invitee did not eat breakfast with the inviter but went home and ate at morning time, he does not become hanith, because for hanth in an oath, the existence of the condition of eating with the inviter is necessary. When that condition is not present, he does not become hanith by eating at home or elsewhere besides with the inviter. Because, in responding to the inviter's invitation, the invitee's absolute words 'If I eat the morning meal' are sufficient for not participating in the invitation. Later, whether he eats at another time with the inviter or elsewhere, he does not become hanith. If the invitee responds with the words 'I will not eat the morning meal today,' then wherever he eats that day, he becomes hanith, because the invitee added the word 'today' and did not make his speech as a response to the inviter, but rather made it anew and conditioned it to the day. And it has passed earlier that for hanth in an oath, the existence of the condition is necessitated. Thus, after the condition or restriction of the word 'today', wherever the invitee eats the morning meal that day, he becomes hanith, because the invitee's mentioned sentence ('I will not eat the morning meal today') does not contain the condition of eating with the inviter. Therefore, if he eats the morning meal elsewhere besides with the inviter, hanth is necessitated due to the presence of the condition regarding the word 'today', meaning his oath is broken and his slave becomes free.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya explains a ruling on a specific type of oath conditional on eating with an inviter. The text distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the invitee responds, 'If I eat the morning meal,' the oath is implicitly tied to eating with the inviter. Only eating with him triggers hanth. (2) If the invitee says, 'I will not eat the morning meal today,' the oath becomes absolute for that day, and eating anywhere breaks it. This follows Hanafi principles that oaths are interpreted literally based on the wording used, not unspoken intentions.

Key Terms
حانث (ḥānith): The person who breaks his oath; here, the one fulfilling the condition triggering hanth.
یمین (yamīn): An oath; in this context, a conditional oath with a penalty.
شرط (sharṭ): The condition upon which the oath's consequence depends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person responds to an inviter, 'If I eat the morning meal, my slave is free.' He then eats the morning meal at home alone. What is the ruling?
A) He becomes hanith and his slave is free.
B) He does not become hanith.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) The oath is void because the condition is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the wording of an oath determines the condition; hanth occurs only when the condition is fulfilled exactly as expressed.

Oath Not to Ride a Person's Animal: Ma'dhun Slave Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on riding ma'dhun slave's animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three intra-Hanafi positions on whether riding a ma'dhun slave's animal breaches an oath, and apply the correct ruling based on slave's debt status and intention.

Core Idea
Riding a ma'dhun slave's animal may or may not break an oath; it depends on the slave's debt and the imam's view.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths concerning property. The core question: when a person swears not to ride a specific person's animal, does riding that person's ma'dhun slave's animal constitute a breach? The three primary Hanafi authorities—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—offer different analyses based on ownership attribution, intention, and the slave's debt status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the animal's owner
The swearer (halif) swears not to ride the animal of a specific person (mahloof alayh). The animal ridden belongs to the mahloof alayh's ma'dhun slave. Determine whether the slave's animal is considered the master's property for oath purposes.
The ma'dhun slave is authorized to trade but remains a slave; his property is nominally his but ultimately belongs to the master.
2
Determine the slave's debt status
Check if the slave is mustaghraq fi al-qard (debts exceed assets). This status affects ownership attribution: if mustaghraq, the animal is considered owned by the master in some views.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on this; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not.
3
Apply each imam's principle
For Abu Hanifa: If slave is not mustaghraq, animal is owned by slave, so no hanth. If mustaghraq, animal is attributed to master, so hanth depends on intention. For Abu Yusuf: Hanth occurs in all cases if the swearer intended to ride the slave's animal. For Muhammad: Hanth occurs in all cases regardless of intention, because real ownership is with the master.
Intention is key for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad disregards intention.
4
Conclude whether hanth occurs
Based on the scenario (debt status, intention), determine under each imam's view whether the swearer becomes hanith and thus must perform kaffara.
Hanth requires expiation (kaffara) per Hanafi fiqh.
5
Note the practical consequence
The difference in rulings affects whether the swearer must pay kaffara. The choice of which imam's view to follow depends on the mufti's tarjih and the specific circumstances.
The text does not specify a mufta bihi; in practice, scholars may adopt the view that best fits the situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی میں فلاں ( کوئی بھی مخصوص شخص کے جانور پر سوار نہیں ہوں گا۔ لیکن اس (حالف) نے مخلوف علیہ کے ماذون غلام کے جانور پر سواری کر لی تو وہ امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک حانث نہ ہوگا۔قطع نظر اس سے کہ مخلوف علیہ کا اجازت یافتہ غلام مقروض ہے یا نہیں۔ کیونکہ اجازت یافتہ غلام تا حال معلوف علیہ کی غلامی ہے آزاد نہیں ہوا۔ لیکن اجازت یافتہ ہے۔ اسلئے مقروض یا غیر مقروض دونوں صورتوں میں غلام کا جانور خود اس کی ملکیت میں ہوگا۔ جبکہ حالف نے مخلوف علیہ کے جانور پر سوار نہ ہونے کی قسم کھائی تھی۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک حالف کا ماذون في التجارۃ غلام کے جانور پر سوار ہونا اسے حانث نہیں کرتا۔ غلام مقروض ہو یا نہ ہو۔ البتہ مستغرق فی القرض کی صورت میں ماذون عبد کے جانور پر سوار ہونا حالف کو حانث کر دے گا۔ بایں وجہ کہ آقا کی ملکیت پر مبنی نسبت باقی ہے۔ چنانچہ اس صورت میں حنث کا دارو مدار حالف کی نیت پر ہو گا۔
امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ خالف اس شرط کے ساتھ تمام صورتوں میں حانث ہو گا کہ اس نے ماذون غلام کے جانور پر سوار ہونے کی نیت کی ہو۔ کیونکہ آقا کی طرف اس کی نسبت ناقص ہے۔
امام محمد کا قول ہے کہ حالف پر ہر صورت میں حنث لازم آئے گا۔ ماذون غلام کے جانور پر سوار ہونے کی نیت ضروری نہیں۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں آقا کا حقیقی ملکیت ہونا معتبر ہے۔ صاحبین ایسے غلام جو ماذون بھی ہو اور متفرق فی القرض بھی ہو آقا کی ملکیت کے حوالے سے ان دونوں . مازون و مستغرق فی القرض کو مانع قرار نہ دینے پر متفق ہیں۔ جبکہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مازون غلام مستغرق فی القرض کی صورت میں صاحبین سے اختلاف رکھتے ہیں۔ امام محمد کے ہاں حانث ہونے کیلئے نیت ضروری نہیں۔ اگر مازون غلام مستغرق في القرض نہیں ہے تو امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام ابو یوسف کے قول پر نیت شرط ہے۔ جبکہ امام محمد کے نزدیک نیست شرط نہیں۔
If someone swears, 'I will not ride the animal of so-and-so (a specific person),' but then rides the animal of that person's ma'dhun (authorized) slave, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa he does not become hanith, regardless of whether the authorized slave is in debt or not. Because the authorized slave is still a slave of the mahloof alayh, not free, but he is authorized. Therefore, in both cases (in debt or not), the slave's animal is in his own ownership, while the swearer swore not to ride the mahloof alayh's animal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the swearer's riding the animal of a ma'dhun slave does not make him hanith, whether the slave is in debt or not. However, if the slave is mustaghraq fi al-qard (completely indebted), then riding the ma'dhun slave's animal will make the swearer hanith, because the attribution based on the master's ownership remains. Thus in that case, hanth depends on the swearer's intention. Imam Abu Yusuf says that the swearer becomes hanith in all cases with the condition that he intended to ride the ma'dhun slave's animal, because his attribution to the master is deficient. Imam Muhammad's statement is that hanth is necessitated in all cases for the swearer; intention is not necessary, because in this case the master's real ownership is considered. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree that in the case of a slave who is both ma'dhun and mustaghraq fi al-qard, they do not consider these two (ma'dhun and mustaghraq) as preventing factors regarding the master's ownership, while Imam Abu Hanifa differs from the Sahibayn regarding the mustaghraq slave. According to Imam Muhammad, intention is not necessary for hanth. If the ma'dhun slave is not mustaghraq fi al-qard, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, intention is a condition (for hanth), while according to Imam Muhammad, it is not a condition.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on an oath: swearing not to ride the animal of a specific person (mahloof alayh). The complication arises when the animal belongs to that person's ma'dhun (authorized) slave. The three imams differ: Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the slave's debt status; if the slave is not mustaghraq (indebted beyond assets), the animal is owned by the slave, so riding it does not break the oath. If the slave is mustaghraq, ownership reverts to the master, so intention matters. Abu Yusuf requires intention in all cases. Muhammad does not require intention, as ownership is real. The text culminates by stating the relied-upon distinctions, showing the school's nuanced approach to property attribution in oaths.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhūn): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
مستغرق فی القرض (mustaghraq fī al-qarḍ): A ma'dhun slave whose debts exceed the value of his assets, causing his property to be considered owned by the master.
حنث (ḥanth): Violation of the oath; here, determining when the swearer is liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, when does a person become hanith after riding a ma'dhun slave's animal?
A) Only if the slave is mustaghraq.
B) In all cases, regardless of intention.
C) In all cases, but only if he intended to ride the slave's animal.
D) Only if he intended to ride the slave's animal and the slave is not mustaghraq.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on oath violations with ma'dhun slaves centers on ownership attribution and intention; the slave's debt status and the swearer's intent are decisive factors.

Oath on Eating from a Palm Tree: Interpretation and Effect of Change
Ruling on oath interpretation and change of state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of metaphorical interpretation for oaths on inedible objects and determine when a change in state or processing affects the obligation of the oath.

Core Idea
An oath on an inedible thing is interpreted metaphorically as its fruit, but a change through new processing or natural maturity removes the binding effect.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted according to custom and the intent of the swearer. When the literal subject of an oath is not edible, the oath is understood metaphorically to refer to what is normally consumed from that source. However, this interpretation is conditioned on the object remaining in its original state. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Quduri's matn on swearing by a palm tree, along with the crucial condition that the fruit not be transformed by new processing or natural change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath’s literal subject
Read the oath wording carefully. Here, 'I will not eat from this palm tree' – the literal subject is the tree itself, which is inedible. This triggers the Hanafi rule of metaphorical interpretation.
If the subject were edible, the oath would apply literally.
2
Apply the metaphorical interpretation (majaz)
Because the tree is not eaten, the oath is diverted to its fruit (thamar), which is the intended object. The tree is a cause (sabab) for the fruit, and using 'cause' to mean 'effect' is a valid metaphor.
This is based on custom (urf) and the impossibility of literal meaning.
3
Check the condition of no new processing
The fruit must not have been altered by a new manufacturing process (sin'a jadida). If it is turned into nabidh, vinegar, or cooked dibs, the oath is not broken because the subject of the oath has changed.
This condition applies specifically to processing, not to natural change.
4
Distinguish natural change from processing
Natural changes in the fruit (e.g., unripe to ripe, fresh to dry) are considered changes in description, not in essence. Since the description (e.g., being unripe) motivated the oath, the oath is restricted to that state.
Similarly, milk changes to cheese are considered new processing, while milk turning sour naturally? The text focuses on processing.
5
Apply the analogy to speaking to a child
The passage contrasts the oath on fruit with an oath about speaking to a child. If one swears not to speak to a child and the child grows up, the oath is broken because the description of 'child' is not considered a motive for the oath – the prohibition of boycotting a Muslim overrides.
This shows that motivation matters: the specific quality must be the reason for the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْيَمِينِ فِي الَّا كُل وَالشَّرْبِ
ترجمہ باب کھانے اور پینے کے بارے میں قسم کھانا
قسم کھائی کہ اس کھجور سے نہیں کھاؤں گا اس سے کھجور کا پھل مراد ہے
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هذِهِ النَّخْلَةِ فَهُوَ عَلَى ثَمَرِهَا لِأَنَّهُ أَضَافَ الْيَمِيْنَ إِلَى مَا لَا يُؤْكَلُ فَيَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى مَا يَخْرُجُ مِنْهُ وَهُوَ الثَّمَرُ لِأَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ لَهُ فَيَصْلُحُ مَجَازَاعَنْهُ لَكِنَّ الشَّرْطَ اَنْ لَّا يَتَغَيَّرُ بِصُنْعَةٍ جَدِيدَةٍ حَتَّى لَا يَحْنَتْ بِالنَّبِيدِ وَالْخَلِ وَالدَّبْسِ الْمَطْبُوحَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُؤْكَلُ مِنْ هَذَا الْمُسْرِ فَصَارَ رَطْبًا فَأَكَلَهُ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَكَذَا إِذَا خَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ مِنْ هذَا الرَّطْبِ أَوْ مِنْ هَذَا اللَّيْنِ فَصَارَتَمْرًا اَوْصَارَ اللَّبَنُ شِيْرَارًا لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ صِفَةَ الْمُسِّورَةِ وَالرُّطُوبَةِ دَاعِيَةٌ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ وَكَذَا كَوْنُهُ لَبَنًا فَيَتَقَيَّدُبِهِ وَلَانَّ اللَّبَنَ مَا كُولٌ فَلَا يَنْصَرِقُ الْيَمِينُ إِلَى مَا يُتَّخَذُ مِنْهُ ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اس درخت سے نہیں کھاؤں گا۔تو یہ تم اس کے پھلوں سے متعلق ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اس نے قسم اس چیز کی طرف منسوب کی ہے جو خود نہیں کھائی جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے مجاز اس قسم کا تعلق ایسی چیزوں سے ہو جائے گا جو اس درخت سے کھانے کے قابل پیدا ہو اور وہ اس کا پھل ہے کیونکہ درخت ہی ان پھلوں کے پیدا ہونے اور بڑھنے کا سبب ہوتا ہے۔ اس کے مجاز اسبب بول کر مسبب مراد لینا جائز ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن پھل سے حانث ہونے میں شرط یہ ہے کہ اسے کسی نئی ترکیب سے اصل حالت میں بدلا نہ لیا ہو۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر فرمایا انگور کے پھل سے نبیذ یاسر کہ یا پکائی ہوئی تاڑی یا کچے کھٹے انگور کھائے تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر قسم کھائی کہ میں اس درخت کے گدر (ادھ پکے ) پھل نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر رطب ( پورا پکا ہوا) سے کھایا تو حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ اسی طرح اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اس رطب یعنی تازہ پکے ہوئے چھوہارہ سے یا اس دودھ سے نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر اس کے شمر یعنی چھوہارہ خشک ہونے کے بعد یا دودھ کا شیرازہ ہونے کے بعد کھایا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ گدر ہونایا پورا پختہ ہونا ایسی صفت ہے جو کبھی قسم پر واقعتا آمادہ کرتی ہے اور دودھ کا بھی یہی حال ہے۔ لہذا قسم اسی صفت تک باقی رہے گی کیونکہ دودھ خود کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے قسم میں اس سے پھیر کر اسی چیز شامل نہیں کی جائے گی جو دودھ سے بنائی جاتی ہے۔ تشریح حاصل کلام یہ کہ اگر کسی چیز کے نہ کھانے کی قسم کھائی مثلاً کھجور یا دودھ تو جس صفت پر یہ چیزیں ہیں اس وقت تک قسم محدود ہوگی جب ان کی صفت میں تبدیلی آئے گی۔ بایں طور کہ کھجور خشک ہوگئی یا دودھ سے ملائی یا برفی وغیرہ بنائی تو اب حالف کھانے سے حانث نہ ہوگا۔ قسم کھائی کہ اس بچے سے یا جو ان سے کلام نہیں کرے گا پھر بوڑھا ہونے کے بعد کلام کیا حانث ہو جائے گا
بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَتَكَلَّمُ هَذَا الصَّبِيَّ اَوْهَذَا الشَّابُ فَكَلَّمَهُ بَعْدَ مَا شَاحَ لِأَنَّ هِجْرَانَ الْمُسْلِمَ بِمَنْعِ الْكَلَامِ مَنْهى عَنْهُ فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ الدَّاعِي دَاعِيَا فِي الشَّرْعِ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ لَحْمَ هَذَا الْحَمَلِ فَاكَلَ بَعْدَ مَا صَارَ كَبْشًا حَيْثُ لَانَّ صِفَةَ الصِّغَرِ فِى هَذَا لَيْسَتْ بدَاعِيَةِ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ فَإِنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَ عَنْهُ اَكْثَرُ امْتِنَا عَاعَنْ
Chapter on oaths concerning eating and drinking. Translation: Chapter on swearing about eating and drinking. He swore that he will not eat from this date palm, meaning its fruit. He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever swears, 'I will not eat from this palm tree,' it applies to its fruit, because he attributed the oath to something that is not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it, which is the fruit, because it is a cause for it, so it is valid as a metaphor for it. But the condition is that it does not change through new processing, so that he does not become hanith by nabidh, vinegar, or cooked dibs. And if he swears 'I will not eat from this unripe date' and it becomes ripe and he eats it, he does not become hanith. Likewise, if he swears 'I will not eat from this ripe date' or 'from this milk' and it becomes dry dates or the milk becomes cheese, he does not become hanith, because the description of being unripe or ripe is a motive for the oath, and likewise its being milk, so the oath is restricted to it. And because milk is an edible substance, so the oath does not turn to what is made from it. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If someone swears, 'I will not eat from this tree,' it pertains to its fruit, because he attributed the oath to something that is not itself eaten, so metaphorically the oath relates to the edible produce of the tree, which is its fruit, because the tree is the cause of the fruit. It is permissible to use 'cause' metaphorically to mean 'effect'. But for hanth from the fruit, the condition is that it is not changed by new processing. Thus, if the fruit is made into nabidh, vinegar, or cooked dibs, he does not become hanith. And if he swears, 'I will not eat the unripe fruit of this tree,' then eats the ripe fruit, he does not become hanith. Similarly, if he swears, 'I will not eat this ripe date or this milk,' then eats it after it becomes dried dates or the milk becomes thickened, he does not become hanith, because the quality of being unripe or ripe is what motivates the oath, and the same for milk. So the oath remains limited to that quality, because milk itself is eaten, so the oath does not extend to what is made from it. Explanation: The gist is that if one swears not to eat something, e.g., dates or milk, the oath is limited to the state of that item until its quality changes. For example, if the dates become dry or the milk becomes cream or butter, then the swearer eating it does not break the oath. If he swears not to speak to this child or youth, then after he becomes old, he speaks, he becomes hanith. Unlike if he swears not to speak to this child or youth, then after he becomes old, he speaks, because boycotting a Muslim by preventing speech is prohibited, so the motive is not considered a valid motive in law. And if he swears not to eat the meat of this lamb, then eats it after it becomes a ram, because the quality of smallness is not a motive for the oath, as the one who abstains does so more?? (incomplete).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on oaths concerning eating and drinking presents the ruling from al-Quduri's matn. The key principle: if someone swears not to eat from a palm tree, the oath is interpreted as referring to the fruit, because the tree itself is not eaten. However, the fruit must be in its original, unprocessed state. If it undergoes new processing (like making nabidh or vinegar), the oath is not broken. Similarly, if the fruit changes from unripe to ripe or from fresh to dried, the oath is not broken because the description (e.g., being unripe) is the reason for the oath. The same applies to milk: the oath refers to liquid milk, not its derivatives. This illustrates the Hanafi method of interpreting oaths based on custom (urf) and the specific description that motivated the oath. The text also includes an analogous ruling about speaking to a child: if the child grows up, the oath is not broken because the description 'child' remains? Actually, the text says if he swears not to speak to a child and he becomes old, he is hanith. But then it contrasts with a case where the description is not a motive. The Arabic at the end is cut off but the Urdu clarifies: eating the meat of a lamb after it becomes a ram breaks the oath because the smallness is not a motive. This shows the complexity of Hanafi oath interpretation.

Key Terms
نخلة (nakhla): The palm tree itself, not eaten; the oath is interpreted as referring to its fruit.
ثمر (thamar): The fruit of the palm tree, which is the intended object of the oath.
صنعة جديدة (ṣin'a jadīda): New processing that changes the state of the fruit, exempting from hanth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a person swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree' and then drinks nabidh made from its dates, what is the ruling?
A) He is hanith.
B) He is not hanith.
C) He is hanith only if he intended to drink nabidh.
D) The oath is void.

Key Takeaway
An oath on a palm tree is interpreted as its fruit, but a change through new processing or natural maturity exempts from breach.

Oath on a Palm Tree: Scope and Transformation
Ruling on oath scope with transformation condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath not to eat from a palm tree, identify when a transformation of the fruit nullifies the oath's application, and apply the condition of new manufacture.

Core Idea
An oath not to eat from a tree applies only to its fruit, and only if the fruit hasn't been fundamentally transformed.

Oaths can be tricky when the subject is not directly edible. This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi case: swearing not to eat from a palm tree. The oath is understood metaphorically to refer to the fruit, but with a critical condition – if the fruit is processed into something new (like vinegar or syrup), the oath no longer applies. We will explore the reasoning behind this and examine related scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its object
The oath is 'I will not eat from this palm tree.' The object is the tree itself, which is not eaten. The Hanafi jurist reasons that the oath must refer to something that can be eaten, so it turns to the fruit.
This is a metaphorical extension (majaz) based on causality.
2
Isolate the condition restricting the ruling
The author states a condition: 'provided it does not change by a new manufacture (sun'ah jadidah).' This means if the fruit undergoes a fundamental transformation (e.g., into nabidh, vinegar, dibs), the oath no longer applies.
The transformation must be complete; mere ripening is not a new manufacture.
3
Apply to secondary examples
If the oath is on unripe dates (busr), it does not apply once they become ripe (rutab). Similarly, an oath on milk does not extend to milk products. The quality that motivated the oath is considered.
The motive (da'iyah) restricts the oath to the specific state.
4
Derive the underlying principle
The principle is: when an oath is attached to something not directly consumed (like a tree), it is interpreted to refer to its produce, but only in its original, unprocessed form. The transformation cuts the metaphorical link.
This reflects the Hanafi approach to interpreting oaths based on custom and intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْيَمِينِ فِي الَّا كُل وَالشَّرْبِ قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هذِهِ النَّخْلَةِ فَهُوَ عَلَى ثَمَرِهَا لِأَنَّهُ أَضَافَ الْيَمِيْنَ إِلَى مَا لَا يُؤْكَلُ فَيَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى مَا يَخْرُجُ مِنْهُ وَهُوَ الثَّمَرُ لِأَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ لَهُ فَيَصْلُحُ مَجَازَاعَنْهُ لَكِنَّ الشَّرْطَ اَنْ لَّا يَتَغَيَّرُ بِصُنْعَةٍ جَدِيدَةٍ حَتَّى لَا يَحْنَتْ بِالنَّبِيدِ وَالْخَلِ وَالدَّبْسِ الْمَطْبُوحَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُؤْكَلُ مِنْ هَذَا الْمُسْرِ فَصَارَ رَطْبًا فَأَكَلَهُ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَكَذَا إِذَا خَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ مِنْ هذَا الرَّطْبِ أَوْ مِنْ هَذَا اللَّيْنِ فَصَارَتَمْرًا اَوْصَارَ اللَّبَنُ شِيْرَارًا لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّ صِفَةَ الْمُسِّورَةِ وَالرُّطُوبَةِ دَاعِيَةٌ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ وَكَذَا كَوْنُهُ لَبَنًا فَيَتَقَيَّدُبِهِ وَلَانَّ اللَّبَنَ مَا كُولٌ فَلَا يَنْصَرِقُ الْيَمِينُ إِلَى مَا يُتَّخَذُ مِنْهُ
Chapter of oath regarding eating and drinking. He said: Whoever swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree', it applies to its fruit, because he added the oath to something not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it, which is the fruit, because it is a cause for it, so it is valid metaphorically. But the condition is that it does not change by a new manufacture, such that he does not become sinful with nabidh, vinegar, or cooked dibs. If he swears not to eat from this unripe date, then it becomes ripe and he eats it, he does not become sinful. Likewise if he swears not to eat from this ripe date or this milk, then it becomes dried dates or the milk becomes [syrup], he does not become sinful, because the quality of being unripe and ripe is a motive for the oath, and likewise the milk, so the oath is restricted to it, and because milk is eaten, so the oath does not turn to what is made from it.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on oaths regarding eating and drinking establishes a key principle: when an oath is attached to something that is not directly eaten (like a tree), it metaphorically refers to what is produced from it (its fruit). The proof lies in the linguistic cause: the term 'from this tree' implies consumption of what it yields. However, an important condition is that the fruit must not undergo a complete transformation into a different substance through a new process (such as making nabidh, vinegar, or cooked date syrup). If the oath is specifically on unripe (busr) dates, it does not apply once they become ripe (rutab), because the quality that motivated the oath has changed. Similarly, an oath on milk does not extend to products made from milk (like cheese or syrup), since milk itself is edible and the oath is narrowly interpreted based on the specific item named.

Key Terms
الْيَمِين (al-yamin): An oath affirming or negating an action with the name of Allah or an equivalent.
ثَمَر (thamar): The fruit of a tree; in oaths, the intended meaning when swearing by the tree.
صُنْعَةٍ جَدِيدَةٍ (sun'ah jadidah): A new manufacturing process that changes the original substance entirely.
دَاعِيَة (da'iyah): Motive or reason prompting the oath; in Hanafi fiqh, it can restrict the scope of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from this palm tree, then eats cooked date syrup (dibs made from its fruit), does he become sinful according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Yes, because the syrup still comes from the tree.
B) No, because syrup is a new manufactured product.
C) Yes, because he swore not to eat from the tree, and the syrup is from its fruit.
D) No, because the tree no longer produces fruit.

Key Takeaway
An oath on a non-edible item metaphorically refers to its produce, but if the produce is fundamentally transformed by a new process, the oath no longer applies.

Oath Not to Speak to a Child or Eat Lamb Meat: Effect of Aging
Rulings on oaths regarding speaking to a child and eating lamb meat after growth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath regarding a person or animal remains binding despite changes in age, distinguishing when the motive (da'i) is considered and when it is not.

Core Idea
An oath is not voided by aging of the subject unless the motive is recognized in Shariah.

This lesson examines two contrasting cases: swearing not to speak to a child, and swearing not to eat a lamb's meat. In both, the person later changes—the child grows old, the lamb becomes a ram. Does the oath still bind? The Hanafi answer is yes, and the reasoning reveals a key principle: the motive for the oath (da'i) is only considered if it is a valid Shariah concern. Boycotting a Muslim is prohibited, so the motive of avoiding a child does not justify breaking the oath. Similarly, the smallness of a lamb is not a valid motive, as one who avoids meat would avoid it more when the animal is larger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and the change
Read the passage and note the two oaths: 'I will not speak to this child/youth' and 'I will not eat the meat of this lamb.' The change is the person aging and the lamb becoming a ram.
The change is in age/size, not in the essence of the subject.
2
Extract the ruling for each case
The passage says that speaking to the aged person still violates the oath, and eating the ram's meat also violates the oath. So the oath remains binding.
The ruling is that the oath applies to the substance, not the temporary quality.
3
Identify the rationale (ta'lil) for the first case
The reason given: 'because boycotting a Muslim by refusing speech is prohibited, so the motive is not considered a motive in the law.'
The prohibition of hajr al-muslim (boycott beyond three days) overrides the consideration of the child's age as a motive.
4
Identify the rationale for the second case
The quality of smallness (being a lamb) is not a motive for the oath because one who refrains from meat would refrain more from the meat of a large sheep.
The da'i (motivating factor) is not the size but the meat itself.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider an oath not to drink milk from a specific cow. If the cow grows older, does the oath still apply? According to the same principle, yes, because the motive is the milk, not the cow's age.
The key is whether the change is in the essence or in a peripheral attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَتَكَلَّمُ هَذَا الصَّبِيَّ اَوْهَذَا الشَّابُ فَكَلَّمَهُ بَعْدَ مَا شَاحَ لِأَنَّ هِجْرَانَ الْمُسْلِمَ بِمَنْعِ الْكَلَامِ مَنْهى عَنْهُ فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ الدَّاعِي دَاعِيَا فِي الشَّرْعِ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ لَحْمَ هَذَا الْحَمَلِ فَاكَلَ بَعْدَ مَا صَارَ كَبْشًا حَيْثُ لَانَّ صِفَةَ الصِّغَرِ فِى هَذَا لَيْسَتْ بدَاعِيَةِ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ فَإِنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَ عَنْهُ اَكْثَرُ امْتِنَا عَاعَنْ لَحْمِ الْكَيش
Contrary to when he swears 'I will not speak to this child or this youth' then speaks to him after he has aged, because boycotting a Muslim by refusing speech is prohibited, so the motive is not considered a motive in the law. And if he swears 'I will not eat the meat of this lamb' then eats after it has become a ram, since the quality of smallness is not a motive for the oath, for the one refraining from it refrains more from the meat of a large sheep.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a contrast to the previous rules about changing states. In the case of an oath not to speak to a child or a young person, if the person grows old, speaking to him still violates the oath. The reason given is that the prohibition against speaking to a Muslim (boycotting) is itself forbidden in Shariah, so the motive (the person's youth) is not considered a valid reason to limit the oath. In other words, the oath is taken seriously, and one cannot claim he only meant the child's younger age. Similarly, for an oath not to eat the meat of a lamb, if the lamb grows into a ram, eating its meat still violates the oath. The rationale is that the quality of being a lamb (smallness) is not the motivating factor; rather, one who refrains from meat would refrain more from older meat (which may be tougher or less preferred). The principle here is: the oath's application depends on the substance (meat) not on temporary qualities of age/size that do not affect edibility. This differs from dates where ripeness changes the nature as an edible item.

Key Terms
هَجْرَانُ الْمُسْلِمِ (hajr al-muslim): The act of boycotting or abandoning a Muslim by not speaking to him.
الدَّاعِي (al-da'i): The motivating factor that prompts one to take the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not eat the meat of this lamb.' The lamb becomes a ram. According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the status of the oath?
A) The oath is nullified because the animal changed.
B) The oath still binds; eating ram meat violates it.
C) The oath is suspended until the animal is a lamb again.
D) The oath applies only if the meat is called 'lamb'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath regarding a specific person or animal remains binding despite changes in age or size, because the motive for the oath is only considered if it is a valid Shariah concern.

Oath on Busr and Rutab: The Mudhannab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath violations with mixed-ripeness dates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether eating a mudhannab date violates an oath against busr or rutab, and explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind each view.

Core Idea
A mixed date (mudhannab) violates an oath against either type for Abu Hanifa but not for the Sahibayn, because naming follows the majority.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about oaths on specific types of dates. The basic rule is clear: a busr is not a rutab, and vice versa. The complication arises when a date contains both: a mudhannab. Should a person who swore not to eat busr be considered sinful if he eats a date that is mostly rutab but has a busr tail? The two leading students of Abu Hanifa (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree with their teacher, and the debate hinges on whether the naming of the date or the actual consumption of the part matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and dispute context
First, note the undisputed rule: an oath against busr is not violated by eating pure rutab, and vice versa. The dispute arises when the date is mixed (mudhannab).
The mudhannab is a single date with two parts: one busr, one rutab.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that eating mudhannab violates the oath regardless of which type is predominant, because the eater consumes both parts. The act of eating targets each part individually.
His dalil: the mudhannab contains both; each is intended in eating.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn hold that the oath is only violated if the named type is the predominant part. They reason that the date is named by its majority, and the oath applies to the named fruit, not its constituents.
They analogize to purchase: if you buy a mixed item, the whole follows the majority.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response distinguishing oath from purchase
Abu Hanifa responds that purchase deals with the whole (the entire batch follows the majority), whereas eating is an act that consumes each part. In an oath, the intention is to avoid a specific substance, not a label.
Thus, the analogy with purchase fails because the nature of the act differs.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, a person swearing not to eat busr must avoid any date with even a trace of busr. Under the Sahibayn, only dates predominantly busr are prohibited.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon view) is not explicit here, but caution suggests Abu Hanifa's view to avoid sin.

Source Text

قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ بُسْرًا فَأَكَلَ رَطْبًا لَمْ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِبُسْرٍ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ رُطَبًا أَوْ يُسْرًا أَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ رُطَبًا وَلَابُسْرًا فَأَكَلَ مُذَنَّبًا حَيْثُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا يَحْنَتُ فِي الرُّطَبِ يَعْنِي بِالْبُسْرِ الْمُذَنَّبِ وَلَا فِي الْمُسْرِ بِالرُّطَبِ الْمُذَنَّبِ لَاتَ الرُّطَبَ الْمُذَنَّبَ يُسَمَّى رُطَبًا وَالْمُسْرُ الْمُذَنَّبُ يُسَمَّى بُسْرًا فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْيَمِيْنُ عَلَى الشَّرَاءِ وَلَهُ اَنَّ الرُّطَبَ الْمُذَنَّبَ مَا يَكُونُ فِي ذَنْبِهِ قَلِيلٌ بُسْرِ وَالْبُسْرُ الْمُذَنَّبُ عَلَى عَكْسِهِ فَيَكُونُ اكلة اكِلَ الْمُسْرِوَ الرُّطَبِ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدِ مَقْصُودٌ فِى الاَ كُلِ بِخِلَافِ الشَّرَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ يُصَادِقُ الْجُمْلَةَ فَيَتَّبِعُ الْقَلِيلُ فِيهِ الْكَثِيرَ
He said: Whoever swears 'I will not eat unripe dates (busr)' and then eats ripe dates (rutab), he does not become sinful (hanith) because it is not busr. And whoever swears 'I will not eat ripe dates' or 'unripe dates' or swears 'I will not eat ripe dates and unripe dates' and then eats mudhannab (a date that has a part ripe and part unripe) — according to Abu Hanifa he becomes sinful, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not become sinful in [eating] ripe dates with the unripe tail [mudhannab] and not in unripe dates with the ripe tail, because the ripe mudhannab is called ripe dates and the unripe mudhannab is called unripe dates. Thus it becomes like when the oath is made upon a purchase (shira')... And for him (Abu Hanifa), the ripe mudhannab is that which has a little unripe part in its tail, and the unripe mudhannab is the opposite, so he would be eating both unripe and ripe dates, and each is intended in eating, unlike purchase because it encompasses the whole and the little follows the much.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths on specific types of dates. The basic rule is clear: an oath not to eat unripe dates (busr) does not apply to fully ripe dates (rutab), and vice versa, because they are distinct types. The dispute arises over mudhannab dates, which have a tail of one type while the rest is the other (e.g., a rutab with a busr tip). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that eating such a mixed date violates an oath sworn against either busr or rutab (or both), because the eater consumes both substances. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) argue that the date is named according to its predominant part: if the tail is busr but the rest is rutab, it is called rutab, so an oath on rutab is violated but not on busr. They analogize to a purchase: if one buys a mixed item, the whole is ruled by the majority. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: in eating, one deliberately consumes both parts, so each part matters. The practical difference: in an oath, Abu Hanifa's position (mufta bihi?) is stricter: a person swearing not to eat certain dates should avoid mudhannab entirely.

Key Terms
مُذَنَّب (mudhannab): A date that has a tail of one type (busr or rutab) while the rest is the other type.
بُسْر (busr): Unripe dates, still hard and not sweet.
رُطَب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates that are soft and sweet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a person swears not to eat busr and eats a mudhannab date that has a busr tail but is predominantly rutab, does he become sinful?
A) Yes, because he ate busr.
B) No, because the date is called rutab.
C) Yes, because the tail is busr.
D) No, because he did not intend.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether mixed-ripeness dates (mudhannab) violate oaths against a specific type; Abu Hanifa focuses on the act of eating, the Sahibayn on naming conventions.

Oath on Eating Fresh Dates: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute over Rutf Muthannab
Intra-Hanafi dispute on definition of fresh dates in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether eating rutf muthannab (a partially dried fresh date) violates an oath not to eat fresh dates (rutf), and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, any moisture qualifies a date as fresh; for the Sahibayn, only fully ripe dates count.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah unpacks a classic intra-Hanafi difference on the precise scope of an oath when a person swears not to eat fresh dates (rutf). Does eating a date that has begun to dry at the tail (rutf muthannab) constitute a violation? The answer depends on how the Hanafi imams define the term 'rutf' in the context of oaths. We will explore the arguments of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and understand why this difference matters for the obligation of expiation.

Guided Path
1
State the contested issue
Identify the precise legal question: What does 'rutf' mean in an oath? Is it limited to fully ripe fresh dates, or does it include dates that have begun to dry but retain some moisture?
The answer determines whether the oath is broken.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa holds that eating rutf muthannab violates the oath; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it does not.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Examine each side's dalil (evidence)
Abu Hanifa argues that rutf muthannab retains a little moisture at the tail, so it is still called rutf. The Sahibayn argue that customary usage applies 'rutf' only to fully ripe dates, not partially dried ones.
The dispute is over linguistic vs. customary definition.
4
Connect to the broader principle
The passage contrasts oaths about eating with oaths about buying: in buying, the majority of the bunch determines the ruling, but in eating, each individual item is considered independently.
This distinction is key to understanding the Sahibayn's argument against qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if one eats rutf muthannab after swearing not to eat rutf, Abu Hanifa requires kaffarah (expiation), while the Sahibayn do not.
This affects whether kaffarah is wajib or not.

Chapter: Oath about eating and drinking. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume 6. He will become a breaker of the oath [hanath] from eating. But the two companions [Sahibayn] have said that in the case of fresh dates (rutf) he will not become a breaker. When he has sworn that I will not eat fresh dates. And he will also not become a breaker from eating fresh dates that have a tail (rutf muthannab) when he has sworn that I will not eat dry dates (busr). Because rutf muthannab is called fresh dates, and busr muthannab is called dry dates. So the oath about eating becomes like an oath about buying, whose discussion will come in the next issue. And the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that rutf muthannab is that which has a little passage at the tail side, and muthannab is its opposite. Therefore, eating it became included, and eating each one is intended, so he becomes a breaker. Unlike buying, because buying is of the whole bunch, so whatever part of unripe or ripe is less in that bunch becomes subordinate to the majority, so it will not be analogized to buying.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah begins the chapter on oaths related to eating and drinking. It immediately presents a ruling from the matn: eating breaks the oath. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) qualify this: if the oath is specifically against eating fresh dates (rutf), then eating rutf muthannab (a fresh date beginning to dry) does not violate the oath. Similarly, if the oath is against eating dry dates (busr), eating busr muthannab does not violate. Their reasoning is that the term 'rutf' is typically applied to fully ripe fresh dates, not to those that have begun to dry. Abu Hanifa, on the other hand, holds that rutf muthannab is still a fresh date because it retains some moisture at the tail. The passage notes that this distinction parallels the difference between eating and buying: in buying, minor components are subordinate to the whole, while in eating, each item is independently considered. The author thus sets up the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
رطب (rutf): Fresh, fully ripe dates
رطب مذنب (rutf muthannab): Dates that have begun to dry at the tail but are still considered fresh by some
صاحبین (Sahibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because rutf muthannab is still considered rutf.
B) No, because rutf muthannab is a different type of date.
C) Yes, because the oath includes all types of dates.
D) No, because the oath only applies to fully ripe dates.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, intra-madhhab disputes often hinge on the precise definition of terms in the context of oaths, and the mufti must carefully consider both linguistic and customary usage.

Oath on Buying Mixed Dates: The Principle of Subordination
Ruling on oath for buying mixed dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when an oath concerns purchase, the majority determines non-breach, but when the oath concerns eating, each item is considered individually.

Core Idea
In oaths, purchase applies to the whole (the majority rules), but eating applies item by item (each component matters).

This passage clarifies a nuance in oaths: the same action—buying a mixed product—has different legal effects depending on the type of oath. It uses the principle of subordination for purchases and individual intention for consumption, supported by a clear analogy to barley and wheat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oaths
Note that the passage considers two distinct oaths: one regarding purchase (not to buy fresh dates) and one regarding eating (not to eat fresh dates).
2
Recognize the difference in rulings
The purchase of a mixed bunch does not break the oath, but eating the few fresh dates from the bunch does break the oath.
3
Understand the illa (effective cause)
For purchase, the act applies to the whole; the minority is subordinate to the majority. For eating, the act is done item by item; each piece is independently intended.
4
Apply the analogy to barley/wheat
The same logic holds: buying wheat with a few barley grains does not break an oath not to buy barley, but eating those barley grains does break an oath not to eat barley.
5
Summarize the principle
The ruling hinges on the nature of the act: purchase is a single transaction covering the whole, while eating is a series of individual consumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم کھائی کہ رطب نہیں خریدوں گا پھر بسر کا خوشہ خرید لیا جس میں رطب بھی تھیں حانث نہیں ہوگا
وَ لَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَشْتَرِى رَطْبًا فَاشْتَرَى كِبَاسَةَ مُسْرِفِيهَا رُطَبٌ لَا يَحْنَتُ لأنَّ الشَّرَاءَ يُصَادِقُ الْجُمْلَةَ وَالْمَعْلُوبُ تَابِعٌ وَلَوْ كَانَتِ الْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْأَكْلِ يَحْنَتُ لِأَنَّ الْأَكْلَ يُصَادِقُهُ شَيْئًا فَشَيْئًا فَكَانَ كُلُّ مِنْهُمَا مَقْصُودًا وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَشْتَرِى شَعِيْرًا أَوْلَايَا كُلُهُ فَاشْتَرى حِنْطَةٌ فِيهَا حَبَّاتُ شَعِيرِ وَ أَكَلَهَا يَحْنَتْ فِي الْأَكْلِ دُونَ
الشَّرَاءِ لِمَا قُلْنَا
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ رطب نہیں خریدوں گا پھر اس نے بسر یعنی گدر چھواروں کا خوشہ خرید لیا جس میں رطب یعنی پختہ بھی تھوڑے ہیں تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خریداری تو پورے خوشہ کی ہوتی ہے۔ اور یہاں قلیل کثیر کے تابع ہو جائیں گے۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے پر ہوتی یعنی رطب نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اگر گدر خوشہ کے اندر سے رطب کو بھی کھا جاتا ہے تو حانث ہو جاتا۔ کیونکہ کھانا تو ایک کے بعد ایک کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے کھانے میں رطب اور بسر ہر ایک مقصود ہوا ۔ یعنی اس میں سے کوئی تابع نہ ہوگا۔ اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قسم کھائی کہ میں شعیر یعنی جو نہیں خریدوں گا یا یہ قسم کھائی کہ جو نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر گیہوں خریدے۔ جن میں جو کے کچھ دانے بھی ملے ہوئے ہیں۔ اور جو کے ساتھ گیہوں کو کھا لیا تو کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن تفرید نے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اوپر کی بتائی ہوئی دلیل کی وجہ سے۔
فائدہ یعنی خریداری تو سب کی ایک ساتھ ہوتی ہے اس لئے جب گیہوں زیادہ ہیں تو انہیں کی خریداری ہوگی اور جو کا خرید نانہیں کیا جائے گا۔ لیکن کھانے میں ہر طرح کا دانہ مقصود ہوتا ہے اس لئے گیہوں کے ساتھ جو کا کھانا بھی مقصود ہوا۔ اس لئے حانث ہو جائے گا۔
If he swore that he will not buy fresh dates (rutf), then he bought a bunch of dry dates (busr) in which there were some fresh dates, he does not become a breaker. And if he swore not to buy fresh dates, and he bought a bunch whose ripe dates are fresh, he does not break, because buying applies to the whole, and the submerged is subordinate. And if the oath was concerning eating, he breaks, because eating applies to it item by item, so each one is intended. And it becomes like when he swore not to buy barley or not to eat it, then he bought wheat in which there are grains of barley and ate them: he breaks in eating but not in buying, for what we said. Translation: And if he swore that he will not buy fresh dates, then he bought a bunch of dry dates (busr) in which there are a few fresh dates (rutf), he does not break. Because buying is of the whole bunch, and here the small becomes subordinate to the large. And if the oath was concerning eating, i.e., I will not eat fresh dates, then if he also eats the fresh dates from inside the dry bunch, he breaks, because eating is done one after another, so in eating each of fresh and dry is intended, none is subordinate. And this becomes like when he swore not to buy barley or not to eat it, then he bought wheat in which there are some barley grains, and he ate the barley with the wheat: he breaks in eating but not in buying for the reason stated above. Lesson: buying is of all together, so when wheat is more, the purchase is of it, and the barley is not purchased. But in eating, every kind of grain is intended, so eating the barley with the wheat is also intended, so he breaks.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses a situation where a person swears not to buy fresh dates (rutf) but then buys a bunch of dry dates (busr) that contains a few fresh dates. The ruling is that he does not break the oath (la yahnuth) because the purchase is of the entire bunch; the small amount of fresh dates is subordinate to the majority. However, if the oath were about eating (not eating rutf), then eating the few fresh dates from the bunch would break the oath because eating is done item by item, and each piece is independently intended. The proof is provided by analogy to an oath regarding barley: if one swears not to buy barley but buys wheat with a few barley grains mixed in, the purchase is valid and does not break the oath; but if he eats the barley grains along with the wheat, he breaks the oath about eating. The commentary (fa'ida) emphasizes that in purchases, the majority governs, while in consumption, each component is separately considered.

Key Terms
كباسة (kibasa): A bunch of dates (especially dry dates)
مشرف (musrif): Dates that have begun to ripen; often used in conjunction with fresh dates

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to buy fresh dates (rutf) but buys a bunch of dry dates (busr) that contains some fresh dates, does he break his oath according to this passage?
A) Yes, because he bought fresh dates indirectly.
B) No, because the purchase is of the whole bunch, and the fresh dates are subordinate.
C) Yes, but only if he eats the fresh dates.
D) No, because the oath only applies to buying pure fresh dates.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, the mode of action (purchase vs. consumption) determines whether a mixture is treated as a whole or as individual components.
