Oath on Eating Meat: Defining 'Lahm' in the Hanafi School
Ruling on what constitutes meat in oaths with istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of 'lahm' to determine whether eating fish, pork, human flesh, or offal breaks an oath not to eat meat.

Core Idea
Hanafi defines meat by blood origin, using istihsan to override literal Quranic usage.

Oaths often hinge on precise definitions. The Hanafi school here shows how it balances a clear Quranic text that calls fish 'lahm' with a biological reality – meat originates from blood, and fish have no blood. Through istihsan, they rule that fish does not break a meat oath, while pork and human flesh do because they are real meat. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and extends it to offal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the act
Start with the scenario: a person swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats fish, pork, human flesh, or offal. The question is whether the oath is broken.
2
Examine the qiyas (analogy)
Qiyas would argue that the oath is broken because the Quran (16:14) calls fish meat. The literal meaning of 'lahm' includes fish.
The Quranic reference is Surah Al-Nahl 16:14.
3
Apply the istihsan (juristic preference)
Istihsan overrides qiyas: the Quranic usage is metaphorical. True meat originates from blood, but fish have no blood (due to living in water). Thus, fish is not considered 'real meat' and the oath is not broken.
This is based on biological origin, not common usage.
4
Extend to pork and human flesh
Despite being forbidden, pork and human flesh are real meat (originating from blood). Eating them breaks the oath because the oath can be made to prevent the forbidden.
The ruling treats the substantive nature over permissibility.
5
Distinguish offal (liver, kidney, heart)
Offal like liver and kidney is considered true meat in the Hanafi school because it grows from blood and is used like meat. However, some scholars, including a view in al-Khulasa, consider custom (urf): if it is not called meat in common parlance, the oath is not broken.
This shows a tension between biological definition and custom; the Hanafi position favors biological origin.

Source Text

قسم اٹھائی گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر مچھلی کا گوشت کھالیا حانث نہیں ہوگا
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُلُ لَحْمًا فَاكَلَ لَحْمَ السَّمَكِ لَا يَحْنَتْ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُحْنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى لَحْمًا فِي الْقُرْآنِ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَانِ اَنَّ التَّسْمِيَةَ مَجَازِيَةٌ لِاَنَّ اللَّحْمَ مَنْشَأْهُ مِنَ الدَّمِ وَلَادَمَ فِيْهِ لِسُكُونِهِ فِي الْمَاءِ وَإِنْ أَكَلَ لَحْمَ خِنْزِيرٍ أَوْلَحْمَ إِنْسَانِ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ لَحْمٌ حَقِيقِيٌّ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ حَرَامٌ وَالْيَمِينُ قَدْيُعْقَدُ لِلْمَنْعِ مِنَ الْحَرَامِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَّ كَبِدًا أَوْ كِرْسَالاَنَّهُ لَحْمٌ حَقِيقَةٌ فَإِنْ نُمُوَّهُ مِنَ الدَّمِ وَيُسْتَعْمَلُ اِسْتِعْمَالَ اللَّحْمِ وَقِيلَ فِي عُرْفِنَا لَا يَحْنَتُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعَدُّ لَحْمًا كَذَا فِي الْخُلَاصَةِ
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ رطب نہیں خریدوں گا پھر اس نے بسر یعنی گدر چھواروں کا خوشہ خرید لیا جس میں رطب یعنی پختہ بھی تھوڑے ہیں تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خریداری تو پورے خوشہ کی ہوتی ہے۔ اور یہاں قلیل کثیر کے تابع ہو جائیں گے۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے پر ہوتی یعنی رطب نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اگر گدر خوشہ کے اندر سے رطب کو بھی کھا جاتا ہے تو حانث ہو جاتا۔ کیونکہ کھانا تو ایک کے بعد ایک کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے کھانے میں رطب اور بسر ہر ایک مقصود ہوا ۔ یعنی اس میں سے کوئی تابع نہ ہوگا۔ اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قسم کھائی کہ میں شعیر یعنی جو نہیں خریدوں گا یا یہ قسم کھائی کہ جو نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر گیہوں خریدے۔ جن میں جو کے کچھ دانے بھی ملے ہوئے ہیں۔ اور جو کے ساتھ گیہوں کو کھا لیا تو کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن تفرید نے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اوپر کی بتائی ہوئی دلیل کی وجہ سے۔
فائدہ یعنی خریداری تو سب کی ایک ساتھ ہوتی ہے اس لئے جب گیہوں زیادہ ہیں تو انہیں کی خریداری ہوگی اور جو کا خرید نانہیں کیا جائے گا۔ لیکن کھانے میں ہر طرح کا دانہ مقصود ہوتا ہے اس لئے گیہوں کے ساتھ جو کا کھانا بھی مقصود ہوا۔ اس لئے حانث ہو جائے گا۔
قسم اٹھائی گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر مچھلی کا گوشت کھالیا حانث نہیں ہوگا
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُلُ لَحْمًا فَاكَلَ لَحْمَ السَّمَكِ لَا يَحْنَتْ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُحْنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى لَحْمًا فِي الْقُرْآنِ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَانِ اَنَّ التَّسْمِيَةَ مَجَازِيَةٌ لِاَنَّ اللَّحْمَ مَنْشَأْهُ مِنَ الدَّمِ وَلَادَمَ فِيْهِ لِسُكُونِهِ فِي الْمَاءِ وَإِنْ أَكَلَ لَحْمَ خِنْزِيرٍ أَوْلَحْمَ إِنْسَانِ يَحْنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ لَحْمٌ حَقِيقِيٌّ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ حَرَامٌ وَالْيَمِينُ قَدْيُعْقَدُ لِلْمَنْعِ مِنَ الْحَرَامِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَّ كَبِدًا أَوْ كِرْسَالاَنَّهُ لَحْمٌ حَقِيقَةٌ فَإِنْ نُمُوَّهُ مِنَ الدَّمِ وَيُسْتَعْمَلُ اِسْتِعْمَالَ اللَّحْمِ وَقِيلَ فِي عُرْفِنَا لَا يَحْنَتُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعَدُّ لَحْمًا كَذَا فِي الْخُلَاصَةِ
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ رطب نہیں خریدوں گا پھر اس نے بسر یعنی گدر چھواروں کا خوشہ خرید لیا جس میں رطب یعنی پختہ بھی تھوڑے ہیں تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خریداری تو پورے خوشہ کی ہوتی ہے۔ اور یہاں قلیل کثیر کے تابع ہو جائیں گے۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے پر ہوتی یعنی رطب نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اگر گدر خوشہ کے اندر سے رطب کو بھی کھا جاتا ہے تو حانث ہو جاتا۔ کیونکہ کھانا تو ایک کے بعد ایک کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے کھانے میں رطب اور بسر ہر ایک مقصود ہوا ۔ یعنی اس میں سے کوئی تابع نہ ہوگا۔ اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قسم کھائی کہ میں شعیر یعنی جو نہیں خریدوں گا یا یہ قسم کھائی کہ جو نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر گیہوں خریدے۔ جن میں جو کے کچھ دانے بھی ملے ہوئے ہیں۔ اور جو کے ساتھ گیہوں کو کھا لیا تو کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن تفرید نے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اوپر کی بتائی ہوئی دلیل کی وجہ سے۔
فائدہ یعنی خریداری تو سب کی ایک ساتھ ہوتی ہے اس لئے جب گیہوں زیادہ ہیں تو انہیں کی خریداری ہوگی اور جو کا خرید نانہیں کیا جائے گا۔ لیکن کھانے میں ہر طرح کا دانہ مقصود ہوتا ہے اس لئے گیہوں کے ساتھ جو کا کھانا بھی مقصود ہوا۔ اس لئے حانث ہو جائے گا۔
He took an oath: 'I will not eat meat,' then he ate fish meat; he does not break the oath. He (Imam Muhammad) said: And if he swore not to eat meat, then he ate fish meat, he does not break. Analogy would dictate that he breaks, because it is called meat in the Quran. The face of istihsan is that the naming is metaphorical, because meat originates from blood, and there is no blood in it due to it living in water. And if he ate pork or human flesh, he breaks, because it is real meat, except that it is forbidden, and the oath is sometimes made to prevent the forbidden. Similarly, if he ate liver or kidney, because it is real meat; indeed, its growth is from blood, and it is used like meat. And it was said in our custom he does not break because it is not considered meat; thus in al-Khulasa. Translation: And if he swore that he will not eat fresh dates... (The translation of the previous case is repeated due to OCR).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This section deals with the definition of 'meat' (lahm) in the context of oaths. The matn states that if someone swears not to eat meat and then eats fish meat, he does not break the oath. Qiyas (analogy) would suggest that he does, because fish is called 'lahm' in the Quran (Surah Al-Nahl 16:14). However, istihsan (juristic preference) rules otherwise because the Quranic usage is metaphorical. The reason given is that true meat originates from blood, and fish have no blood (due to living in water). The passage then extends the ruling: if one eats pork or human flesh, he does break the oath because these are real meat, albeit forbidden. Similarly, eating liver or kidney (kabid or kirs) breaks the oath because they are essentially meat, though some scholars differ based on custom (urf). The commentary sides with the view that in custom, offal is not considered meat, so one does not break the oath. The author then discusses further sub-issues: raw meat, head/fax, heart, fat, etc., noting differences with Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): Meat; true meat from animals, defined by blood origin in Hanafi fiqh.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to override qiyas in certain cases.
عرف (urf): Custom; used to determine common understanding of terms in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does eating fish break an oath not to eat meat (lahm)?
A) Yes, because the Quran calls fish meat.
B) No, because fish meat is metaphorical and fish have no blood.
C) Yes, because all animal flesh is meat.
D) No, because fish are not considered animals.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of meat (lahm) in oaths is based on blood origin, overriding Quranic literal usage and custom, with istihsan as the key tool.

Oath on Meat: Raw Meat, Parts, and Intention
Collection of rulings on oaths about meat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on oaths concerning raw meat, head/feet, heart, fat, and different animal species, and identify when a person becomes hanith (oath-breaker).

Core Idea
In oaths about meat, raw meat, head, feet, heart, back fat, and intended fat break the oath; cow and buffalo are distinct species.

This lesson covers a series of specific rulings (masā'il) on oaths related to meat. You will learn which parts of an animal and which types of meat cause an oath to be broken according to the Hanafi school, and where other schools differ. The lesson also clarifies the role of intention and the distinction between similar species.

Guided Path
1
List the categories of meat and parts
Identify each item discussed: raw meat, head and feet, heart, fat (with intention), back fat, goat/sheep, cow/buffalo.
These are the specific cases addressed in the text.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each category
For each case, determine whether the person becomes hanith (oath-breaker) or not according to the Hanafi position.
Raw meat has two opinions; the relied-upon view is that it breaks the oath.
3
Note the cross-madhhab differences
Highlight where Imam Shafi'i or Imam Malik agree or disagree with the Hanafi ruling.
E.g., heart breaks the oath for Hanafis but not for Shafi'is.
4
Apply the intention rule for fat
Understand that if the person intended to include fat when swearing, then fat breaks the oath; otherwise it does not.
Back fat is always treated as meat, regardless of intention.
5
Distinguish between animal species
Learn that goat and sheep are considered the same (so eating one breaks the oath for the other), but cow and buffalo are distinct (so eating buffalo does not break an oath on cow meat).
This is the mufta bihi (fatwa) position for goat/sheep.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل
اور اگر کچا گوشت کھایا تو ایک قول میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔ امام شافعی واحمد رحمتہ اللہ علیہما کا قول بھی یہی ہے۔ اور دوسرے قول میں حانث نہیں ہو گا اور امام مالک کا یہی قول ہے۔ اور اگر سری و پائے کھائے تو حانث ہوگا۔ امام شافعی کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔ اور اگر دل کھایا تو ہمارے نزدیک حانث ہوگا۔ لیکن امام شافعی کے نزدیک حانث نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر اس نے گوشت نہ کھانے کی قسم میں چربی اور چکسی کی بھی نیت کی تو حانث ہو گا ورنہ نہیں۔ امام شافعی واحد کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔ اور اگر پیٹھ کی چربی کھائی تو وہ بھی گوشت کے حکم میں ہے۔ یعنی حانث ہوگا۔ امام شافعی کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔
فائدہ .... اور اگر تم کھائی کہ بکری کا گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر بھیڑ یا دنبہ کا گوشت کھالیا تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اس پر فتویٰ ہے اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کے گائے کا گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر بھینس کا گوشت کھا لیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔
Issues: If he eats raw meat, in one opinion he becomes hanith. This is also the opinion of Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad. In another opinion he does not become hanith, and this is the opinion of Imam Malik. If he eats head and feet, he becomes hanith. Imam Shafi'i also holds this. If he eats the heart, according to us (Hanafis) he becomes hanith, but according to Imam Shafi'i he does not. If in an oath not to eat meat he also intends fat and grease, then he becomes hanith; otherwise not. Imam Shafi'i also holds this. If he eats back fat, it is in the ruling of meat, i.e., he becomes hanith. Imam Shafi'i also holds this. Benefit: If someone swears not to eat goat meat and then eats sheep or mutton, he becomes hanith. This is the fatwa. If he swears not to eat cow meat and then eats buffalo meat, he does not become hanith.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (مسائل) on various aspects of oaths regarding meat. It lists several specific cases: raw meat (head and feet are considered meat and break the oath; heart breaks the oath according to Hanafis but not Shafi'is; intention regarding fat affects whether the oath is broken; back fat is treated as meat. The second part (فائدہ) addresses oaths on specific animal types: if one swears not to eat goat meat, eating sheep or mutton (similar animals) breaks the oath; but if one swears not to eat cow meat, eating buffalo does not break the oath because they are different species. The author states that the fatwa is on the goat/sheep ruling.

Key Terms
سری و پائے (surī wa pā'e): Head and feet of an animal; considered meat in oaths.
چربی (charbī): Fat; if intended in the oath, it is included; back fat is treated like meat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears not to eat meat and then eats the heart of an animal, what is the ruling?
A) He does not become hanith because heart is not meat.
B) He becomes hanith.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) He becomes hanith only if he eats it cooked.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths about meat require attention to specific categories: raw meat, parts like head/feet/heart/back fat, and intention for fat, as well as animal species distinctions.

Oath on Fat: Belly Fat vs Back Fat
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proofs and language condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on what types of fat break an oath on fat, distinguish the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule regarding intention and language.

Core Idea
In an oath on fat, only belly fat is certain; back fat is considered meat by Abu Hanifa.

This lesson explores a specific intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of the word 'shahm' (fat) in oaths. When someone swears not to eat or buy fat, which type of fat triggers the breach? The answer depends on whether the fat is from the belly, the back, or even the tail of a sheep. We will walk through the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and see how intention and language affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the disputed fat type
The scenario: a person swears not to eat or buy 'shahm' (fat). The dispute is whether back fat (شحم الظهر) is included. First, understand the legal significance: the oath is broken (حنث) only if the item falls under the term used.
The ruling on oath breaking is a consequence, not a hukm on the act itself.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that only belly fat breaks the oath. His argument: back fat is really meat because it originates from blood, is used like meat, and provides strength. Therefore, it does not fall under 'shahm' but under 'lahm' (meat).
He uses the biological origin and common usage as evidence.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani include back fat as well. Their evidence: back fat melts by fire, a property of fat. Thus, it is 'fatty meat' and is included in 'shahm' by virtue of this property.
They emphasize the physical property over the biological origin.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and the tarjih
The text gives a counter-argument: if back fat were true fat, selling it would break an oath on selling fat, but it does not (it only breaks an oath on selling meat). This supports Abu Hanifa. The author then declares Abu Hanifa's view as correct (الصحیح), citing al-Dhakhira and al-Tahawi. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
However, if the swearer specifically intended back fat, all agree the oath is broken.
5
Note the language condition and the third type (ilyah)
The dispute only applies if the oath is in Arabic; in Persian, the word 'pih' never applies to back fat. Also, the fat tail (إلية) of a sheep is a third type; buying or eating it does not break an oath on meat or fat because it is a distinct category.
This highlights the role of 'urf (custom) and language in defining legal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم کھائی چربی نہ کھائے گانہ خریدے گا کونسی چربی مراد ہوگی
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ اَوْلَا يَشْتَرِى شَحْمَالَمْ يَحْنَتْ إِلَّا فِى شَحْمِ الْبَطْنِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَحْنَتُ فِي شَحْمِ الظُّهْرِ أَيْضًا وَ هُوَ اللَّحْمُ السَّمِينُ لِوُجُودِ خَاصِيَّةِ الشَّحْمِ فِيهِ وَهُوَ الدَّوبُ بِالنَّارِ وَلَهُ إِنَّهُ لَحْمٌ حَقِيقَةٌ اَلَاتَرى أَنَّهُ يَنْشَأُ مِنَ الدَّمِ وَيُسْتَعْمَلُ اسْتِعْمَالَهُ وَيَحْضُلُ بِهِ قُوَّتُهُ وَلِهَذَا يَحْنِتُ بِأَكْلِهِ فِي الْيَمِيْنِ عَلَى أَكْلِ اللَّحْمِ وَلَا يَحْنِتُ بِبَيْعِهِ فِي الْيَمِينِ عَلَى بَيْعِ الشَّحْمِ وَقِيلَ هَذَا بِالْعَرْبِيَّةِ فَأَمَّا اِسْمُ بِيَهِ بِالْفَارِسِيَّةِ لَا يَقَعُ عَلَى شَحْمِ الظَّهْرِ بِحَالٍ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَشْتَرِى اَوْلَا يَأْكُلُ لَحْمًا أَوْشَحْمًا فَاشْتَرَى إِلْيَةٌ أَوْا كَلَهَا لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ نَوْعٌ ثَالِثُ حَتَّى لَا يُسْتَعْمَلَ اِسْتِعْمَالَ اللحُومِ وَالشُّحُومِ
ترجمہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں دبا نہیں کھاؤ لگا انہیں اخر مروں گا۔ تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ
پیٹ کی چربی کے سوا کسی دوسری چربی کے کھانے یا خریدنے میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ پیٹھ کی چربی میں بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ پیٹھ کی چربی ایک طرح کا چر بیلا گوشت ہوتا ہے۔ اس وجہ سے کہ اس میں چربی کی خاصیت پائی جاتی ہے یعنی وہ گوشت آگ سے پکھل جاتا ہے۔ اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ در حقیقت گوشت ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ وہ خون سے پیدا ہوتا ہے۔ اور گوشت ہی کی طرح استعمال بھی کیا جاتا ہے۔ اور گوشت ہی کی قوت بھی دیتا ہے۔ اسی لئے اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا اور اسے کھالیا تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں چربی نہ بیچوں گا تو اس کے بیچنے سے حانث نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور بعض فقہا نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے کہ جب عربی میں مقسم انظہر کہا ہو۔ کیونکہ اگر فاری میں پسی کا لفظ کہا تو کسی حال میں یہ لفظ ہم اظہر (پیٹھ کی چربی) پر واقع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں گوشت یا چہ بی نہیں خریدوں گا یا نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اس نے دنبہ کی چکنی خریدی یا کھائی تو حانث نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ یہ تیسری قسم ہے یہاں تک کہ یہ چکتی خالص گوشت یا چربی کی طرح استعمال نہیں کی جاتی ہے۔
تشریح امام ابوحنیفہ اور صاحبین کے درمیان پیٹھ کی چربی شھم (انتظہر کالفظ کہنے میں) اختلاف ہے یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک پیٹ کی چربی کے سوائے کسی چربی میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ پیٹھ کی چربی میں بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر فارسی میں لفظ پیہ کہا تو یہ لفظ کسی حال میں فحم الظہر پر واقع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ فائدہ امام ابوحنیفہ کا قول ہی صحیح ہے۔ الذخیرہ اور طحاوی نے کہا ہے کہ یہی قول امام محد و امام شافعی امام مالک رحمتہ اللہ علیہم کا ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے پیٹھ کی چربی کی بھی نیت کی ہو تو بالاتفاق حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور کافی میں ہے کہ چربی چارتسم کی ہوتی ہے۔ نمبرا پیٹھ کی چربی ۔ نمبر وہ چربی جو ہڈی سے ملی ہوتی ہے۔ نمبر وہ چربی جو آنتوں پر ہوتی ہے۔ ائمہ کا مذکورہ اختلاف ان ہی تینوں قسموں میں ہے۔ نمبر پیٹ کی چربی میں کوئی اختلاف نہیں ہے۔
If someone swears not to eat or not to buy fat: which fat is intended? He (Imam Muhammad) said: If someone swears not to eat or not to buy shahm (fat), he does not become hanith except in belly fat according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He becomes hanith also in back fat, because it is fatty meat, as the property of fat exists in it, which is melting by fire. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it is really meat; do you not see that it originates from blood and is used like meat and gives strength? For this reason, one becomes hanith by eating it in an oath on eating meat, but does not become hanith by selling it in an oath on selling fat. It is said that this is in Arabic; as for the name in Persian (pih), it does not apply to back fat at all. If someone swears not to buy or not to eat meat or fat, and then buys or eats ilyah (fat tail), he does not become hanith because it is a third type, as it is not used like meats and fats. Translation: Imam Muhammad said in Jami' al-Saghir that if someone swears... According to Abu Hanifa, he does not become hanith in eating or buying any fat except belly fat. And the Sahibayn said that he becomes hanith also in back fat because back fat is a type of fatty meat; because the property of fat exists in it, i.e., it melts by fire. Abu Hanifa's argument is that it is really meat; do you not see that it originates from blood, is used like meat, and gives strength? Therefore, if someone swears not to eat meat and eats it, he becomes hanith; but if he swears not to sell fat and sells it, he does not become hanith. Some jurists said that this difference applies when the oath is in Arabic; because if in Persian the word 'pih' is used, this word never applies to back fat. If someone swears not to buy or eat meat or fat, and then buys or eats ilyah (fat tail of sheep), he does not become hanith because it is a third type, as it is not used like pure meat or fat. Explanation: The difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is regarding back fat (شحم الظهر) when the word is uttered; i.e., according to Abu Hanifa, he does not become hanith in any fat except belly fat. The Sahibayn said he becomes hanith also in back fat. And if in Persian the word 'pih' is said, this word never applies to back fat. Benefit: Abu Hanifa's opinion is correct. Al-Dhakhira and al-Tahawi said this is also the opinion of Imam Muhammad, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Malik. And if he intends back fat, then by agreement he becomes hanith. In al-Kafi it is said that fat is of four types: (1) back fat, (2) fat mixed with bone, (3) fat on intestines, and (4) belly fat. The mentioned difference among the imams concerns these three types; there is no difference regarding belly fat.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about which types of fat are included in an oath not to eat or buy 'shahm' (fat). The text begins with the Arabic statement from Imam Muhammad (one of the Sahibayn) that according to Abu Hanifa, only belly fat (شحم البطن) triggers breaking the oath, while the two (Sahibayn) add back fat (شحم الظهر). The Sahibayn argue that back fat has the property of fat (melting by fire) and is fatty meat. Abu Hanifa counters that back fat is really meat because it originates from blood and is used like meat; thus, eating it breaks an oath on meat but selling it does not break an oath on fat. The ruling is also affected by language: if the oath is in Arabic, the dispute exists; if in Persian (pih), the word never applies to back fat, so only belly fat is included. The text then discusses a third type, ilyah (fat tail), which is a separate category: if one swears not to eat meat or fat, eating ilyah does not break the oath because it is a third type not used like meat or fat. The commentary (تشریح) reaffirms the difference and states that Abu Hanifa's view is correct, citing Al-Dhakhira and al-Tahawi, and adding that if the swearer intends back fat, all agree the oath is broken. Finally, a quote from al-Kafi lists four types of fat: back fat, bone-mixed fat, intestinal fat, and belly fat, noting that the dispute is only over the first three; belly fat is undisputed.

Key Terms
شحم (šahm): Fat; in oaths, generally refers to belly fat, with dispute about back fat.
إلية (ilyah): Fat tail of a sheep; a third type not considered meat or fat in oaths.
حنث (hanith): The state of breaking an oath; legal consequence of fulfilling the prohibited action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'I will not eat fat' (شحم) in Arabic, and then eats back fat (شحم الظهر), does he break the oath according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Yes, he breaks the oath because back fat is fat.
B) No, because back fat is considered meat, not fat.
C) Only if he also intended back fat.
D) Yes, because the Sahibayn say so.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'shahm' in an oath is interpreted restrictively: only belly fat certainly breaks the oath; back fat is treated as meat unless intended otherwise.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Oath on Wheat and Bread
Dispute on whether an oath on wheat includes bread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on whether an oath on wheat includes bread, explain his literal meaning argument, and identify the practical difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An oath on raw wheat is broken only by chewing the grain, not by eating its bread, according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of an oath on wheat (حنطة). Does swearing not to eat wheat also prohibit consuming bread made from it? The answer depends on whether the literal meaning of 'wheat' takes precedence over customary usage. We will examine the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and explore the principle that determines the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa
Identify the ruling that Abu Hanifa gives: that eating bread from the wheat does not break the oath. Note that the oath is only broken by chewing the raw grain (قضمة).
This is the literalist position.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's opposing view
The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that eating bread does break the oath, because customarily an oath on wheat is understood to include its common forms.
They emphasise عرف (custom).
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the word 'wheat' has a used literal meaning (حقيقة مستعملة): wheat is boiled, fried, and eaten as chewing. This literal meaning is dominant over the customary metaphorical meaning (المجاز المتعارف).
The principle: al-haqiqah muqaddamah ala al-majaz (literal takes precedence over metaphorical).
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that because the literal meaning of 'wheat' is not commonly used alone (people rarely eat raw wheat), the customary understanding (عرف) that bread is included takes effect. They draw an analogy to an oath not to place one's foot in a house, which includes any entry.
They use qiyas to the 'foot' analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, a person who eats bread after swearing not to eat wheat does not need to make kaffarah for breaking the oath; under the Sahibayn, he does.
The consequence affects whether atonement (kaffarah) is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم کھائی کہ اس گندم کو نہیں کھاؤں گا چہائے بغیر کھانے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَايَاكُلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْحِنْطَةِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ حَتَّى يَقْضِمَهَا وَلَوْ أَكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهَا لَمْ يَحْنِتُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا إِنْ أَكَلَ مِنْ خُيْرِهَا حَنَتْ أَيْضًا لِاَنَّهُ مَفْهُومٌ مِنْهُ عُرْفَا وَلَا بِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ مُسْتَعْمَلَةٌ فَإِنَّهَا تُعْلَى وَتُقْلَىٰ وَتُؤْكَلُ قَضْمَاوَهِيَ قَاضِيَةٌ عَلَى الْمَجَازِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْأَصْلُ عِنْدَهُ وَلَوْقَضَمَهَا حَدَثَ عِنْدَ هُمَا هُوَ
الصَّحِيحُ لِعُمُومِ الْمَجَازِ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَضَعُ قَدَمَهُ فِي دَارِفُلَانِ وَإِلَيْهِ الْإِشَارَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ فِي الْخُبْزِ حَنَتَ أَيْضًا ترجمہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں اس گیہوں سے نہیں کھاؤں گا تو جب تک اس کو چبا کر نہیں کھائے گا وہ حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر گیہوں کے آٹے سے روٹی پکا کر کھائی تو بھی امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ لیکن صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اگر اس کی روٹی بھی کھالی جب بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عرف میں اس قسم سے روٹی کھانا بھی سمجھا جاتا ہے۔ یعنی اس کی روٹی بھی نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ گیہوں کھانے کے حقیقی معنی بھی مستعمل ہیں۔ کیونکہ گیہوں ابال کر اور بھون کر چبا کر بھی کھائے جاتے ہیں۔ اور جو حقیقت مستعمل ہو وہ متعارف مجاز سے اعلی وارفع ہوتی ہے۔ جیسا کہ امام اعظم کے نزدیک اصل مقرر ہے۔ اور اگر اسے چبا کر کھایا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک بھی عموما مجاز کی وجہ سے حانث ہو
If someone swears not to eat this wheat, he does not become hanith until he chews it (raw). And if he eats bread made from it, he does not become hanith according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Sahibayn) said: If he eats bread made from it, he becomes hanith as well, because it is understood from it customarily. And for Abu Hanifa, it has a used literal meaning (ḥaqīqah musta'malah), because it is boiled, fried, and eaten as chewing, and that literal meaning is dominant over the customary metaphorical meaning, as is his principle. And if he chews it, it becomes (hanith) according to both? The correct (opinion) is due to the generality of the metaphorical, like if someone swears not to place his foot in so-and-so's house. And to this he pointed with his saying: 'in bread he also becomes hanith.' Translation: If someone swears 'I will not eat from this wheat', he does not become hanith until he chews it. If he cooks flour from the wheat and eats bread, according to Abu Hanifa he does not become hanith. But the Sahibayn said that even if he eats its bread, he becomes hanith, because in custom, such an oath is understood to include eating bread, i.e., 'I will not eat its bread either.' Abu Hanifa's argument is that eating wheat has a literal meaning that is used: wheat is boiled, fried, and eaten by chewing. And a used literal meaning is superior and higher than the customary metaphorical meaning, as is the principle of the Great Imam. But if he chews it (raw), then according to the Sahibayn also, due to the generality of the metaphorical, he becomes hanith.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether an oath not to eat wheat (حنطة) includes bread made from that wheat. The text begins by stating the ruling: the oath is only broken by chewing the raw wheat grains, not by eating the wheat in other forms like cooked. Then it presents the difference: Abu Hanifa holds that eating bread made from the wheat does not break the oath, because the literal meaning of 'eating wheat' is chewing the raw grain. He argues that wheat can be eaten raw, boiled, or fried, so the literal meaning is common and takes precedence over the customary metaphorical meaning that might include bread. The Sahibayn argue that customarily, an oath not to eat wheat is understood to include any form of that wheat, especially bread, so eating bread breaks the oath. They analogize to an oath not to place a foot in a house, which includes entering by any means. The commentary (تشریح from the Urdu translation) explains that if the person chews the raw wheat, even the Sahibayn agree that the oath is broken because the literal meaning is fulfilled. The text also notes a parallel: an oath not to 'put one's foot' in a house is broken by any entry, not just stepping with the foot. This illustrates the principle that customary understanding can override literal meaning, but Abu Hanifa gives precedence to the used literal meaning.

Key Terms
حنطة (hintah): Wheat; in oaths, the literal meaning is the raw grain.
قضمة (qaḍmah): A bite; chewing raw grain constitutes the literal act of eating wheat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what action must be performed to break an oath not to eat wheat?
A) Eating any product made from the wheat, including bread.
B) Chewing the raw wheat grain.
C) Swallowing the wheat without chewing.
D) Cooking the wheat before eating.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, a used literal meaning (haqiqah musta'malah) takes precedence over customary metaphorical meaning, but later practice often adopts the Sahibayn's custom-based view.

Oath Not to Eat Fat: Dispute Over Back Fat
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on back fat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether back fat constitutes fat or meat in an oath, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Back fat is meat according to Abu Hanifa — an oath against fat does not include it.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the classification of back fat (shahm al-dhahr). When someone swears not to eat fat, does eating back fat break the oath? Abu Hanifa says no because back fat is essentially meat, while the Sahibayn say yes because it has the properties of fat. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining when an oath is violated.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Imam Abu Hanifa rules regarding back fat in an oath against fat.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and how it differs.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the Sahibayn's evidence: back fat cooks on fire like fat, so it is fatty meat.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain Abu Hanifa's counter-evidence: back fat originates from blood, is used like meat, and gives strength like meat.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: when one swears not to eat fat, does eating back fat trigger hanth?
Also note the effect when one swears not to eat meat.

Source Text

اليمين في الاكل والشرب
پیٹ کی چربی کے سوا کسی دوسری چربی کے کھانے یا خریدنے میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ پیٹھ کی چربی میں بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ پیٹھ کی چربی ایک طرح کا چر بیلا گوشت ہوتا ہے۔ اس وجہ سے کہ اس میں چربی کی خاصیت پائی جاتی ہے یعنی وہ گوشت آگ سے پکھل جاتا ہے۔ اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ در حقیقت گوشت ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ وہ خون سے پیدا ہوتا ہے۔ اور گوشت ہی کی طرح استعمال بھی کیا جاتا ہے۔ اور گوشت ہی کی قوت بھی دیتا ہے۔ اسی لئے اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا اور اسے کھالیا تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں چربی نہ بیچوں گا تو اس کے بیچنے سے حانث نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور بعض فقہا نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے کہ جب عربی میں مقسم انظہر کہا ہو۔ کیونکہ اگر فاری میں پسی کا لفظ کہا تو کسی حال میں یہ لفظ ہم اظہر (پیٹھ کی چربی) پر واقع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں گوشت یا چہ بی نہیں خریدوں گا یا نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اس نے دنبہ کی چکنی خریدی یا کھائی تو حانث نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ یہ تیسری قسم ہے یہاں تک کہ یہ چکتی خالص گوشت یا چربی کی طرح استعمال نہیں کی جاتی ہے۔ فائدہ .... اور اگر تم کھائی کہ بکری کا گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر بھیڑ یا دنبہ کا گوشت کھالیا تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اس پر فتویٰ ہے اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کے گائے کا گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر بھینس کا گوشت کھا لیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ تشریح امام ابوحنیفہ اور صاحبین کے درمیان پیٹھ کی چربی شھم (انتظہر کالفظ کہنے میں) اختلاف ہے یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک پیٹ کی چربی کے سوائے کسی چربی میں حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ پیٹھ کی چربی میں بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر فارسی میں لفظ پیہ کہا تو یہ لفظ کسی حال میں فحم الظہر پر واقع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ فائدہ امام ابوحنیفہ کا قول ہی صحیح ہے۔ الذخیرہ اور طحاوی نے کہا ہے کہ یہی قول امام محد و امام شافعی امام مالک رحمتہ اللہ علیہم کا ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے پیٹھ کی چربی کی بھی نیت کی ہو تو بالاتفاق حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور کافی میں ہے کہ چربی چارتسم کی ہوتی ہے۔ نمبرا پیٹھ کی چربی ۔ نمبر وہ چربی جو ہڈی سے ملی ہوتی ہے۔ نمبر وہ چربی جو آنتوں پر ہوتی ہے۔ ائمہ کا مذکورہ اختلاف ان ہی تینوں قسموں میں ہے۔ نمبر پیٹ کی چربی میں کوئی اختلاف نہیں ہے۔
Oath regarding eating and drinking. He will not be guilty (hanith) by eating or buying any fat except belly fat. And the two companions (Sahibayn) said that he will be guilty also with back fat. Because back fat is a kind of fatty meat, because it possesses the property of fat, i.e., that meat cooks on fire. And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that it is in reality meat. Do they not see that it is produced from blood? And it is used like meat, and it gives strength like meat. Therefore, if one swears 'I will not eat meat' and eats it, he becomes guilty. And if one swears 'I will not sell fat', selling it does not make him guilty. And some jurists said that this difference is when he said the phrase in Arabic 'shahm al-dhahr'. Because if he said the Persian word 'pih', this word never applies to shahm al-dhahr. And if one swears 'I will not buy or eat meat or fat', then buys or eats sheep fat, he does not become guilty, because it is a third type, since this fat is not used like pure meat or fat. Benefit: And if one swears 'I will not eat goat meat', then eats sheep or mutton meat, he becomes guilty; this is the fatwa. And if one swears 'I will not eat beef', then eats buffalo meat, he does not become guilty. Explanation: There is a difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding back fat (when saying the phrase shahm al-dhahr). That is, according to Abu Hanifa, he will not become guilty with any fat except belly fat. And the two companions said he will become guilty also with back fat. And if he says the Persian word 'pih', this word never applies to back fat. Benefit: Abu Hanifa's statement is correct. Al-Dhakhirah and Al-Tahawi said that this is the statement of Imam Muhammad, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on them). And if he intended also back fat, then by consensus he becomes guilty. And in Al-Kafi, it is said that fat is of four types: first, back fat; second, fat that is mixed with bone; third, fat that is on the intestines. The mentioned difference of the imams is regarding these three types. Fourth, belly fat: there is no difference regarding it.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on oaths related to eating or buying fat. The central dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerns whether back fat (shahm al-dhahr) is considered fat or meat for the purpose of an oath. The Sahibayn argue that back fat is fatty meat and thus falls under the category of fat. Abu Hanifa counters that back fat is essentially meat—it originates from blood, is used like meat, and provides similar sustenance. Therefore, if one swears not to eat meat and eats back fat, they have broken their oath; but if one swears not to eat fat and eats back fat, they have not broken their oath. The text also notes that this dispute applies only when the Arabic word 'shahm' is used; if the Persian word 'pih' is used, it never refers to back fat. Furthermore, the text clarifies that if one swears not to eat a specific animal's meat (e.g., goat) and eats another (e.g., sheep), the ruling depends on custom. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, and this is supported by later authorities like Al-Dhakhirah and Al-Tahawi. Finally, the text categorizes fat into four types: back fat, bone fat, intestinal fat, and belly fat, with the dispute only applying to the first three.

Key Terms
شھم (shahm): Fat; in this passage, specifically back fat (shahm al-dhahr) is in dispute.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks his oath; the legal consequence of violating a valid oath.
تسم (tasammun): Types/categories; here the four types of fat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not eat fat' and then eats back fat (shahm al-dhahr), what is the ruling?
A) He becomes hanith (breaks his oath).
B) He does not become hanith because back fat is considered meat.
C) He becomes hanith only if he also intended back fat.
D) He becomes hanith by consensus because back fat is clearly fat.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view that back fat is meat governs the mufta bihi: an oath against fat does not include back fat unless intended.

Oath Not to Eat Wheat: Chewing Requirement and Bread
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'akl' in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the meaning of 'akl' in wheat oaths and apply the principle of haqiqah musta'malah vs majaz to determine whether hanth occurs.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the real meaning of 'akl' (chewing) governs oaths unless custom (majaz) prevails—hence the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether eating bread made from wheat breaks an oath not to eat that wheat. The dispute highlights a foundational usul principle: when a word has both a real (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meaning, which should govern the interpretation of an oath? The answer affects whether kaffarah is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
A person swears 'I will not eat this wheat'. The act in question is eating bread made from that wheat. First, determine what 'akl' means in this context.
2
Locate the dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no hanth occurs from bread unless the wheat is chewed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that eating bread also breaks the oath.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the real meaning of 'akl' for wheat is chewing the grain (since wheat can be boiled/fried and chewed). This real meaning is still in active use (haqiqah musta'malah), so it takes precedence over metaphorical usage.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn rely on custom (urf): when people vow not to eat wheat, they intend all its forms including bread. Metaphor is general and widely understood, so it should be applied.
They analogize to swearing not to place a foot in a house: riding in also counts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'this is the correct saying' for the Sahibayn's view on bread. For raw wheat, chewing is still required. The operative ruling: eating bread from the wheat makes one hanith, but eating raw wheat without chewing does not.

Source Text

قسم کھائی کہ اس گندم کو نہیں کھاؤں گا چہائے بغیر کھانے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَايَاكُلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْحِنْطَةِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ حَتَّى يَقْضِمَهَا وَلَوْ أَكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهَا لَمْ يَحْنِتُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا إِنْ أَكَلَ مِنْ خُيْرِهَا حَنَتْ أَيْضًا لِاَنَّهُ مَفْهُومٌ مِنْهُ عُرْفَا وَلَا بِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ مُسْتَعْمَلَةٌ فَإِنَّهَا تُعْلَى وَتُقْلَىٰ وَتُؤْكَلُ قَضْمَاوَهِيَ قَاضِيَةٌ عَلَى الْمَجَازِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْأَصْلُ عِنْدَهُ وَلَوْقَضَمَهَا حَدَثَ عِنْدَ هُمَا هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِعُمُومِ الْمَجَازِ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَضَعُ قَدَمَهُ فِي دَارِفُلَانِ وَإِلَيْهِ الْإِشَارَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ فِي الْخُبْزِ حَنَتَ أَيْضًا ترجمہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں اس گیہوں سے نہیں کھاؤں گا تو جب تک اس کو چبا کر نہیں کھائے گا وہ حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر گیہوں کے آٹے سے روٹی پکا کر کھائی تو بھی امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ لیکن صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اگر اس کی روٹی بھی کھالی جب بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عرف میں اس قسم سے روٹی کھانا بھی سمجھا جاتا ہے۔ یعنی اس کی روٹی بھی نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ گیہوں کھانے کے حقیقی معنی بھی مستعمل ہیں۔ کیونکہ گیہوں ابال کر اور بھون کر چبا کر بھی کھائے جاتے ہیں۔ اور جو حقیقت مستعمل ہو وہ متعارف مجاز سے اعلی وارفع ہوتی ہے۔ جیسا کہ امام اعظم کے نزدیک اصل مقرر ہے۔ اور اگر اسے چبا کر کھایا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک بھی عموما مجاز کی وجہ سے حانث ہو

جائے گا۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ جیسے کہ کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی ہو کہ میں فلاں کے گھر میں اپنا قدم بھی نہیں رکھوں گا۔ مجاز کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے اب اس گھر میں اپنا قدم رکھے یا سوار ہو کر جائے بہر صورت حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور کتاب میں اسی طرف اشارہ بھی کیا ہے کہ روٹی کھانے سے بھی حانت ہو جاتا ہے (ف: یعنی لفظ سے بھی ظاہر ہوا کہ چبانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے قسم کھائی۔ میں گندم نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ تو وہ اس وقت تک حانث نہ ہو گا۔ جب تک اسے چبا کر نہ کھائے۔ کیونکہ کی چیز کو کھانے کا عمل اس وقت واقع ہو گا۔ جب اسے چبایا جائے۔ اکل (کھانا) بھی ایک عمل ہے۔اس کیلئے تین باتوں کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔
چبانا
۲۔ لگنا ۔ ایصال فی الجوف (پیٹ میں پہنچانا )
مذکورہ صورت میں امام ابو حنیفہ اور صاحبین (امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد) کے مابین اختلاف ہے۔ مجو شہ اختلاف کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ جس لفظ کے دو معنی (حقیقی اور مجازی) مراد لئے جاسکتے ہوں تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ لفظ حقیقی معنی میں مستعمل ہو گا۔ جبکہ صاحبین اسے مجازی معنی کیلئے استعمال کرتے ہیں۔ چونکہ اکل کے حقیقی معنی چبا کر کھانے کے متقاضی ہیں۔ اسلئے امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک جب تک گندم چبا کر نہ کھائی جائے گی۔ اس وقت تک حالف پر حث مستلزم نہ ہو گا۔ خواہ وہ گندم ابلی ہوئی ہو یا بھوئی ہوئی کیونکہ یہ چبا کر کھائی جاتی ہے۔ لہذا اکل“ کا حقیقی معنی ( یہاں پر ) زیادہ قابل ترجیح ہے۔
He swore that he will not eat this wheat; he does not become guilty until he chews it. If he ate from its bread, he does not become guilty according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: If he eats from its bread, he becomes guilty too, because it is understood from it by custom. And Abu Hanifa said that it has a real meaning that is used, for it is boiled and fried and eaten by chewing, and that (literal meaning) overrides the conventional metaphorical meaning, as is his principle. And if he chewed it, according to both it is correct because of the generality of the metaphor, as when one swears 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house'; and to this there is reference in his saying 'in bread, he also becomes guilty'. Translation: If someone swore 'I will not eat this wheat', he does not become guilty until he chews and eats it. And if he cooked bread from the wheat flour and ate it, according to Abu Hanifa he also does not become guilty. But the two companions said that if he ate its bread, he becomes guilty. Because by custom, eating bread is also understood from this oath, i.e., 'I will not eat its bread either'. And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that the real meaning of 'eating wheat' is used, for wheat is boiled, fried, and eaten by chewing. And a real meaning that is used is higher and loftier than a conventional metaphor, as is the established principle of Imam al-A'zam. And if he chewed it, according to the two companions also, generally due to metaphor, he becomes guilty. This is the correct saying. For example, if someone swore 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house', due to the generality of metaphor, whether he places his foot or goes riding, in any case he becomes guilty. And in the book, it is indicated that eating bread also makes him guilty. Explanation: The scenario is that if a person swore 'I will not eat wheat', he does not become guilty until he chews it. Because the action of eating occurs when it is chewed. 'Eating' (akl) is an action requiring three things: chewing, swallowing, and reaching the stomach. In this case, there is a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions. The reason for the difference is that when a word has two meanings (real and metaphorical), Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the word is used in its real meaning, while the two companions use it in its metaphorical meaning. Since the real meaning of 'akl' necessitates chewing, according to Abu Hanifa, until the wheat is chewed, the swearer does not incur hanth, whether the wheat is boiled or fried, because it is eaten by chewing. Therefore, the real meaning of 'akl' is more preferable here.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses a scenario where a person swears not to eat a specific quantity of wheat. The central issue is whether eating bread made from that wheat breaks the oath. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between eating the wheat in its original form (chewed) and consuming it as bread; he holds that the literal meaning of 'akl' (eating) involves chewing, so the oath is only broken when the wheat is chewed. The two companions (Sahibayn) argue that customarily, the oath includes eating anything derived from the wheat, such as bread, because the intent is to consume the substance. They analogize to the case of swearing not to enter a house by placing one's foot—riding in also constitutes entry by metaphorical extension. The text explains that this ikhtilaf stems from a broader usul al-fiqh principle: when a word has both a real and a metaphorical meaning, Abu Hanifa prioritize the real meaning if it is commonly used, while the Sahibayn give weight to the metaphorical meaning if it is widespread in custom. The relied-upon position according to the text is that of the Sahibayn for bread (i.e., hanth occurs), but for raw wheat, chewing is required.

Key Terms
حِنْطَة (hintah): Wheat; the grain itself, not its flour or bread.
قَضْم (qadm): Chewing; the action of breaking food with teeth before swallowing.
حقيقة مستعملة (haqiqah musta'malah): A literal meaning that is still in active common use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person swears not to eat wheat and eats bread made from that wheat, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes hanith immediately.
B) He does not become hanith because bread is not wheat.
C) He becomes hanith only if he chews the bread.
D) He does not become hanith because the literal meaning of eating wheat is chewing the grain.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the literal meaning of 'akl' (chewing) or the customary understanding (any consumption) governs oaths involving wheat; the relied-upon view holds that bread breaks the oath.

Oath on Flour: Interpreting Terms by Custom
Ruling on eating flour directly vs. bread from that flour

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath on an inedible ingredient refers to its cooked form, and state how custom determines the meaning of 'bread' in oaths.

Core Idea
Flour is not eaten raw; an oath on flour refers to its bread.

When someone swears an oath on an ingredient that is not typically consumed in its raw state, the Hanafi school interprets the oath to refer to the prepared food. This lesson examines the rulings from Al-Hidayah on oaths regarding flour and bread, and how local custom ('urf) defines what counts as 'bread'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the oath
Determine the exact term used: is it 'flour' (دقيق) or 'bread' (خبز)? This sets the scope for interpretation.
2
Assess literal possibility
Ask whether the literal meaning can be fulfilled. If the object is not typically eaten raw (like flour), the literal meaning is impossible, so we move to metaphorical interpretation.
3
Apply the principle of majaz
When literal is impossible, the oath is understood to refer to the customary product made from that ingredient. For flour, that is bread.
4
Determine customary bread for oaths on 'bread'
If the oath is on 'bread', the default is the bread commonly eaten in that city (usually wheat or barley). Special breads (e.g., qata'if, rice bread) are excluded unless customary or intended.
5
Check for exceptions and local custom
In a place where rice bread is the staple, it becomes the customary 'bread', so eating it breaks the oath. Similarly, if the swearer specifically intends a special bread, that intention governs.

قسم کھائی اس آٹے سے نہیں کھائے گا پھر اس کی روٹی کھالی حانث ہو جائے گا
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هَذَا الدَّقِيقِ فَاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهِ حَنَتْ لِاَنَّ عَيْنَهُ غَيْرَ مَا كُولٍ فَانْصَرَفَ إِلَى مَا يُتَّخَذُ مِنْهُ وَلَوِ اسْتَقَهُ كَمَا هُوَ لَا يَحْنِتُ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِتَعَيْنِ الْمَجَازِ مُرَادَا وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ خُبْزَا فَيَمِيْتُهُ عَلَى مَايُعْتَادُ أَهْلُ المصرا كلُهُ خُبْرًا وَ ذَالِكَ خَيْزُ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُعْتَادُ فِي غَالِبِ الْبُلْدَانِ وَلَوْاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِ الْقَطَائِفِ لا يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى خُبْرًا مُطْلَقًا إِلَّا إِذَانَوَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَ خُبْزَ الْارُزِ بِالْعِرَاقِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لاَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعْتَادٍ عِندَهُمْ حَتَّى لَوْ كَانَ بِطِبْرِسْتانَ اَوْ فِي بَلْدَةٍ طَعَامُهُمْ ذَالِكَ يَحْنِتُ
He said: And if one swears that he will not eat from this flour, then ate from its bread, he becomes guilty, because its essence is not edible, so it refers to what is made from it. And if he swallowed it as it is, he does not become guilty; this is the correct saying, because the metaphorical meaning is determined as intended. And if one swears that he will not eat bread, then it applies to what is customary for the people of the city to eat as bread, and that is bread of wheat and barley, because that is customary in most countries. And if he eats from qata'if bread, he does not become guilty, because it is not called bread absolutely unless he intends it, because it is possible in his speech. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not become guilty, because it is not customary among them, unless he is in Tabaristan or in a city where their food is that, then he becomes guilty.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary provides rulings for oaths involving flour and bread. It establishes a key principle: when an oath is made regarding an ingredient that is not typically consumed in its raw form (like flour), the oath is understood to refer to the prepared food (bread). Therefore, eating bread made from that flour breaks the oath. Conversely, consuming the raw ingredient (swallowing flour) does not break the oath because it is not the intended way to eat it. When the oath is about 'bread' itself, the default reference is the bread commonly eaten in that city—typically wheat or barley bread. Special breads like qata'if (a stuffed pastry) or rice bread are not included unless customary in the locality or specifically intended. This highlights the role of 'urf (custom) in interpreting oaths.

Key Terms
دَقِيق (daqiq): Flour; ground grain, not typically eaten raw.
خُبْز (khubz): Bread; the prepared food from flour.
مَجَاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning; used when literal eating of flour is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat this flour' and then swallows a spoonful of raw flour, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes hanith because flour is the object of the oath.
B) He does not become hanith because raw flour is not eaten.
C) He becomes hanith only if he intends to break the oath.
D) He does not become hanith unless he eats bread from it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths on inedible raw ingredients refer to their customary cooked form, and the meaning of 'bread' is determined by local custom.

Oath Not to Eat Bread: Custom and Specific Types
Ruling on bread types and local custom in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an oath not to eat 'bread' is broken by a specific baked item, by applying the Hanafi rule of local customary staple.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'bread' means the customary staple bread of the locality.

When someone swears not to eat 'bread,' the term is interpreted according to local custom. This lesson explains how to determine which breads are included and which are not, based on the Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom).

Guided Path
1
Identify the swearer's locality
Determine the city or region where the oath was made. This is crucial because custom varies by location.
2
Determine the customary staple bread
What type of bread is commonly eaten by the people there? Typically, it is wheat or barley bread in most regions.
Examples: in Iraq, rice bread is not customary; in Tabaristan, rice bread is customary.
3
Apply the default rule
The oath applies only to that customary bread unless the swearer intended a different type. Eating a non-customary bread does not break the oath.
4
Check for exceptions
If the bread eaten is not customary, no hanth occurs unless the swearer specifically intended that type. Also, if the bread is customary in the locality of the swearer (even if not in the place of eating), the oath is broken.
Example: qatā'if bread is not called 'bread' absolutely, so it is excluded by default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ خُبْزَا فَيَمِيْتُهُ عَلَى مَايُعْتَادُ أَهْلُ المصرا كلُهُ خُبْرًا وَ ذَالِكَ خَيْزُ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُعْتَادُ فِي غَالِبِ الْبُلْدَانِ وَلَوْاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِ الْقَطَائِفِ لا يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى خُبْرًا مُطْلَقًا إِلَّا إِذَانَوَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَ خُبْزَ الْارُزِ بِالْعِرَاقِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لاَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعْتَادٍ عِندَهُمْ حَتَّى لَوْ كَانَ بِطِبْرِسْتانَ اَوْ فِي بَلْدَةٍ طَعَامُهُمْ ذَالِكَ يَحْنِتُ
And if one swears that he will not eat bread, then it applies to what the people of the city customarily eat as bread, and that is bread of wheat and barley, because that is customary in most countries. And if he eats from qata'if bread, he does not become guilty, because it is not called bread absolutely unless he intends it, because it is possible in his speech. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not become guilty, because it is not customary among them; unless he is in Tabaristan or in a city where their food is that, then he becomes guilty.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on oaths related to bread. It establishes that the default reference of 'bread' in an oath is the staple bread of the locality—typically wheat or barley bread. Specialty breads like qata'if (a filled pastry) or rice bread are not automatically included. However, if the swearer specifically intends such types, or if they are the customary bread in his region (e.g., rice bread in Tabaristan), then the oath applies. This ruling underscores the importance of 'urf in contractual and oath-based obligations in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
مُعْتَاد (mu'tad): Customary; what is commonly eaten in a locality.
خُبْز الْقَطَائِف (khubz al-qata'if): A type of stuffed pancake or pastry; not considered ordinary bread.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He becomes hanith because rice bread is bread.
B) He does not become hanith because rice bread is not customary in Iraq.
C) He becomes hanith only if he intended all types of bread.
D) He does not become hanith because rice bread is not called bread at all.

Key Takeaway

Interpreting 'Shawa' in Oaths: Custom Over Literal Meaning
Ruling on roasted meat includes meat, not vegetables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default meaning of 'shawa' in Hanafi oaths and apply it to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
In legal oaths, words are understood by common custom — 'shawa' means roasted meat, not any roasted food.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario: a person swears not to eat something described as 'roasted.' What exactly does that prohibition cover? The Hanafi school answers by looking at customary usage, not literal or dictionary definitions. Understanding this principle prevents unnecessary oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the disputed term
The person swears not to eat 'shawa'. The term is ambiguous: it could mean any roasted food or specifically roasted meat.
Ambiguity in oaths is resolved by custom.
2
Determine the default meaning by custom
In common usage ('urf), 'shawa' primarily refers to roasted meat, not roasted vegetables like eggplant or carrots.
This is the foundation of the ruling.
3
Apply to vegetables
Since 'shawa' means roasted meat, eating roasted vegetables does not violate the oath.
The passage explicitly excludes eggplant and carrots.
4
Extract the underlying principle
The reasoning (talil) is that the term is interpreted according to custom. This is a consistent Hanafi principle in oaths and contracts.
Custom ('urf) governs ambiguous expressions.

Oath on Flour: Eating Bread vs. Swallowing Raw Flour
Ruling on oaths about flour, with exception for raw consumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi ruling that an oath on flour applies to bread made from it, and distinguish when the oath is broken versus when it is not.

Core Idea
An oath on flour is interpreted metaphorically to mean its customary product (bread), but raw flour is not covered.

This lesson examines a specific case from Al-Hidayah: someone swears not to eat from a particular flour. The ruling clarifies when the oath is broken. The key is the distinction between the literal substance (flour) and its customary use (bread). This case illustrates how Hanafi fiqh relies on custom (urf) and metaphorical interpretation (majaz) when the literal meaning is not practically intended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its immediate ruling
Start with the oath: 'I will not eat from this flour.' According to the passage, eating bread made from that flour breaks the oath. Identify this as the primary ruling.
2
Identify the exception for raw consumption
Next, note that swallowing the flour as-is does not break the oath. Understand that this is because flour is not normally eaten directly; the metaphorical meaning (the product) is intended.
The phrase 'huwa al-sahih' indicates this is the correct view.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The passage states 'because the substance itself is not eaten, so it refers to what is made from it.' This is the principle: when the literal object is not customarily consumed, the oath applies to its customary derived product.
This is an application of majaz (metaphorical interpretation) in oaths.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Think of other foods that are typically cooked before eating, like rice or oats. The same principle would apply: an oath on the raw ingredient applies to the cooked dish.
Custom (urf) may vary; always check local practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هَذَا الدَّقِيقِ فَاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهِ حَنَتْ لِاَنَّ عَيْنَهُ غَيْرَ مَا كُولٍ فَانْصَرَفَ إِلَى مَا يُتَّخَذُ مِنْهُ وَلَوِ اسْتَقَهُ كَمَا هُوَ لَا يَحْنِتُ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِتَعَيْنِ الْمَجَازِ مُرَادَا وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ خُبْزَا فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَايُعْتَادُ أَهْلُ المصر كلُهُ خُبْرًا وَ ذَالِكَ خُبْزُ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُعْتَادُ فِي غَالِبِ الْبُلْدَانِ وَلَوْاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِ الْقَطَائِفِ لا يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى خُبْرًا مُطْلَقًا إِلَّا إِذَانَوَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَ خُبْزَ الْارُزِ بِالْعِرَاقِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لاَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعْتَادٍ عِندَهُمْ حَتَّى لَوْ كَانَ بِطِبْرِسْتانَ اَوْ فِي بَلْدَةٍ طَعَامُهُمْ ذَالِكَ يَحْنِتُ
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے یہ سم کھائی کہ میں اس آٹے سے نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر اس کی پکی ہوئی روٹی کھائی تو حانث ہو جائے گا کیونکہ آٹا بعینہ نہیں کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس آٹے سے جو چیز بنا کر کھائی جائے گی وہی مراد ہوگی۔ اور اگر آٹے کو اسی طرح پھانک کر کھا لیا یا منہ میں رکھ کر نگل لیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا ۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ اس جگہ مجازی معنی ہی مراد لینا متعین ہے۔ اور اگر یہ تم کھائی کہ میں روٹی نہیں کھاؤں گا تو اس قسم میں وہی روٹی داخل ہوگی جو عموما اور عادتا اس شہر میں کھائی جاتی ہو۔ اور وہ جو یا گیہوں کی روٹی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اکثر شہروں میں یہی روٹیاں کھائی جاتی ہیں۔ اور اگر قطائف ( چار مغزیعنی کھیرا۔ کرو۔ خربوزہ ۔ لکڑی اور بادام کے بیجوں کے مغز) سے تیار کی ہوئی روٹی کھائی تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عموما اسے مطلقا روٹی نہیں کہتے ہیں۔ لیکن اگر اس کی بھی نیت کی ہو تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ کہنے والے کے کلام کے یہ معنی بھی مراد لئے جا سکتے ہیں۔ اسی طرح اگر اس نے عراق میں چاول کی روٹی کھائی تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہاں کے رہنے والوں میں اس کی عادت نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے والا طبرستان کا یا کسی ایسے شہر کا رہنے والا ہو جن کا کھانا چاول ہوتا ہے تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ قسم کھائی بھنا ہوا نہیں كھائے گا گوشت مراد ہوگا
He (Al-Quduri) said: If someone swears 'I will not eat from this flour', then eats from its bread, he breaks his oath, because the substance itself is not eaten, so it refers to what is made from it. If he swallows it as it is, he does not break his oath – that is the correct opinion, because the metaphorical meaning is specifically intended. And if he swears 'I will not eat bread', his oath is upon what the people of the city customarily eat as bread, and that is wheat and barley bread, because that is what is customary in most lands. If he eats bread made from qata'if (stuffing), he does not break his oath, because it is not called bread absolutely, unless he intended it, because it is possible in his speech. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath, because it is not customary among them. However, if he is in Tabaristan or a town where their food is that, he breaks his oath. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If someone takes an oath 'I will not eat from this flour', then eats bread made from it, he becomes a breaker, because flour itself is not eaten, so what is intended is what is made from it. If he swallows the flour as it is, he does not break his oath – this is the correct opinion, because here the metaphorical meaning is specifically intended. If he swears 'I will not eat bread', the oath includes only the bread that is commonly eaten in that city, which is wheat or barley bread, because that is the custom in most cities. If he eats bread made from qata'if (a type of stuffing), he does not break his oath, because it is not generally called bread, unless he intended it, because his speech can bear that meaning. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary there. But if the oath-taker is from Tabaristan or a town where their food is rice bread, then he breaks his oath.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths concerning flour and bread. The key ruling: an oath on flour applies to bread made from it, because flour itself is not typically eaten directly. Swallowing raw flour does not break the oath; the metaphorical meaning (the product) is intended. For oaths on bread, the default is the common bread of the locality (wheat or barley). Special breads like qata'if or rice bread are excluded unless the person intended them, as they are not called 'bread' absolutely. Custom (urf) plays a crucial role: in Iraq, rice bread is not customary, so an oath on bread does not include it; but in Tabaristan, where rice bread is the staple, it does. This shows that legal rulings on oaths are shaped by prevalent custom, not just literal definitions.

Key Terms
دقيق (daqīq): Flour, which is not eaten directly but used to make bread.
خبز (khubz): Bread; in oaths, it refers to the commonly eaten bread of the region (wheat or barley).
عرف (urf): Custom; used here to interpret the scope of the oath (which type of bread is included).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears 'I will not eat from this flour' and eats bread made from it, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break his oath because bread is not flour.
B) He breaks his oath because the oath refers to the product.
C) The oath is invalid.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
An oath on flour applies to its customary product (bread), but not to raw flour; metaphorical interpretation based on custom is key.

Oath on Bread: Custom Determines the Scope
Ruling on oaths about bread, with custom as a determinant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how custom (urf) determines which types of bread are included in an oath on bread, and apply this to different regions.

Core Idea
An oath on 'bread' applies only to the bread commonly eaten in that region; unusual breads are excluded unless intended.

This lesson continues from the previous: when someone swears 'I will not eat bread', what exactly does that include? The Hanafi school, as presented in Al-Hidayah, relies on the custom of the people in that city. The default is wheat or barley bread; specialty breads like qata'if or rice bread are excluded unless the oath-taker intended them. Custom also varies by region: in Iraq, rice bread is not included, but in Tabaristan it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath wording and default ruling
The oath is 'I will not eat bread'. The default ruling: it applies to the bread commonly eaten in that city, which is usually wheat or barley bread.
2
Recognize exceptions for unusual breads
Breads like qata'if (stuffed pastry) or rice bread are not included in the absolute term 'bread' when they are not customary. They only break the oath if the person intended them.
The phrase 'لا يسمى خبزا مطلقا' indicates they are not called bread absolutely.
3
Apply regional custom variation
Custom differs by location. In Iraq, rice bread is not customary, so it is excluded. In Tabaristan, where rice bread is the staple, it is included. The oath-taker's local custom matters.
This shows the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh: the same oath can have different scopes based on location.
4
Understand the principle of intention
If the oath-taker intended a specific unusual bread (e.g., qata'if), then it becomes included because his speech can bear that meaning (muhtamal kalamih).
Intention can broaden the scope beyond custom.

قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هَذَا الدَّقِيقِ فَاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهِ حَنَتْ لِاَنَّ عَيْنَهُ غَيْرَ مَا كُولٍ فَانْصَرَفَ إِلَى مَا يُتَّخَذُ مِنْهُ وَلَوِ اسْتَقَهُ كَمَا هُوَ لَا يَحْنِتُ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِتَعَيْنِ الْمَجَازِ مُرَادَا وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ خُبْزَا فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَايُعْتَادُ أَهْلُ المصر كلُهُ خُبْرًا وَ ذَالِكَ خُبْزُ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُعْتَادُ فِي غَالِبِ الْبُلْدَانِ وَلَوْاكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِ الْقَطَائِفِ لا يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى خُبْرًا مُطْلَقًا إِلَّا إِذَانَوَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا أَكَلَ خُبْزَ الْارُزِ بِالْعِرَاقِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لاَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعْتَادٍ عِندَهُمْ حَتَّى لَوْ كَانَ بِطِبْرِسْتانَ اَوْ فِي بَلْدَةٍ طَعَامُهُمْ ذَالِكَ يَحْنِتُ
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے یہ سم کھائی کہ میں اس آٹے سے نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ پھر اس کی پکی ہوئی روٹی کھائی تو حانث ہو جائے گا کیونکہ آٹا بعینہ نہیں کھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس آٹے سے جو چیز بنا کر کھائی جائے گی وہی مراد ہوگی۔ اور اگر آٹے کو اسی طرح پھانک کر کھا لیا یا منہ میں رکھ کر نگل لیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا ۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ اس جگہ مجازی معنی ہی مراد لینا متعین ہے۔ اور اگر یہ تم کھائی کہ میں روٹی نہیں کھاؤں گا تو اس قسم میں وہی روٹی داخل ہوگی جو عموما اور عادتا اس شہر میں کھائی جاتی ہو۔ اور وہ جو یا گیہوں کی روٹی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اکثر شہروں میں یہی روٹیاں کھائی جاتی ہیں۔ اور اگر قطائف ( چار مغزیعنی کھیرا۔ کرو۔ خربوزہ ۔ لکڑی اور بادام کے بیجوں کے مغز) سے تیار کی ہوئی روٹی کھائی تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عموما اسے مطلقا روٹی نہیں کہتے ہیں۔ لیکن اگر اس کی بھی نیت کی ہو تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ کہنے والے کے کلام کے یہ معنی بھی مراد لئے جا سکتے ہیں۔ اسی طرح اگر اس نے عراق میں چاول کی روٹی کھائی تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہاں کے رہنے والوں میں اس کی عادت نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر قسم کھانے والا طبرستان کا یا کسی ایسے شہر کا رہنے والا ہو جن کا کھانا چاول ہوتا ہے تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ قسم کھائی بھنا ہوا نہیں كھائے گا گوشت مراد ہوگا
He (Al-Quduri) said: If someone swears 'I will not eat from this flour', then eats from its bread, he breaks his oath, because the substance itself is not eaten, so it refers to what is made from it. If he swallows it as it is, he does not break his oath – that is the correct opinion, because the metaphorical meaning is specifically intended. And if he swears 'I will not eat bread', his oath is upon what the people of the city customarily eat as bread, and that is wheat and barley bread, because that is what is customary in most lands. If he eats bread made from qata'if (stuffing), he does not break his oath, because it is not called bread absolutely, unless he intended it, because it is possible in his speech. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath, because it is not customary among them. However, if he is in Tabaristan or a town where their food is that, he breaks his oath. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If someone takes an oath 'I will not eat from this flour', then eats bread made from it, he becomes a breaker, because flour itself is not eaten, so what is intended is what is made from it. If he swallows the flour as it is, he does not break his oath – this is the correct opinion, because here the metaphorical meaning is specifically intended. If he swears 'I will not eat bread', the oath includes only the bread that is commonly eaten in that city, which is wheat or barley bread, because that is the custom in most cities. If he eats bread made from qata'if (a type of stuffing), he does not break his oath, because it is not generally called bread, unless he intended it, because his speech can bear that meaning. Similarly, if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary there. But if the oath-taker is from Tabaristan or a town where their food is rice bread, then he breaks his oath.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths concerning flour and bread. The key ruling: an oath on flour applies to bread made from it, because flour itself is not typically eaten directly. Swallowing raw flour does not break the oath; the metaphorical meaning (the product) is intended. For oaths on bread, the default is the common bread of the locality (wheat or barley). Special breads like qata'if or rice bread are excluded unless the person intended them, as they are not called 'bread' absolutely. Custom (urf) plays a crucial role: in Iraq, rice bread is not customary, so an oath on bread does not include it; but in Tabaristan, where rice bread is the staple, it does. This shows that legal rulings on oaths are shaped by prevalent custom, not just literal definitions.

Key Terms
دقيق (daqīq): Flour, which is not eaten directly but used to make bread.
خبز (khubz): Bread; in oaths, it refers to the commonly eaten bread of the region (wheat or barley).
عرف (urf): Custom; used here to interpret the scope of the oath (which type of bread is included).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person swears 'I will not eat bread' in Iraq, which of the following would break his oath?
A) Rice bread
B) Wheat bread
C) Qata'if bread
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
An oath on 'bread' is interpreted by local custom; only the habitual bread of the region is included unless the speaker intended a specific unusual bread.
