Oath on Roasted or Cooked Food: Applying Custom
Rulings on oath interpretation based on custom and istiḥsān

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that oath terms are interpreted according to custom (urf) and state the exception when specific intention overrides the default meaning.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'roasted' means meat; 'cooked' means meat in water—custom defines the default scope.

This passage from Al-Hidayah demonstrates how custom (urf) and istiḥsān shape the interpretation of oath terms. When someone swears not to eat roasted (shawā') or cooked (ṭabīkh) food, the default meaning is restricted to meat dishes based on prevailing custom. However, the oath-taker's personal intention can expand or limit the scope if it imposes a stricter obligation on himself.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for 'shawā'
Identify that 'roasted' defaults to roasted meat; other roasted items like eggplant or carrots are not included unless explicitly intended.
The term is restricted by custom, not literal generality.
2
State the ruling for 'ṭabīkh'
Identify that 'cooked' defaults to meat cooked in water; other cooked items are excluded unless intended. This ruling is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference) giving weight to custom.
Custom here is the common dish of the region.
3
Explain the underlying principle
Note that generalizing the term to all roasted/cooked items is impractical (mutāʿadhdhir), so it is restricted to the customarily known instance—meat prepared in the common way.
This is a qiyās khafī (subtle analogy) rooted in custom.
4
Apply the exception for specific intention
If the oath-taker intended a non‑default item (e.g., roasted eggs), his intention is accepted because it imposes a stricter burden (tashdīd) on himself. Similarly, eating the broth of cooked meat breaks the oath because broth contains meat particles and is called 'ṭabīkh'.
The oath is about the substance, not the form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الشَّوَاءَ فَهُوَ عَلَى اللَّحْمِ دُونَ الْبَاذِنْجَانِ وَالْجَزَرِلَانَّهُ يُرَادُ بِهِ اللَّحْمُ الْمَشْوِيُّ عِنْدَ الْإِطْلَاقِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَنْوِيَ مَايُشْوَى مِنْ بَيْضِ أَوْغَيْرِهِ لِمَكَانِ الْحَقِيقَةِ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الطَّبِيحَ فَهُوَ عَلَى مَا يُطْبَخُ مِنَ اللَّحْمِ وَهَذَا إِسْتِحْسَانُ اِعْتِبَارًا لِلْعُرْفِ وَهَذَا لِاَنَّ التَّعْمِيمَ مُتَعَذَّرٌ فَيُصْرَفُ إِلَى خَاصٍ هُوَ مُتَعَارَفٌ وَهُوَ اللَّحْمُ الْمَطْبُوحُ بِالْمَاءِ إِلَّا إِذَانَوَى غَيْرَ ذَالِكَ لَانَّ فِيهِ تَشْدِيدًا وَإِنْ أَكَلَ مِنْ مَرَقِهِ يَحْنِتُ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ اللَّحْمِ وَلِأَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى طَبِيحًا
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں بھنا ہوا نہیں كھاؤں گا۔ تو اس کا اطلاق فقط گوشت پر ہوگا۔ اور بیگن اور گاجر و شکر قند پر نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جب مطلق بھنا ہوا کہا جاتا ہے تو بھنا ہوا گوشت مراد ہوتا ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے انڈے وغیرہ بھنے ہوئے کی نیت کی ہو تو حقیقی معنی پائے جانے کی وجہ سے نیت کے مطابق قسم واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ اور اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں طبيح یعنی پکائی ہوئی چیز نہیں کھاؤں گا تو اس قسم کا اطلاق پکائے ہوئے گوشت پر ہو گا۔ یہ استحسانی حکم عرف کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ہر پکائی ہوئی چیز کو قسم میں داخل کرنا مشکل ہے اس لئے اس کے خاص معنی ہی مراد لئے جائیں گے۔ جو رائج ہوں یعنی پانی میں پکایا ہوا گوشت۔ لیکن اگر اس نے گوشت کے علاوہ کسی دوسری چیز کی بھی نیت کی ہو تو اس کی بات کی تصدیق کی جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ ایسا ہونے سے خود اس پر سختی زیادہ لازم آتی ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے پکائے ہوئے گوشت کا شوربا بھی کھایا تو بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس میں بھی گوشت کے اجزاء پائے جاتے ہیں۔ اس لئے اسے بھی طبيح کہا جاتا ہے۔ تشریح حاصل یہ کہ قسم کے الفاظ سے وہ معنی مراد لیئے جائیں گے جو عرف عام میں معتبر ہوں اور ان کا رواج ہو شرعی یا لغوی معنی غیر معتبر ہوں گے۔
If someone swears 'I will not eat roasted [food]', it applies to meat, not to eggplant or carrots, because absolutely it means roasted meat, unless he intends something roasted like eggs or other things, due to the literal meaning. If he swears 'I will not eat cooked [food]', it applies to what is cooked from meat, and this is istihsan considering custom. This is because generalization is impossible, so it is turned to a specific thing that is commonly known, which is meat cooked with water, unless he intends something else, because that would be a stricter imposition. If he eats from its broth, he breaks his oath, because it contains parts of meat, and because it is called cooked. Translation: If someone swears 'I will not eat roasted food', it applies only to meat, not to eggplant or carrots, because when 'roasted' is said absolutely, roasted meat is meant. If he intends something roasted like eggs, then due to the literal meaning, the oath applies according to his intention. If he swears 'I will not eat cooked food (tabeeh)', the oath applies to cooked meat. This is an istihsani ruling based on custom, because including every cooked thing in the oath is difficult, so only the specific common meaning is intended, i.e., meat cooked in water. If he intends something else, his word is accepted because it imposes a stricter obligation on him. If he eats the broth of the cooked meat, he breaks his oath because it contains meat particles and is called 'cooked'.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage covers oaths on roasted (shawa) and cooked (tabeeh) food. For 'roasted', the default meaning is roasted meat, not vegetables like eggplant or carrots. However, if the oath-taker intends something else like roasted eggs, his intention is accepted because the literal meaning allows it. For 'cooked' (tabeeh), the default is meat cooked in water, not other cooked items. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) giving weight to prevailing custom (urf). The reasoning: generalizing 'cooked' to everything is impractical, so custom restricts it to the most common dish: meat cooked in water. If the oath-taker intends something else, his claim is accepted because it makes his oath more restrictive (tashdid) for himself. Importantly, eating the broth of the cooked meat also breaks the oath, because the broth contains meat particles and is called 'cooked' as well. The concluding note in the commentary states: the words of an oath are interpreted according to prevalent custom, not literal or shar'i definitions.

Key Terms
شواء (shawā'): Roasted food; in oaths, it refers to roasted meat by default.
طبيح (tabeeh): Cooked food; in oaths, it means meat cooked in water by default.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; here used to give priority to custom over strict literal interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat cooked food (tabeeh)' and then eats broth of cooked meat, does he break his oath?
A) No, because broth is not solid food.
B) Yes, because broth contains meat particles and is called 'cooked'.
C) No, unless he intended broth.
D) Yes, only if he eats the meat itself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jurisprudence, oath terms like 'roasted' and 'cooked' are interpreted according to prevailing custom, with specific intention accepted only if it imposes a stricter obligation.

Oath on Heads: Role of Custom in Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths with heads; custom as determinant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differed on which heads are included in an oath and apply the principle that custom (urf) determines the scope of the oath.

Core Idea
Custom determines which heads are covered in an oath—only those commonly eaten, not all meaty heads.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath not to eat heads (ru'us). Imam Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, held different views on whether the oath covers cow and sheep heads or only sheep heads. The reason for their disagreement is explicitly attributed to the difference in custom (urf) in their respective times. The passage then generalizes that fatwa must follow the prevalent custom of one's own time and place. Understanding this ruling illustrates the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh, where custom can override literal linguistic generality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the act is an oath: 'I will not eat heads'. The dispute is about which animal heads are included. Abu Hanifa says cow and sheep; Sahibayn say only sheep.
The oath is binding (wajib) but its scope is ambiguous.
2
State each position clearly
Imam Abu Hanifa includes heads of cattle (baqar) and sheep (ghanam). The two companions restrict to sheep alone. Note that both positions are within the Hanafi school.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf recorded in Al-Jami' al-Saghir.
3
Understand the cause of the disagreement
The text says the difference is due to 'ikhtilaf misr wa zaman'—difference in city and time. In Abu Hanifa's era, both cow and sheep heads were commonly eaten; in the Sahibayn's era, only sheep heads were common.
Custom (urf) is the operative factor, not a difference in legal principle.
4
Apply the general rule: fatwa follows custom
The author states that fatwa is given according to the custom of the age (hasab al-'adah). This means the ruling changes with time and place.
This reflects the Hanafi maxim 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).
5
Recognize the limitation: not all edible heads
The commentary clarifies that heads of birds or pigs (even if halal/haram) are not included because they are not customarily eaten. Only heads commonly consumed in the locality are meant.
Literal generality ('any head') is restricted by customary usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الرُّؤْسَ فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَا يُكْبَسُ فِى التَّنَانِيرِ وَيُبَاعُ فِى الْمِصْرِوَيُقَالُ يَكْنِسُ وَ فِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ رَأْسًا فَهُوَ عَلَى رُؤْسِ الْبَقَرِ وَالْغَنَمِ عِنْدَابِي حَنِيْفَةَ وَقَالَ أَبُوْ يُوْسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ عَلَى الْغَنَمِ خَاصَّةً وَهذَا اخْتِلَافُ مِصْرٍ وَزَمَانِ كَانَ الْعُرْفُ فِي زَمَنِهِ فِيهِمَا وَفِي زَمَنِهِمَا فِي الْغَنَمِ خَاصَّةٌ وَفِي زَمَانِنَا يُفْتَى عَلَى حَسْبِ الْعَادَةِ كَمَاهُوَ الْمَذْكُوِّرُ فِي الْمُخْتَصَرِ
ترجمہ اور اگر یہ قسم كھائی كہ میں سریاں (سر کا مغز وغیرہ) نہیں کھاؤں گا تو اس کی قسم سے مراد وہ سریاں (مثلاً بکری بھیڑ کا سر وغیرہ) ہوں گی جو تنوروں میں پکائی جاتے ہیں اور شہروں میں فروخت کی جاتے ہیں۔ اور جامع صغیر میں مذکور ہے کہ اگر قسم کھائی کہ میں سر نہیں کھاؤں گا تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ قسم گائے اور بکری دونوں کے سروں پر واقع ہوگی۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ فقط بکری کے سر سے قسم متعلق ہوگی۔ یہ اختلاف اپنے اپنے زمانہ کے اختلاف سے ہے۔ چنانچہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے زمانہ میں گائے اور بکری دونوں کے سروں پر بولا جاتا تھا۔ اور صاحبین رحمہما اللہ کے زمانہ میں فقط بکری کے سروں پر بولا جاتا تھا۔ اور ہمارے زمانہ میں جیسی عادت ہو ویسا ہی فتویٰ دیا جائے گا مختصر قدوری میں یہی مذکور ہے۔ تشریح. الغرض کہ یمین کے باب میں وہ خاص سری مراد ہوگی جو از روئے عرف لوگوں میں کھانے کے طور پر استعمال ہوتی ہے۔ مطلق سری سے کھائے جانے والے یا گوشت والے ہر جانور کی سری مراد نہ ہوگی۔ مثلاً پرندوں کی سری انسان گوشت والا ہے۔ خنزیر کا گوشت بھی یورپ میں كھايا جاتا ہے۔ اگر چہ اسلام میں یہ دونوں گوشت حرام ہیں۔ لیکن كیم ( گوشت والا) ہونے کے باعث ان پر ( انسان حیوان ناطق ہے) جاندار یا جانور و حیوان کا اطلاق ہوتا ہے۔ چنانچہ ان دونوں کی سری وغیرہ ڈلک مراد نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عرف عام میں عادةً یا شاذ و نادر بھی انہیں انسان ، خنزیر) کھایا نہیں جاتا۔ جب عرف میں ان جانوروں کی سری (جن کا گوشت کھایا جاتا ہے) کھانے سے حالف پر حث لازم نہیں آتا۔ جن
Whoever swears 'I will not eat heads', his oath is upon what is baked in ovens and sold in the city and is called 'heads'. In Al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he swears 'I will not eat a head', then according to Abu Hanifa it applies to heads of cattle and sheep, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it applies only to sheep. This is a difference of city and time: the custom in his time included both, and in their time it was specific to sheep. In our time, fatwa is given according to the custom, as mentioned in Al-Mukhtasar. Translation: If someone swears 'I will not eat heads', it refers to heads that are cooked in ovens and sold in cities. In Al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he swears 'I will not eat a head', according to Imam Abu Hanifa the oath applies to heads of cows and sheep. The two companions say it applies only to sheep. This difference is due to the difference of time and place: the custom in Abu Hanifa's time included both, while in the time of the two companions, only sheep heads were common. In our time, fatwa is given according to the prevalent custom, as mentioned in Al-Mukhtasar. Explanation: In oaths, the specific head meant is that which is customarily eaten by people. It does not include heads of all edible or meaty animals. For example, bird heads or pork (though pork is haram in Islam) are not intended because they are not customarily eaten, even if they have meat. Hence, the oath is limited to the heads of animals whose meat is commonly consumed.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths about heads (ru'us). The core issue: which animal heads are covered by an oath not to eat heads? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it includes both cow and sheep heads, based on the custom of his time. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) restrict it to sheep heads only, based on the custom of their time. The reason for disagreement is explicitly stated: the custom (urf) changed over time. The author then generalizes: in any era, fatwa is given according to the custom of that time and place. The commentary further clarifies that not all edible heads are included; only those commonly eaten in the locality. For example, bird heads or pig heads (even if theoretically edible) are excluded because they are not customarily consumed. This ruling underscores the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh, where custom and social practice can determine the scope of legal obligations.

Key Terms
رؤس (ru'ūs): Heads of animals; in oaths, it refers to heads commonly eaten in the locality.
عرف (urf): Custom, which is the basis for interpreting the oath's scope and resolving the dispute.
اختلاف مصر وزمان (ikhtilāf miṣr wa zamān): Difference due to location and time; the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn is attributed to differing customs of their respective eras.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions differ on which heads are included in an oath?
A) They disagreed on the definition of 'head'.
B) The custom in their times was different: Abu Hanifa's time had both cow and sheep heads, while the companions' time had only sheep.
C) They differed on the method of cooking.
D) Abu Hanifa considered all meat heads, but the companions restricted to sheep.

Key Takeaway
The scope of an oath on heads is determined by the local custom, not by the literal meaning of 'head'.

Oath on Roasted and Cooked Food: Custom-Based Ruling
Ruling on oaths regarding roasted and cooked food based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi custom-based interpretation to oaths involving 'roasted' and 'cooked' food and determine when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) determines the default meaning of food terms in oaths, unless specific intention is voiced.

This lesson examines how the Hanafi school interprets terms like 'roasted' and 'cooked' in oaths. Rather than applying a literal general meaning, the school relies on common custom to avoid undue hardship. You will learn the default rules and the effect of specific intention, as well as the ruling on eating broth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath terms
Note the specific words used: 'mashwi' (roasted) and 'tabikh' (cooked). The ruling focuses on their default meanings in common usage.
In Hanafi oaths, words are interpreted according to custom unless a specific intention is expressed.
2
Determine the default meaning by custom
For 'roasted', the default is roasted meat. For 'cooked', it is meat cooked in water. This is based on 'urf and istihsan.
The default is not absolute; the swearer may override it by explicit intention.
3
Apply the intention exception
If the swearer specifically intended something else (e.g., roasted eggs), that intention is accepted, and the oath applies accordingly.
This exception is allowed because it imposes a stricter (more limiting) interpretation on the swearer.
4
Analyze broth consumption
Eating the broth of cooked meat breaks the oath because the broth contains meat particles and is customarily considered part of the cooked dish.
The reasoning: the broth is called 'tabikh' and includes meat essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الْمَشْوِيَّ فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى اللَّحْمِ فِي الْإِطْلَاقِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَنْوِيَ مَا يُشْوَى مِنْ بَيْضٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهِ لِمَكَانِ الْحَقِيقَةِ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الطَّبِيحَ فَهُوَ عَلَى مَا يُطْبَخُ مِنَ اللَّحْمِ وَهَذَا إِسْتِحْسَانُ اِعْتِبَارًا لِلْعُرْفِ وَهَذَا لِاَنَّ التَّعْمِيمَ مُتَعَدَّرٌ فَيُصْرَفُ إِلَى خَاصٍ هُوَ مُتَعَارَفٌ وَهُوَ اللَّحْمُ الْمَطْبُوحُ بِالْمَاءِ إِلَّا إِذَا نَوَى غَيْرَ ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ فِيهِ تَشْدِيدًا وَإِنْ أَكَلَ مِنْ مَرَقِهِ يَحْنِتُ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ اللَّحْمِ وَلِأَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى طَبِيحًا
And if he swears not to eat roasted meat, his oath applies to meat in absolute terms, unless he intends something roasted like eggs or other things due to the literal meaning. And if he swears not to eat cooked food, it applies to what is cooked of meat. This is istihsan taking into account custom. This is because generalization is difficult, so it is directed to a specific that is customary, which is meat cooked with water, unless he intends other than that, because in that there is a stricter consequence. And if he eats from its broth, he breaks his oath because of the parts of meat in it, and because it is called cooked food.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on oaths concerning 'roasted' (مشوي) and 'cooked' (طبيح) food. The Hanafi school holds that in such oaths, the default meaning is determined by common custom ('urf). For 'roasted', the default is roasted meat, but if the swearer specifically intends other roasted items like eggs, his intention is accepted due to the literal meaning. For 'cooked', the term is restricted to meat cooked in water, as this is the most common understanding. However, if he explicitly intends something else, that is accepted because it imposes a stricter obligation on himself. Additionally, eating the broth of such meat breaks the oath because it contains meat particles and is commonly referred to as 'cooked food'. The underlying proof is istihsan (juristic preference) based on the difficulty of generalization and reliance on custom.

Key Terms
مشوي (mashwi): In this context, 'roasted' default refers to roasted meat.
طبيح (tabikh): ‘Cooked food’ here is customarily understood as meat cooked with water.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departure from strict qiyas in favor of custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat 'cooked food' (طبيح) and then eats cooked chicken, does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because chicken is meat.
B) No, because the custom is that 'cooked food' refers to meat cooked in water, and chicken is often cooked differently.
C) Yes, if he intended chicken.
D) No, because 'cooked food' includes all cooked items.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, custom defines the default meaning of food terms, but specific intention overrides it; eating broth also breaks the oath.

Oath on Not Eating Heads: The Role of Custom in Determining Scope
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath scope resolved by custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on which animal heads are included in an oath not to eat heads, and apply the rule that fatwa follows the prevailing custom.

Core Idea
In oaths, the meaning of ambiguous terms is determined by custom, which may change over time and place.

When someone swears not to eat 'heads,' what does that include? The answer depends on custom. In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir. Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differed based on the customs of their respective times. The passage ultimately rules that fatwa is given according to the custom of the swearer's time and place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the ambiguous term
The oath is 'I will not eat heads' (الرؤس). The dispute is whether this includes heads of cows and sheep or only sheep.
The term 'heads' is ambiguous; custom clarifies it.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa held that the oath applies to heads of both cows and sheep. This was based on the custom of his time, where both were commonly baked and sold.
Al-Jami' al-Saghir records this as his view.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restricted the oath to sheep heads only, reflecting the custom of their era.
They differed because custom in their time focused on sheep.
4
Understand the rationale for the difference
The difference is not arbitrary but stems from a change in custom ('urf) between the time of Abu Hanifa and that of his disciples. This is an example of how custom influences legal interpretation.
The text explicitly says: 'this is a difference of city and time.'
5
Apply the final ruling: fatwa follows custom
The passage concludes that in later times (and by extension, today), fatwa is given according to the prevailing custom, as stated in al-Mukhtasar. Thus, one must determine the current custom of the locality.
No fixed opinion; it depends on what is commonly eaten and sold as 'heads'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الرُّؤْسَ فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَا يُكْبَسُ فِي التَّنَانِيرِ وَيُبَاعُ فِي الْمِصْرِ وَيُقَالُ يَكْنِسُ وَفِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ رَأْسًا فَهُوَ عَلَى رُؤْسِ الْبَقَرِ وَالْغَنَمِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيْفَةَ وَقَالَ أَبُوْ يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ عَلَى الْغَنَمِ خَاصَّةً وَهَذَا اخْتِلَافُ مِصْرٍ وَزَمَانِ كَانَ الْعُرْفُ فِي زَمَنِهِ فِيهِمَا وَفِي زَمَنِهِمَا فِي الْغَنَمِ خَاصَّةً وَفِي زَمَانِنَا يُفْتَى عَلَى حَسْبِ الْعَادَةِ كَمَا هُوَ الْمَذْكُورُ فِي الْمُخْتَصَرِ
Whoever swears not to eat heads, his oath applies to what is baked in ovens and sold in the city, and it is said 'yuknas'. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he swears not to eat a head, then it applies to heads of cows and sheep according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said it applies to sheep specifically. And this is a difference of city and time: the custom in his time was for both, and in their time for sheep specifically. And in our time, fatwa is given according to custom, as mentioned in al-Mukhtasar.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of the word 'heads' (الرؤس) in oaths. The ruling is based on the custom of the place and time. Abu Hanifa (رحمه الله) held that the oath applies to heads of both cows and sheep because in his era, both were commonly eaten and sold. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) restricted it to sheep heads, as that was the custom in their time. The text notes that in the compiler's time, fatwa is given according to the prevailing custom, as mentioned in al-Mukhtasar. This illustrates a key principle: in oaths, the common custom ('urf) determines the meaning, and since custom can change, the ruling may change accordingly.

Key Terms
الرؤس (al-ru'us): Heads of animals that are commonly eaten, specifically cows and/or sheep depending on custom.
جامع صغير (al-Jami' al-Saghir): One of the six core books of Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani containing zahir al-riwayah rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only cows' heads
B) Only sheep heads
C) Both cows' and sheep heads
D) Also camel heads

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the scope of an ambiguous term in an oath is determined by the prevailing custom ('urf) of the time and place, not by a fixed definition.

Defining Fruit in Oaths: The Hanafi Intra-Scholarly Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the definition of fruit in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on what constitutes 'fruit' in oaths, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the custom-based definition to new cases.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'fruit' is defined by custom ('urf), not by botanical classification.

When someone swears not to eat 'fruit,' what items are included? The Hanafi school does not rely on a fixed list but on what is customarily enjoyed as a treat. This leads to a known dispute between the founding imāms: Abu Hanifa excludes certain items that his students include. Understanding this dispute reveals how custom shapes legal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling consequences
The act is swearing not to eat fruit (fakihah). The ruling depends on what one eats. If it is considered 'fruit,' the oath is broken (hanatha) and kaffarah becomes wajib. If not, the oath remains intact.
The consequence is binary: breaking the oath requires expiation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's list and his basis
Abu Hanifa holds that grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins are not fruits. Apples, watermelon, and apricots are fruits. His basis is the principle that fruit is what is enjoyed as a treat beyond the ordinary meal, and these items are not customarily so.
Note that cucumbers and gherkins are not sweet; they are more like vegetables.
3
State the Sahibayn's list and their basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates are also fruits. They likely consider these items customarily enjoyed as treats in their time/region.
They agree with Abu Hanifa on apples, watermelon, apricots, and also on cucumbers/gherkins.
4
Identify the shared principle (al-asl)
Both parties agree on the general definition: fruit is what is customarily enjoyed (yutafakkahu bihi) before or after a meal, as a delicacy beyond the ordinary. The dispute is about which items satisfy this definition in a given custom.
The principle is 'al-ism li ma yutafakkahu bihi' — the name applies to what is enjoyed as fruit.
5
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider strawberries. If in a particular locality strawberries are routinely eaten as a dessert treat, they would be fruit. If they are rare or not considered a treat, they would not. The Hanafi ruling depends on 'urf.
This shows the dynamic nature of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ فَاكِهَةً فَأَكَلَ عِنَبًا أَوْ رُمَّانًا أَوْ رُطَبًا أَوْ قِثَّاءً أَوْ خِيَارًا لَمْ يَحْنَثُ وَإِنْ أَكَلَ تُفَّاحًا أَوْ بِطِّيخًا أَوْ مِشْمِشًا حَنِثَ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ حَنِثَ فِي الْعِنَبِ وَالرُّطَبِ وَالرُّمَّانِ أَيْضًا وَالْأَصْلُ أَنَّ الْفَاكِهَةَ اسْمٌ لِمَا يُتَفَكَّهُ بِهِ قَبْلَ الطَّعَامِ وَبَعْدَهُ أَيْ يُتَنَعَّمُ بِهِ زِيَادَةً عَلَى الْمُعْتَادِ وَالرُّطَبُ وَالْيَابِسُ فِيهِ سَوَاءٌ بَعْدَ أَنْ يَكُونَ التَّفَكُّهُ بِهِ مُعْتَادًا حَتَّى لَا يَحْنَثَ بِيَابِسِ الْبُرِّيِّ وَهَذَا الْمَعْنَى مَوْجُودٌ فِي التُّفَّاحِ
He said: And if he swears not to eat fruit, then he eats grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, or gherkins, he does not break his oath. And if he eats apples, watermelon, or apricots, he breaks his oath. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said he breaks his oath also with grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates. The principle is that 'fruit' (fakihah) is a name for what is enjoyed before or after a meal, i.e., what is savored as an extra beyond the ordinary. Fresh and dry are the same in this regard, provided that enjoying it is customary, such that he does not break his oath with dry barley. And this meaning is present in apples.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition of 'fruit' (فاكهة) in the context of oaths. According to Abu Hanifa (رحمه الله), certain items like grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins are not considered 'fruit' in the technical sense relevant to oaths, likely because they are more like staples or are not typically eaten as a treat. Therefore, eating them does not break the oath. However, apples, watermelon, and apricots are considered fruits. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) disagree, holding that grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates are also fruits. The underlying principle is that 'fruit' refers to items customarily enjoyed as a delicacy beyond the ordinary meal, whether fresh or dry, as long as it is customary to enjoy them; thus, dry barley is not included. This dispute again shows the reliance on custom ('urf) in determining the meaning of words in oaths.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fakihah): Items customarily eaten for enjoyment before or after a meal, as a treat beyond staple food.
تفكه (tafakkuh): The act of enjoying fruit as a delicacy, the basis for classifying something as 'fruit'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, which of the following breaks an oath not to eat fruit?
A) Grapes
B) Watermelon
C) Cucumbers
D) Fresh dates

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fruit in oaths is custom-based; the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is over which items satisfy that custom, not over the principle itself.

Oath Regarding Fakiha: Definition and Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on definition of fakiha for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates in an oath on fakiha, and apply the custom-based tarjih to resolve the dispute.

Core Idea
Custom determines whether an item is 'fakiha' for oaths; purely nutritional use limits tafakkuh.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute about the definition of 'fruit' in the context of oaths. When someone swears not to eat fruit, does eating grapes, fresh dates, or pomegranates break the oath? Abu Hanifa and his two companions (the Sahibayn) differ, basing their arguments on the meaning of tafakkuh (eating for luxury). The preferred view relies on local custom ('urf), a key Hanafi tool.

Guided Path
1
Define the key term
Understand what 'fakiha' means in this context: any item eaten for tafakkuh (pleasure beyond sustenance), not as a primary meal.
The definition hinges on custom and intended use.
2
State the positions
Abu Hanifa: grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates do not break the oath. Sahibayn: they do break it.
Both positions are within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa argues these items are used as food/medicine, limiting tafakkuh. Sahibayn argue they are the choicest fruits, maximizing pleasure.
The dispute is about the presence or absence of pure tafakkuh.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: a person who eats grapes after swearing not to eat fruit either breaks or does not break his oath depending on whose view is followed.
This directly affects whether kaffarah is due.
5
Apply the tarjih
The author favors the custom-based view from al-Muhit: whatever local custom treats as fruit for luxury is included; otherwise not.
Custom overrides a fixed list, reflecting Hanafi flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم . وَأَخَوَاتِهِ فَيَحْنَثُ بِهَا وَغَيْرُ مَوْجُوْدٍ فِي الْقِثَّاءِ وَالْخِيَارِ لِأَنَّهُمَا مِنَ الْبُقُولِ بَيْعًا وَأَكْلًا فَلَا يَحْنَثُ بِهِمَا وَ الْعِنَبُ وَالرُّطَب وَالرُّمَّانُ فَهُمَا يَقُولَان اَنَّ مَعْنَى التَّفَكُّهِ مَوْجُودٌ فِيْهَا فَإِنَّهَا اَعَزُّ الْفَوَاكِهِ وَالتَّنَعمَ بهَا يَفُوق التنعم بِغَيْرِهَا وَأَبُو حَنِيفَةٌ يَقُولُ إِنَّ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءَ مِمَّا يَتَغَذَّى بِهَا وَيُتَدَاوَى بِهَا فَوَجَبَ قُصُورٌ فِي مَعْنَى التَّفَكُّهِ لِلِاسْتِعْمَالِ فِي حَاجَةِ الْبَقَاءِ وَلِهَذَا كَانَ الْيَابِسُ مِنْهَا مِنَ التَّوَابِلِ أَوْ مِنَ الْأَقْوَاتِ ترجمہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں فاکھہ نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر اس نے انگور یا انار یا تاز و خرما یا لکڑی یا کھیرا کھایا تو حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ اور اگر سیب یا خربوزہ یا کشمش ( خوبانی، زرد آلو کھائی تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کا قول ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ انگور و تازه خرما اور انار کھانے سے بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اس مسئلہ کی اصل یہ ہے کہ فاکہ ایسی چیز کو کہتے ہیں جس کے ساتھ تفلکہ کیا جائے یعنی اصلی اور معمول کے غذا سے زیادہ بطور عیش یعنی زیادتی مزہ کے لئے کھایا جائے خواہ کھانے سے پہلے ہو یا کھانے کے بعد ہو اور جس چیز کے ساتھ ایسی عیش کی عادت جاری ہو تو اس کا خشک و تازہ ہونا برابر ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ خشک خربوزہ سے حانث نہیں ہوتا ہے یعنی خشک خربوزہ کہیں استعمال نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اب چونکہ تشکہ کے یہ معنی سیب اور اس کی مانند چیزوں میں موجود ہیں تو ایسی چیزوں کے کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا اور لکڑی اور کھیرا میں موجود نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ چیزیں تو سبزی و ساگ کے طور پر بیچی اور کھائی جاتی ہیں۔ اس لئے ان کے کھانے سے حانث نہیں ہو و گا۔ لیکن انارو تازہ کھجور اور انگور کے بارے میں صاحبین رحمہما اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ تفکہ کے معنی ان میں بھی موجود ہیں۔ کیونکہ دوسرے فواکہہ میں یہ زیادہ عمدہ ہیں۔ اور دوسری چیزوں کے مقابلہ میں ان میں لذت کے معنی زیادہ پائے جاتے ہیں اور امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ چیزیں بطور غذا اور بطور دوا کے استعمال کی جاتی ہیں۔ اس لئے ان میں صرف لذت کے معنی میں کمی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ ان کا استعمال انسانی زندگی کی ضرورت سے بھی ہوا اور اسی وجہ سے ان میں سے جو خشک ہو جائیں وہ مصالحہ کے طور پر یا قوت کے طور پر یعنی غلہ کے طور پر استعمال ہوتے ہیں۔ فائدہ محیط میں کہا ہے کہ عرف میں جو چیز عاد تا تفکہ کے طور پر کھائی جاتی ہو وہی فواکہہ میں شامل ہے ورنہ نہیں اور یہی قول بہتر ہے۔
And its sisters (i.e., similar fruits), so he breaks the oath by them. And (the case of) cucumber and cucumber is not found (in the meaning of tafakkuh) because they are from the vegetables, sold and eaten as such, so he does not break the oath by them. Grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates: they (the two companions) say that the meaning of tafakkuh is present in them, for they are the noblest fruits, and enjoyment with them surpasses enjoyment with others. Abu Hanifa says that these things are eaten for nourishment and used as medicine, so there is a shortcoming in the meaning of tafakkuh due to their use for the necessities of survival, and for this reason their dried forms are among condiments or grains. Translation: Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If someone swears 'I will not eat fruit' then eats grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, wood-cucumber, or cucumber, he does not break his oath. But if he eats apples, melon, or dried apricots, he breaks his oath. And this is the saying of Abu Hanifa. The two companions said that he also breaks his oath by eating grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates. The basis of this issue is that 'fruit' is that which is eaten for tafakkuh, i.e., as a luxury beyond the main sustenance, whether before or after the meal. For things with which this luxury habit is practiced, their dry and fresh states are equal. Even dry melon does not break the oath because it is not used. Now since this meaning of tafakkuh exists in apples and similar things, eating them breaks the oath, while it does not exist in wood-cucumber and cucumber because they are sold and eaten as vegetables. But regarding pomegranates, fresh dates, and grapes, the two companions say that the meaning of tafakkuh exists in them as they are the best of fruits and surpass others in pleasure. Abu Hanifa says that these things are used as food and medicine, so the element of pleasure is diminished because they are used for survival needs; hence their dried forms become condiments or grains. Benefit: In al-Muhit it is said that whatever is customarily eaten for tafakkuh is included in fruits, otherwise not, and this is the better view.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the definition of 'fakiha' (fruit) in the context of oaths. The core ruling comes from Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir: if one swears not to eat fruit (fakiha), eating items like apples or melon breaks the oath, but eating cucumbers, wood-cucumber, grapes, fresh dates, or pomegranates does not, according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree regarding the latter three, considering them fruits. The disagreement hinges on the meaning of 'tafakkuh'—eating for pleasure rather than sustenance. Abu Hanifa argues that grapes, dates, and pomegranates can be used as food or medicine, so they are not purely for tafakkuh. The Sahibayn consider them the choicest fruits. The author then mentions that the preferred view (as in al-Muhit) is to rely on local custom ('urf): whatever is customarily eaten as a luxury fruit is included, otherwise not.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākiha): In this context, any item eaten for tafakkuh (pleasure beyond sustenance), as determined by custom.
تفكه (tafakkuh): Eating for enjoyment and luxury, not as a primary meal or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person swears not to eat 'fakiha' and then eats grapes, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath.
B) He does not break his oath.
C) It depends on whether the grapes are fresh or dried.
D) He must make an exception.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is decisive in defining 'fakiha' for oaths; Abu Hanifa's position excludes items used for sustenance, while the Sahibayn include them, and the preferred view follows custom.

The Definition of Idam in an Oath: Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of idam in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi definitions of idam, distinguish their proofs, and apply the ruling to determine if an oath is broken, including the effect of intention.

Core Idea
Idam is a subordinate to bread — but by mixing or custom? The definition changes the oath's consequence.

When someone swears not to eat idam, what counts as idam? The Hanafi school has two positions: the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) restrict idam to items eaten solely as a subordinate (tabi'), while Imam Muhammad includes anything commonly eaten with bread. This dispute rests on the derivation from muwadamah (agreement). In this lesson, you will unpack the arguments and see how intention (niyyah) can affect the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario and the disputed term
The oath is 'I will not eat idam'. The dispute is over the definition of 'idam'.
2
State the position of the two shaykhs
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: idam is something eaten as a subordinate (tabi'), either actually mixed (like salt dissolving) or legally not eaten alone (like vinegar). Items like roasted meat are eaten alone, so not idam.
This is the apparent riwayah (zahir al-riwayah).
3
State the position of Imam Muhammad
Muhammad: idam is anything commonly eaten with bread, such as meat or eggs, based on customary agreement (muwadamah).
Also a narration from Abu Yusuf and the view of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad.
4
Compare the proofs
Both use the etymology from muwadamah. The two shaykhs emphasize subordination through mixing or inability to be eaten alone. Muhammad emphasizes customary usage (urf).
5
Apply the intention exception
If the swearer intended a specific item (like meat) to be included as idam, that intention is respected because it increases strictness (tashdid).
This is a concession to the stricter view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْتَدِمُ فَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ اِضْطُبِعَ بِهِ إِدَامٌ وَالشَّوَاءُ لَيْسَ بِإِدَامٍ وَالْمِلْحُ إِدَامٌ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ كُلُّ مَا يُؤْكَلُ مَعَ الْخُبْزِ غَالِبًا فَهُوَ إِدَامٌ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ لِأَنَّ الْإِدَامَ مِنَ الْمُوَادَمَةِ وَهِيَ الْمُوَافَقَةُ وَكُلُّ مَا يُؤْكَلُ مَعَ الْخُبْزِ مُوَافِقٌ لَهُ كَاللَّحْمِ وَالْبَيْضِ وَنَحْوِهِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ الْإِدَامَ مَا يُؤْكَلُ تَبَعًا وَالتَّبَعِيَّةُ فِي الِاخْتِلَاطِ حَقِيقَةٌ لِيَكُونَ قَائِمًا بِهِ وَفِي أَنْ لَا يُؤْكَلَ عَلَى الْإِنْفِرَادِ حُكْمًا وَتَمَامُ الْمُوَافَقَةِ فِي الْأَمْتِزَاجِ أَيْضًا وَ الْخَلُّ وَغَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْمَائِعَاتِ لَا يُؤْكَلُ وَحْدَهَا بَلْ يُشْرَبُ وَالْمِلْحُ لَا يُؤْكَلُ بِالْفِرَادِهِ عَادَةً وَلِأَنَّهُ يَدُوبُ فَيَكُونُ تَبَعًا بِخِلَافِ اللَّحْمِ وَمَا يُضَاهِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ يُؤْكَلُ وَحْدَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُنْوِيَهُ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ التَّشْدِيدِ وَالْعِنَبُ وَالْبِطِّيْخُ لَيْسَ بِإِدَامٍ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ ترجمہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے کہ۔ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں ادام (سالن) کے ساتھ نہیں کھاؤں گا۔ تو ہر وہ چیز جو روٹی کے ساتھ پکا کر کھائی جائے وہ ادام ہے۔ اس بناء پر بھونا ہوا گوشت ادام نہیں ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ یہ تنہا بغیر روٹی کے کھایا جاتا ہے ) اور نمک ادام ہوا ( کیونکہ اسے روٹی سے لگا کر کھایا جاتا ہے) یہ قول امام ابوحنیفہ وابو یوسف کا ظاہر الروایہ ہے۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ ہر وہ چیز جو عموما روٹی کے ساتھ کھائی جاتی ہے۔ وہی ادام ہے اور امام ابو یوسف سے بھی یہ ایک روایت ہے اور امام شافعی واحمد رحمہما اللہ کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔ کیونکہ ادام کا لفظ موادمت سے مشتق ہے جس کے معنی موافقت کے ہیں اور جو چیز روٹی کے ساتھ کھائی جائے وہ اس کے موافق ہے۔ جیسے گوشت انڈا وغیرہ اور اس جیسی دوسری چیز یں اور شیخین یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ و امام ابو یوسف رحمہما اللہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ادام عرف میں ایسی چیز کو کہتے ہیں جو روٹی کے تابع کر کے کھائی جائے اور تابع ہونا ایک تو حقیقتا ملانے میں ہے تا کہ ادام اسی کے ساتھ پایا جائے اور دوسری اس طرح کہ حکما تابع ہو یعنی وہ تنہا نہ کھائی جائے۔ لیکن موافقت تو پوری اسی صورت میں ہوگی جب کہ دونوں ایک دوسرے میں ملائی جائیں اور سر کہ وغیرہ پتلی چیز میں اکیلی نہیں کھائی جاتی ہیں بلکہ وہ پی لی جاتی ہیں اور نمک بھی عادتنا تنہا نہیں کھایا جاتا ہے اور اس لئے بھی کہ یہ پکھل کر تابع ہو جاتا ہے۔ بخلاف گوشت اور اس جیسی چیزوں کے کہ یہ تنہا بھی کھائی جاتی ہیں۔ لیکن اگر قسم کھاتے وقت ادام کے لفظ سے گوشت وغیرہ کی بھی نیت کر لی ہو تو اس کی نیت کے موافق یہ چیزیں بھی قسم میں داخل رہیں گی ۔ کیونکہ ایسی نیت بیان کرنے میں وہ خود اپنے اوپر ختی بڑھتا ہے۔ (یعنی آسانی نہیں چاہتا ہے۔ اور جب قسم کھانے والا ایسی نیت بیان کرے جس سے اس پر ختی بڑھتی ہو تو قاضی بھی اس کی بات کی تصدیق کرتا ہے۔ اور انگور اور خربوزہ ادام نہیں ہیں ۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔
He said: And if he swore he would not eat idam, then everything that is (dipped/used) with it is idam. Roasted meat is not idam, and salt is idam. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Everything that is commonly eaten with bread is idam, and this is a narration from Abu Yusuf, because idam is derived from muwadamah, which is agreement, and everything eaten with bread is in agreement with it, like meat, eggs, and the like. Their (the two shaykhs') argument is that idam is what is eaten as a subordinate; subordination is in mixing in reality so that it is sustained by it, and in that it is not eaten alone, effectively. Complete agreement is also in mixing. Vinegar and other liquids are not eaten alone but drunk, and salt is not eaten alone customarily, and because it dissolves and becomes a subordinate, unlike meat and its equivalents, because they are eaten alone. Unless he intends it (meat) for the sake of strictness. Grapes and melon are not idam; this is the correct view. Translation: Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If someone swore 'I will not eat with idam' then every thing that is cooked with bread is idam. On this basis, roasted meat is not idam because it is eaten alone. Salt is idam because it is eaten smeared on bread. This is the apparent riwayah of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad said: Everything customarily eaten with bread is idam, and this is also a narration from Abu Yusuf, and it is the view of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. Because idam is derived from muwadamah meaning agreement, and whatever is eaten with bread is in agreement with it, like meat, eggs, etc. The two shaykhs' proof is that idam is what is eaten as a subordinate to bread: subordination is either by actually mixing so that it exists with it, or legally, so that it is not eaten alone. Complete agreement is when both are mixed. Vinegar and other liquids are not eaten alone but drunk, and salt is not eaten alone traditionally, and it dissolves to become subordinate. Unlike meat and the like because they are eaten alone. However, if at the time of swearing he intended meat, then his intention is followed because it increases strictness. And grapes and melon are not idam; this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of 'idam' (relish/side dish) in an oath not to eat with idam. The primary ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir: everything that is 'used with' bread is idam? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, idam is something that is eaten as a subordinate ('tabi') to bread—either mixed with it (like salt dissolved in bread) or not eaten alone (like salt, vinegar). Roasted meat is not idam because it can be eaten alone. Imam Muhammad defines idam more broadly as anything commonly eaten with bread, including meat and eggs. This view is also attributed to Abu Yusuf in a different narration. The two sides differ on the nature of subordination (tab'iyyah): the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) insist on actual mixing or inability to eat alone, while Muhammad focuses on general usage. The text also notes that if the swearer has a specific intention (like including meat), that intention is respected if it increases strictness. The correct view according to the author is that grapes and melon are not idam.

Key Terms
إدام (idām): In this context, a side dish eaten with bread as a subordinate (tabi'), not eaten alone.
موادمة (muwādama): Agreement or harmony; the root from which idam is derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent riwayah of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, which of the following is considered idam?
A) Roasted meat
B) Salt
C) Eggs
D) Grapes

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, idam is strictly defined as a subordinate to bread, not simply any accompaniment; but personal intention can override the default definition for greater strictness.

Oath Regarding Meals: Defining Ghada', Asha', and Suhur
Defines meal times and satiety condition for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of ghada', asha', and suhur in the context of oaths, and identify when an oath regarding these meals is considered broken.

Core Idea
An oath about eating a meal is broken only if the meal is a full meal (more than half-saturation) within the defined time.

When someone swears not to have a meal, it is crucial to know what constitutes that meal in Islamic law. The Hanafi school defines specific time periods and a condition of satiety for terms like ghada', asha', and suhur. This lesson breaks down these definitions and explains when an oath is considered broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meal terms
Recognize ghada', asha', and suhur as the three meal types in the context of oaths. Each has a defined time window.
These terms are specific to the Hanafi school in this legal context.
2
Note the time periods
Ghada': from dawn to noon; Asha': from noon to midnight; Suhur: from midnight to dawn. These are based on linguistic origins and hadith.
The time for asha' includes the period of zuhr prayer, which is called 'one of the two asha prayers' in hadith.
3
Understand the satiety condition
A meal must be intended for satiety and must exceed half-saturation. A small snack does not break the oath.
Custom ('urf) of the local people determines what constitutes a full meal.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a scenario, determine if the meal meets both the time and satiety conditions to break the oath.
This is a parsing exercise: the student must apply the definition to a concrete case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَتَغَدَّى فَالْغَدَاءُ مَا أُكِلَ مِنْ طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ إِلَى الظُّهْرِ وَ الْعِشَاءُ مِنْ صَلَاةِ الظُّهْرِ إِلَى نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ لِأَنَّ مَا بَعْدَ الزَّوَالِ يُسَمَّى عِشَاء وَلِهَذَا يُسَمَّى الظُّهْرُ إِحْدَى صَلَاتَيْ الْعِشَاءِ فِي الْحَدِيْثِ وَالسُّحُوْرُ مِنْ نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ إِلَى طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ لِأَنَّهُ مَاخُوذٌ مِنَ السَّحْرِ وَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى مَا يَقْرُبُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ الْغَدَاءُ وَالْعِشَاءُ مَا يُقْصَدُ بِهِ الشِّبَعُ عَادَةً وَيُعْتَبَرُ عَادَةُ أَهْلِ كُلِّ بَلْدَةٍ فِي حَقِّهِمْ وَيُشْتَرَطُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْ نِصْفِ الشِّبَعِ ترجمه اگر کسی نے عربی میں یہ قسم کھائی لا اتغدی ( میں ناشتہ نہیں کروں گا) میں غدار نہیں کھاؤں گا غذاء اس کھانے کو کہا جاتا ہے جو آفتاب نکلنے کے بعد سے ظہر تک ہو۔ اور عشاء اس کھانے کو کہا جاتا ہے جو ظہر کی نماز کے بعد سے آدھی رات ہو جانے تک کھایا جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ زوال کے بعد کے وقت کو عشاء کہا جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے حدیث میں ظہر کی نماز کو عشاء کی دو نمازوں میں سے ایک نماز کہا گیا ہے۔ اور سحری کا کھانا آدھی رات سے طلوع فجر تک ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ لفظ سحر سے ماخوذ ہے۔ اور قریب سحر تک جو کھانا ہو اسے سحری کہتے ہیں۔ یعنی سحر کے اندر نہیں کھایا جاتا ہے۔ پھر غداء اور عشاء اس کھانے کو کہا جاتا ہے جس سے ہر شخص کو اس کی عادت کے مطابق سیری مقصود ہو ۔ یعنی اگر کسی نے صبح کے وقت ایک دو نوالے منہ میں رکھ لئے تو اسے غداء نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ پھر ہر ایک شہر والوں کے حق میں انہیں کی عادت کے مطابق معتبر ہے۔ اس کے لئے شرط یہ ہے کہ اتنا کھالے جس سے پیٹ کو آدھی سے زیادہ سیری حاصل ہو جائے۔
And if he swore he would not have breakfast (ghada'), then ghada' is what is eaten from dawn to noon; asha' is from noon prayer to midnight, because the time after noon is called 'asha', and for this reason zuhr is called one of the two 'asha prayers in the hadith. Suhur is from midnight to dawn, because it is derived from sahr (pre-dawn) and applies to what is close to it. Then ghada' and asha' are that which is intended for satiety customarily, and the custom of the people of each town is considered in their regard, and it is required that it be more than half of satiety. Translation: If someone makes an oath in Arabic 'I will not have ghada'' (breakfast), then ghada' is the meal eaten from sunrise to noon. Asha' is the meal eaten from noon prayer to midnight. Because the time after midday is called 'asha', therefore in a hadith, zuhr prayer is called one of the two 'asha prayers. Suhur (pre-dawn meal) is from midnight to dawn, as it is derived from sahr (pre-dawn) and applies to what is close to it. Then ghada' and asha' refer to meals that are customarily eaten to the point of satiety. So if someone eats only a bite or two in the morning, that is not called ghada'. The custom of each city's people is considered. The condition is that he eats enough to reach more than half of satiety.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'ghada'' (breakfast), 'asha' (supper/dinner), and 'suhur' (pre-dawn meal) in the context of oaths. The times are: ghada' from dawn to noon; asha' from noon to midnight; suhur from midnight to dawn. The ruling states that simply eating a little does not break the oath; the meal must be intended to satiate and must exceed half-saturation, and local custom ('urf) determines what constitutes a full meal. The passage then (in the OCR but not included in this segment) goes on to discuss general oaths about eating, drinking, and wearing, and the rule that if someone later claims a specific intention, it is not accepted by the qadi or in matters of conscience (diyanatan).

Key Terms
غداء (ghadā'): A meal from dawn to noon intended for satiety, more than half-full.
عشاء (‘ashā'): A meal from noon to midnight intended for satiety, more than half-full.
سحور (saḥūr): Pre-dawn meal from midnight to dawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to have 'asha' and eats a full dinner at 1 PM, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because 1 PM is after noon and before midnight.
B) No, because asha' is only eaten after maghrib.
C) Yes, because any meal after noon is asha'.
D) No, because the meal is not intended for satiety.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of meal terms for oaths includes both strict time windows and a condition of more than half-satiety, based on linguistic origins and custom.

Defining Idam in Oaths: The Hanafi Position and the Dispute
Defines idam in oaths and presents the internal Hanafi and cross-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi relied-upon definition of idam in oaths and explain the practical consequence of the dispute with Imam Muhammad and other schools.

Core Idea
Idam is only what is eaten as a subordinate to bread — custom defines it, not mere accompaniment.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies a critical subtlety in oaths about food. When someone swears not to eat idam, does that include meat? The answer depends on how we define 'idam'. The Hanafi school distinguishes itself from other schools and even has an internal difference. We'll examine the proofs and see why the view of Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue and the two positions
The issue is what counts as idam (condiment) in an oath about eating with bread. One position (Imam Muhammad, Shafi'i, Hanbali) says anything eaten with bread is idam. The other (Shaykhayn) says only foods not eaten alone are idam.
This dispute affects whether an oath is broken by eating meat with bread.
2
Examine the proof of the opposing view
The opposing view argues that idam is derived from muwada'ah (conformity), so any food conforming to bread by being eaten with it is idam.
They cite examples like meat and eggs.
3
Analyze the proof of Shaykhayn
Shaykhayn reply that custom ('urf) defines idam as something subordinate to bread, i.e., not eaten alone. Complete conformity requires mixing (like vinegar) or being habitually not eaten alone (like salt). Meat is eaten alone, so it is not subordinate.
They also note that linguistic conformity does not imply subordination in custom.
4
Understand the Hanafi response and the role of custom
The Hanafi response is that custom restricts the literal meaning. The practical rule: if a food is customarily eaten alone, it is not idam. If it is only eaten with bread (like salt), it is idam.
This prevents overly broad interpretations that could cause frequent oath violations.
5
Note the practical consequence and the exception
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Shaykhayn, eating meat alone does not break an oath to avoid idam; under the opposing view, it does. However, if the oath-taker intends a broader meaning, his intention is accepted in court because it increases hardship.
This shows the nuance of oath enforcement in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الاكل والشرب. كھائی جاتی ہے۔ وہی ادام ہے اور امام ابو يوسف سے بھی یہ ایک روایت ہے اور امام شافعی واحمد رحمہما اللہ کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔ کیونکہ ادام کا لفظ موادمت سے مشتق ہے جس کے معنی موافقت کے ہیں اور جو چیز روٹی کے ساتھ کھائی جائے وہ اس کے موافق ہے۔ جیسے گوشت انڈا وغیرہ اور اس جیسی دوسری چیز یں اور شیخین یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ و امام ابو يوسف رحمہا اللہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ادام عرف میں ایسی چیز کو کہتے ہیں جو روٹی کے تابع کر کے کھائی جائے اور تابع ہونا ایک تو حقیقتا ملانے میں ہے تا کہ ادام اسی کے ساتھ پایا جائے اور دوسری اس طرح کہ حکما تابع ہو یعنی وہ تنہا نہ کھائی جائے۔ لیکن موافقت تو پوری اسی صورت میں ہوگی جب کہ دونوں ایک دوسرے میں ملائی جائیں اور سر کہ وغیرہ پتلی چیز میں اکیلی نہیں کھائی جاتی ہیں بلکہ وہ پی لی جاتی ہیں اور نمک بھی عادتنا تنہا نہیں کھایا جاتا ہے اور اس لئے بھی کہ یہ پکھل کر تابع ہو جاتا ہے۔ بخلاف گوشت اور اس جیسی چیزوں کے کہ یہ تنہا بھی کھائی جاتی ہیں۔ لیکن اگر قسم کھاتے وقت ادام کے لفظ سے گوشت وغیرہ کی بھی نیت کر لی ہو تو اس کی نیت کے موافق یہ چیزیں بھی قسم میں داخل رہیں گی ۔ کیونکہ ایسی نیت بیان کرنے میں وہ خود اپنے اوپر ختی بڑھاتا ہے۔ (یعنی آسانی نہیں چاہتا ہے۔ اور جب قسم کھانے والا ایسی نیت بیان کرے جس سے اس پر ختی بڑھتی ہو تو قاضی بھی اس کی بات کی تصدیق کرتا ہے۔ اور انگور اور خربوزہ ادام نہیں ہیں ۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔
Chapter of oath in eating and drinking. ... That which is eaten is idam. And from Imam Abu Yusuf also there is one narration, and Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on them) also hold this opinion. Because the word idam is derived from muwada'ah which means conformity, and what is eaten with bread conforms to it, like meat, egg, and other similar things. And the proof of the two shaykhs, Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them), is that idam in custom refers to something that is eaten as a subordinate to bread. And being subordinate is either by actually mixing it so that idam is found with it, or by being subordinate in ruling, i.e., it is not eaten alone. But complete conformity occurs only when both are mixed together. And vinegar etc. are not eaten alone but drunk, and salt is customarily not eaten alone and it becomes subordinate by being crushed. Unlike meat and similar things which are eaten alone. But if at the time of the oath he intends meat etc. with the word idam, then those things will be included in the oath according to his intention, because in expressing such an intention he increases hardship upon himself. And when the oath-taker expresses an intention that increases hardship, the judge also confirms his statement. And grapes and melon are not idam. This is the correct opinion.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of 'idam' (condiment) in the context of oaths. The text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute and also compares with other schools. According to one view (attributed to Imam Muhammad, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad), any food eaten with bread qualifies as idam, based on the linguistic derivation from 'muwada'ah' (conformity). However, the relied-upon position of the Hanafi school, held by Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, is that idam is specifically something eaten as a subordinate to bread, meaning it cannot be eaten alone. Thus, meat and eggs, which are commonly eaten alone, are not idam, while salt and vinegar, which are not eaten alone, are idam. The text emphasizes that custom ('urf) is the basis for this distinction. An exception is made if the oath-taker explicitly intends a broader meaning; then his intention is accepted in qada (judicial context) because it increases hardship.

Key Terms
ادام (idam): Condiment or side dish eaten with bread; in Shafi'i/Hanbali/Imam Muhammad's view, anything eaten with bread; in Shaykhayn's view, only what is subordinate and not eaten alone.
موادمت (muwada'ah): Linguistic derivation of idam, meaning conformity or accompaniment.
تبع (taba'): Subordination; idam is subordinate to bread, meaning it is not eaten alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following is considered idam in an oath?
A) Meat
B) Eggs
C) Salt
D) Grapes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon position on idam in oaths is defined by custom: it must be a subordinate food not eaten alone, such as salt or vinegar, but not meat or eggs.

Timings and Conditions for Ghada, Asha, and Suhur in Oaths
Defines time windows and satiation condition for meal-related oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the time definitions for ghada, asha, and suhur in Hanafi fiqh and apply the condition of more than half-satiation to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
In oaths about meals, the meal name defines time, but only a full meal (more than half-satiation) breaks the oath.

This passage provides the Hanafi ruling on what constitutes the meals of ghada, asha, and suhur when someone swears an oath regarding them. It not only defines clear time boundaries for each meal but also imposes a substantive condition: the eating must be more than half of satiety. Additionally, the ruling defers to local custom to determine what counts as a satiating meal, ensuring the ruling remains practical across different regions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Understand that the ruling applies when someone swears an oath like "I will not eat ghada/asha/suhur." The oath is conditional on the definition of those meals.
2
Extract time definitions
Note the three time windows: ghada from dawn to zuhr, asha from zuhr to midnight, suhur from midnight to dawn. These are based on linguistic and customary usage.
The hadith about zuhr being called 'one of the two asha prayers' supports the asha time.
3
Understand the satiation condition
The oath is only broken if the food eaten exceeds half of one's full satiation (akthar min nisf al-shab'). A small snack, even within the correct time, does not break it.
This prevents trivial breaches.
4
Apply the role of custom
What counts as a satiating meal varies by locality. The mufti must consider the 'urf (custom) of the people of that city to determine if the food qualifies.
Custom is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh for such determinations.
5
Synthesize the complete ruling
Combine all elements: time window + food amount > half-satiation + according to local custom = oath broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَتَعَلَّى فَالْغَدَاءُ الَّا كُلُّ مِنْ طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ إِلَى الْظُّهْرِ وَ الْعِشَاءِ مِنْ صَلَوَةِ الظُّهْرِ إِلَى نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ لان مَا بَعْدَا الزَّوَالِ يُسَمَّى عِشَاء وَلِهَذَا يُسَمَّى الظُّهْرُ اَحَدَ صَلَاتِي الْعِشَاءِ فِي الْحَدِيْثِ وَالسُّحُوْرِ مِنْ نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ إِلى طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ لِأَنَّهُ مَاخُودٌ مِنَ السَّحْرِ وَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى مَا يَقْرُبُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ الْغَدَاءُ وَالْعِشَاءُ مَا يُقْصَدُ بِهِ السَّبْعُ عَادَةً وَيُعْتَبَرُ عَادَةُ اَهْلِ كُلَّ بَلْدَةٍ فِي حَقِهِمْ وَيُشْتَرَطُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْ نِصْفِ الشَّبْعِ
And if he swears 'I will not eat lunch (ghada),' then lunch is eating from dawn to zuhr. Dinner (asha) is from zuhr prayer to midnight, because what is after zawal is called 'asha, and therefore zuhr is called 'one of the two prayers of asha' in the hadith. Suhur is from midnight to dawn, because it is derived from sahr (pre-dawn) and applies to what is close to it. Then ghada and asha refer to what is intended for satiation by custom, and the custom of the people of each city is considered for them, and it is required that it be more than half-full.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage provides definitions for meal names in oaths. If someone swears not to have 'ghada' (lunch), the time is from dawn to zuhr. 'Asha' (dinner) is from zuhr to midnight; the text cites a hadith where zuhr is called one of the two 'asha prayers' because 'asha time extends after zawal. 'Suhur' (pre-dawn meal) is from midnight to dawn, derived from 'sahr' meaning the last part of night. Importantly, simply eating a small amount does not count as a meal; the oath is only broken if the food is sufficient to achieve more than half of satiation ('akthar min nisf al-shab'). This is determined by the custom of each locality.

Key Terms
غداء (ghada): Lunch; eating from dawn to zuhr.
عشاء (asha): Dinner; eating from zuhr to midnight.
سحور (suhur): Pre-dawn meal; from midnight to dawn.
الشبع (al-shab'): Fullness; requirement is more than half-full.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to eat suhur, what is the latest time they can eat without breaking the oath?
A) Midnight
B) Dawn (fajr)
C) Zuhr
D) Maghrib

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath about a meal is broken only if one eats a customary full meal (more than half-satiation) within the defined time window for that meal.

Intention of Specification in Oaths: General vs Specified Expressions
Ruling on specifying items in a general oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two cases of oath expressions and state whether the swearer's later intention to specify is accepted in court (qada) and religiously (diyanah).

Core Idea
Intention only affects explicitly uttered words; a general oath cannot be restricted later.

When a person swears a general oath without mentioning specific items, can they later claim they only meant certain things? The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction between two formulations: one where no object is mentioned (e.g., 'if I eat') and one where an indefinite noun is used (e.g., 'if I eat food'). This lesson unpacks the ruling and the usul principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath formulations
Read the passage: first case uses absolute 'in labistu aw akaltu aw sharibtu' without specifying what is worn, eaten, or drunk. Second case adds indefinite nouns: 'thawban', 'ta'aman', 'sharaban'.
2
Determine the ruling for the first case
The text says 'lam yudayyan fi al-qada wa ghayrihi' – the specification intention is NOT accepted at all. Explain why: because intention only works on the explicit wording (al-malfuz), and the implied meaning (al-muqtada) has no generality.
The key term is 'al-muqtada' – what is implied but not stated.
3
Determine the ruling for the second case
With nakira in the condition, the expression is general (ya'mum). Intention of specification can take effect, but it contradicts the apparent meaning (khilaf al-zahir). Therefore, it is accepted in diyanah (between the person and Allah) but not in judicial proceedings (qada).
The nakira rule: nakira in a condition gives generality.
4
Trace the usul principle behind the distinction
The underlying rule: intention (niyya) only operates on what is explicitly uttered (malfuz). The muqtada (implied) has no 'umum. Hence, in the first case, the intention to specify is void. In the second case, the wording itself is general, so intention can restrict it, but since it goes against the apparent, it lacks judicial force.
This dalil is based on qiyas and the nature of speech.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider an oath: 'If I enter a house, my slave is free.' Later the swearer says he meant only one specific house. Since 'a house' is nakira in the condition, the intention is accepted religiously but not in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ إِنْ لَبِسْتُ اَوْا كَلْتُ اَوْ شَرِبْتُ فَعَبْدِى حُرِّوَقَالَ عَنَيْتُ شَيْئًا دُونَ شَيْءٍ لَمْ يُدَيَّنُ فِي الْقَضَاءِ وَغَيْرِهِ لانَّ النِّيَّةَ إِنَّمَا تَصِحُ فِى الْمَلْفُوظِ وَالثَّوْبُ وَمَا يُضَاهِيْهِ غَيْرُ مَذْكُورٍ تَنْصِيصًا وَالْمُقْتَضَى لَا عُمُومَ لَهُ فَلَغَتْ نِيَّةٌ التَّخْصِيصِ فِيْهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ إِنْ لَبِسْتُ ثَوْبًا اَوْا كَلْتُ طَعَامًا أَوْ شَرِبْتُ شَرَابًا لَمْ يُدَيِّنْ فِي الْقَضَاءِ خَاصَّةً لِأَنَّهُ نَكِرَةُ فِي مَحَلَّ الشَّرْطِ فَتَعُمُ فَعَمِلَتْ نِيَّةُ التَّخْصِيصِ فِيهِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ فَلَا يُدَيِّنُ فِي الْقَضَاءِ
And whoever says, 'If I wear, or eat, or drink, then my slave is free,' and then says, 'I meant some thing and not others,' he is not believed in the court nor elsewhere, because intention is only valid regarding what is explicitly uttered. And cloth and the like are not mentioned explicitly, and what is implied has no generality, so the intention of specification is void. But if he says, 'If I wear a garment, or eat food, or drink a drink,' then he is not believed in the court specifically, because it is a nakira in the condition so it becomes general, and the intention of specification takes effect, except that it contradicts the apparent meaning, so he is not believed in the court.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths that are general in wording and whether later specification of intention is accepted. The first case: a person swears 'in labistu aw akaltu aw sharibtu' without specifying any particular item. If he later claims he only meant certain things, this is not accepted either in court (qada) nor even in religious matters (diyanah). The reason is that intention only affects what is explicitly uttered ('al-malfuz'). Since 'cloth' etc. are not mentioned, the intended specification is void. The second case: if he says 'in labistu thawban' (a garment) etc., using a nakira (indefinite noun) in the condition, this makes the term general (ya'mum). Here, the intention of specification can take effect because the term is general, but it contradicts the apparent meaning (khilaf al-zahir), so it is accepted in diyanah (religiously) but not in qada (judicially).

Key Terms
نكرة في محل الشرط (nakira fi mahall al-shart): An indefinite noun in the condition clause; it gives generality.
المقتضى (al-muqtada): What is implied by the speech but not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does intention of specification NOT work at all (neither in qada nor diyanah)?
A) When the oath contains a nakira in the condition
B) When the oath is expressed as a general verb without any specified object
C) In all oaths, intention is always accepted in diyanah
D) Only if the swearer is a habitual liar

Key Takeaway
A general oath without a specified object cannot be restricted by intention; only oaths with a clear nakira allow specification, and even then only religiously, not in court.

Oath Not to Drink from a River: Direct Lapping vs. Vessel
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath drinking from a river.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on breaking an oath not to drink 'from' a river, and apply the distinction to similar oath scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath not to drink 'from' a river is only broken by direct lapping, not by using a vessel, according to Abu Hanifa.

When someone swears not to drink from a specific river, does using a cup break the oath? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students differ on this detail based on their interpretation of the word 'min' (from). This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the ruling: only direct lapping (kar') breaks the oath. Drinking from a vessel does not break it.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Identify their ruling: drinking from a vessel also breaks the oath because it is the common custom ('urf).
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
He argues that 'min' literally means partitiveness and lapping. Since lapping breaks by consensus, the literal meaning is preserved, so there is no need to adopt the metaphorical meaning (drinking from a vessel).
Note: This is a usul principle – prefer literal meaning over metaphorical even if custom is widespread.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil
They rely on common custom ('urf): people generally consider drinking from the river's water, whether by lapping or vessel, as breaking the oath.
5
Identify the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the oath is harder to break (only direct lapping). Under the Sahibayn, it is easier to break (any drinking from the river's water). The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
Also note the exception: if the oath is phrased as 'not to drink from the water of Dajlah', then even Abu Hanifa agrees that a vessel breaks the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَشْرَبُ مِنْ دَجْلَةَ فَشَرِبَ مِنْهَا بِإِنَاءٍ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ حَتَّى يَكْرَعَ مِنْهَا كَرْعًا عِنْدَابِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا إِذَا شَرِبَ مِنْهَا بِإِنَاءٍ يَحْنِتُ لِأَنَّهُ مُتَعَارَفُ الْمَشْهُومِ وَلَهُ أَنَّ كَلِمَةَ مِنْ لِلتَّبْعِيْضِ وَ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الْكَرْعِ وَهِيَ مُسْتَعْمَلَةٌ وَلِهَذَا يَحْنِتُ بِالْكَرْعِ إِجْمَاعًا فَمَنَعَتِ الْمَصِيرُ إِلَى الْمَجَازِوَإِنْ كَانَ مُتَعَارَفًا وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَشْرَبُ مِنْ مَاءٍ دَجْلَةَ فَشَرِبَ مِنْهَا بِإِنَاءٍ حَنَتَ لِأَنَّهُ بَعْدَ الْاِغْتِرَافِ بَقِيَ مَنْسُوبًا إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ الشَّرْطُ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا شَرِبَ . مِنْ مَاءِ نَهْرٍ يَأْخُذُ مِنْ دَجْلَةَ
He said: And whoever swears 'I will not drink from Dajlah' and then drinks from it with a vessel does not break his oath until he laps it directly, according to Abu Hanifa. And they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he drinks from it with a vessel, he breaks his oath because that is common custom. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the word 'min' (from) indicates partitiveness, and its literal meaning is lapping, and this usage is common, and therefore he breaks by lapping by consensus. This prevents resorting to the metaphorical meaning even if it is common custom. And if he swears 'I will not drink from the water of Dajlah' and then drinks from it with a vessel, he breaks his oath, because after scooping it remains attributed to it, and that is the condition, so it is like when he drinks from the water of a canal that takes from Dajlah.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths about drinking from a specific river, such as the Tigris (Dajlah). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if someone swears 'la ashrabu min Dajlata' (I will not drink from Dajlah), he only breaks the oath by drinking directly by lapping (kar'an) from the river, not by drinking from a vessel. His reasoning is that 'min' (from) implies partitiveness and its literal meaning is lapping, which is a known usage. Since lapping breaks the oath by consensus, there is no need to adopt the metaphorical meaning (drinking from a vessel) even if customarily used. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, arguing that common custom ('urf) treats drinking from a vessel as part of the oath. However, if the oath is phrased as 'la ashrabu min ma'i Dajlata' (from the water of Dajlah), then even Abu Hanifa agrees that drinking via a vessel breaks the oath, because the water remains attributed to the river after scooping. This is analogous to drinking from a canal that takes water from the river.

Key Terms
كرع (kar'): Drinking directly from a water source by lapping with the mouth, without using a vessel.
من (min): Preposition 'from'; in this context indicates partitiveness and literal lapping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does an oath 'not to drink from Dajlah' break?
A) By drinking from a cup
B) By drinking directly by lapping
C) By touching the water
D) By bathing in the river

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the literal meaning of the oath phrase governs; 'min' in 'not to drink from X' means direct lapping, not drinking via vessel.
